HEADLINE: NUCLEAR SECRETS: POLITICS;
Legislators Ask Pause in Relations With China

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER with FRANK BRUNI 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 26

 BODY:
Members of Congress from both parties pressed today for a pause in relations with China and a reversal of years of liberalized high-technology sales to Beijing, arguing that evidence of Chinese espionage undercuts the Clinton Administration's policy of engagement.
In a letter to President Clinton, four influential members of Congress asked him to "set aside negotiations" with China over its admission to the World Trade Organization, the main diplomatic and economic initiative under way between Beijing and Washington. The signers included two members of the Senate Foreign Relations Committee who rarely agree: the committee's Republican chairman, Jesse Helms of North Carolina, and Russell D. Feingold, the Wisconsin Democrat. 
 Meanwhile, the Senate majority leader, Trent Lott, introduced a package of measures as an amendment to a defense authorization bill that would give the F.B.I. greater authority over security checks at the national laboratories and require the Administration to notify Congress of any investigations into possible violations of export-control laws.
Nonetheless, the Clinton Administration, while embracing some of the recommendations in the declassified version of the Cox Commission's report, tried on two fronts to limit the political and diplomatic recriminations against the Chinese.
In Paris, the United States Trade Representative, Charlene Barshefsky, held a brief meeting with China's Trade Minister, Long Yongtu, in the first effort to rekindle talks about getting China into the World Trade Organization this year.
"We did not really engage in the substance of the issues," Ms. Barshefsky said today in a telephone interview. She and others in the Administration argue that it would be foolish for the United States to walk away from market openings and other concessions China has made in years of negotiations that stopped just short of completion during Prime Minister Zhu Ronji's visit here last month.
But Ms. Barshefsky said that she was a realist, and "clearly the accidental bombing of the embassy and the Cox report have engendered a degree of friction that will take some time to dissipate."
Meanwhile, the State Department is trying to assemble a high-level delegation to visit Beijing in the next week or two, to offer apologies for NATO's bombing of the Chinese Embassy on May 7 and an explanation of how the Central Intelligence Agency and the National Imagery and Mapping Agency misidentified the embassy and then hit it with a satellite-guided bomb.
The very idea of the mea culpa mission, to be led by Thomas R. Pickering, the Undersecretary of State for Political Affairs, has angered many conservative members of Congress. But it has been delayed, officials say, for another reason: The intelligence agencies, the Pentagon and the State Department are bogged down by internal disputes about how much detail to provide to the Chinese.
"If we go and give them the same stuff that's already been said in public, they won't be satisfied, and there is the risk of an even bigger breach," one senior official said today. Inside the C.I.A. and the military, he continued, "There is a reluctance to name names and affix punishments, as the Chinese have demanded."
In public the Clinton Administration is still talking about keeping the three issues -- China the spy, China the aggrieved, China the trading partner -- on separate tracks. But it became increasingly clear today that they all converge on Capitol Hill. The House and the Senate will have to vote in the next two months on a temporary extension of China's trading status, and if a trade organization accord is signed later this year it would have to vote to permanently extend that status to Beijing.
For both parties, calibrating the correct political response to the Cox Report this week has been a delicate matter. Republicans clearly want to stoke as much public outrage as possible, as a way of vilifying President Clinton and blemishing the Presidential aspirant Al Gore.
But, at the same time, many Republicans clearly do not want the fallout over this to be a chill in trade relations with China -- especially members from agricultural states that count on the Chinese as customers. So they are doing a careful two-step, condemning what happened in the strongest terms on the one hand but then cautioning that this should not in any way be held against China when it comes to trade.
Mr. Lott, for example, has excoriated the Clinton Administration in recent days for ignoring evidence of the breach of national security -- while in the next breath cautioning against overreacting in the area of trade. His package of reforms introduced today would only affect events within American borders, tightening security at nuclear labs, control exports of sensitive technology and put the F.B.I. in charge of performing security checks on employees at nuclear labs. A vote on the amendment was expected late tonight.
Mr. Lott's approach is attractive to many members because it gives them a chance to respond to the Cox Report without abandoning the engagement strategy -- which was the Bush Administration's strategy.
Meanwhile, Representative Christopher Cox, who led the select committee that issued the report on Chinese espionage, appeared before a House subcommittee today to answer more questions. Pressed about whether evidence of espionage supports the report's conclusions that China's information about nuclear weapons designs is now on a par with that of the United States, Mr. Cox said the Chinese did get design information "on a par with us."
Even with mounting concerns over the findings of Mr. Cox's committee, it still seems unlikely, several members of Congress and lobbyists for American companies said today, that the annual extension of what used to be called "most-favored nation" status -- it is now called "normal trade relations" -- will be derailed. A permanent extension, which would deprive members of Congress of the chance to vent their anger each year, is more doubtful.
But the letter to Mr. Clinton, also signed by Representative Benjamin Gilman, the New York Republican, and Representative Tom Lantos, the California Democrat, argued that "the Chinese Government's sanctioning of the attack on our embassy in Beijing, the torching of the consulate, and the attacks on American businesses demonstrate clearly that our engagement policy and the delinking of trade from other issues such as human rights have not fostered the deep, stable and meaningful relationship between the United States and China that had been promised." It said that "the most responsible and appropriate step is to set aside negotiations."
The White House quickly responded. "We respectfully disagree," said Gene Sperling, head of the National Economic Council. "A strong China-W.T.O. agreement is exclusively about opening China's market to our businesses and workers, and we think if there is a good agreement there will still be a bipartisan majority for it."

HEADLINE: U.N. Presses Wealthy Countries for More Aid to Third World

BYLINE: By JUDITH MILLER 

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, May 26

 BODY:
Reflecting concern that the war in Kosovo is diverting political energy and scarce resources from other conflicts, Secretary General Kofi Annan appealed today to world leaders to do more next month at the meeting of major industrialized nations to help the developing world.
In a letter to the heads of the Group of Seven industrialized countries and Russia, Mr. Annan pressed for increased development aid and greater debt relief for debt-ridden third-world countries and additional steps to shore up the international financial system, whose stability he said is key to preventing global recession. 
 Meanwhile, diplomats are quietly complaining that the international community, preoccupied with Kosovo, is paying too little attention to conflicts in Angola, Ethiopia and elsewhere. This morning, for instance, the Security Council expressed "deep concern" at the deteriorating situation in Angola, where up until a few months ago, the United Nations thought it had settled a long, bloody civil war only to see fighting erupt again this year.
After hearing a description of the conflict and an impassioned plea for emergency assistance from Francesco Strippoli, the United Nations humanitarian coordinator for Angola, the Council expressed "particular concern" about the relief groups' lack of access to hundreds of thousands of refugees. They have been displaced within Angola by the fighting between the Government and Unita, its rebel opponents who control many of the roads leading to the now besieged provincial cities to which thousands have fled.
In an interview, Mr. Strippoli said that $110 million in food and other assistance was desperately needed just to sustain the 1.6 million internally displaced Angolans.
There was little doubt, he said, that the crisis in Kosovo was preoccupying the international community. While refusing to "compare human misery" and acknowledging that the situation in the Balkans was also horrendous, he said that the conflict in the Balkans was making it even harder for diplomats and donors to focus on the plight of Angolans.
Another war that seems to be escalating is that between Ethiopia and Eritrea, now Africa's, and perhaps the world's, largest. "The Security Council is doing practically nothing," Fesseha A. Tessema, Ethiopia's Minister Counselor, said in an interview.
The Security Council took no action last Wednesday after hearing a briefing on United Nations efforts to end a conflict that has killed thousands and, according to Unicef, displaced more than 550,000 people.
A United Nations special envoy has been trying to mediate an end to the conflict, but the Council decided to wait for further action by the Organization of African Unity, which has brokered a peace agreement, before taking any formal action.
Mr. Tessema blamed what he called a lack of concern about a conflict in poor, remote Africa for the lack of response. "If this war were in Europe, or close to the United States, there would more Council intervention," he said.
Louise Frechette, the Deputy Secretary General, disagreed that the Council or the United Nations was focusing solely on Kosovo at the expense of other conflicts. The United Nations, she said in an interview, was "extremely active" in trying to alleviate human misery and find political solutions to numerous conflicts throughout the world.

HEADLINE: NUCLEAR SECRETS: RELATIONS;
On Unofficial Level, at Least, Chinese Value Ties to the U.S.

BYLINE: By ELISABETH ROSENTHAL and ERIK ECKHOLM 

DATELINE: BEIJING, May 26

 BODY:
One recent afternoon, as the Chinese press continued to rage against "American imperialism," a 36-year-old lawyer named Wang darted from the newly opened Starbucks back into the law conference he was attending -- in place of an American delegate who felt uneasy about traveling to Beijing.
Mr. Wang, who wears cartoon neckties and loafers, went to college in Portland, Ore. At his Chinese law firm he works closely with American clients, and he still keeps in touch with dozens of friends in the United States. 
 He shares the anger of his compatriots about the bombing of the Chinese Embassy in Belgrade and finds it hard to believe that it was purely a mistake.
But like many Chinese today, he also cannot imagine China getting by without the United States.
"There are so many different types of interactions now: economic, cultural and educational," he said. "I think much would be lost on both sides if those ties were sacrificed."
Because of the current anti-American atmosphere here, Mr. Wang, like many other Chinese approached on the streets in recent days, spoke on condition that his full name not be used.
Political relations between China and the United States have entered a tense new phase, battered by China's outrage over the attack on its embassy and by a Congressional committee's charges of extensive Chinese military espionage.
But even as geopolitics and the prospect of a strategic rivalry drive the two countries apart, millions of on-the-ground personal and business relationships form a thick social glue that binds them together. Since China began to open up some 20 years ago, those ties have multiplied with speed and gusto, long ago eclipsing the wary political relationship.
These diverse links tend to work against any severe estrangement, many political experts and others in both countries say, especially since American ties are strongest among China's most influential groups.
China's top three Communist Party leaders all have children who were educated in the United States, just one symbol of a relationship that is vastly more complicated than the cold war standoff between the United States and the Soviet Union.
"The glue is very deep," said Ezra Vogel, head of Harvard University's Asia Center and a former national security official. "Given the depth of contacts, openness and interdependence we have now, it's hard for me to imagine that it could become unstuck."
Nearly every better-off family in Beijing seems to have relatives studying or working in the United States, or working in American joint ventures in China. The personal links are strongest among the country's Communist elite, starting right at the top.
A son of Jiang Zemin, the President and head of the Communist Party, studied at Drexel University in Philadelphia from 1986 to 1991, earning a Ph.D. in electrical engineering. The son, Jiang Mianheng, is now a corporate executive in Shanghai and often makes business trips to the United States.
Li Xiaolin, the 38-year-old daughter of Li Peng -- No. 2 in the Communist Party hierarchy and believed to be a hard-line, anti-American voice in the leadership -- did graduate work in engineering at M.I.T. Now an executive with a Chinese Government power company, she visits the United States several times a year developing bond offerings for power projects.
Zhu Yunlai, the son of Prime Minister Zhu Rongji, spent a decade in the United States, where he did graduate studies in business and worked at Credit Suisse First Boston. Today he is a senior official of the first Chinese investment bank, a joint venture between a state-owned bank and Morgan Stanley Dean Witter.
Close to 50,000 Chinese students are now attending American universities, mainly in graduate schools. Many of the hundreds of thousands of Chinese who studied in America stayed on to work there, but a growing share of those who have studied abroad are returning to work in China.
Alumni clubs from universities like Harvard and Stanford thrive in Beijing. And thousands of extended families here brag about the grandchild with an American passport, born while the parents were studying abroad.
In business, American companies' direct investment in China now surpasses $21 billion. A few companies, including Motorola and I.B.M., are believed to sell more than $1 billion worth of goods here each year. A new Kodak film developing counter opens in China nearly every day.
Equally striking, though, are the multitudes of smaller private exchanges and activities, which have mushroomed far beyond the ability of either government to track. Each year, more that 700,000 Americans travel to China.
A group run by Ned Graham, a son of the Rev. Billy Graham, is allowed to distribute Bibles to "underground" churches in the Chinese countryside. A Hong Kong millionaire has paid for Chinese Army colonels to attend the Kennedy School of Government at Harvard, while Gloria's Place of Hope, a small New Jersey foundation, has a waiting list of almost 600 Chinese children who want to go the United States for heart surgery.
The weeks since the bombing of China's embassy in Belgrade on May 7 have been an awkward time for millions of people in both countries as they wait to see how their personal, professional and business ties will be affected.
Many have sought to insulate their most precious relationships with colleagues and friends from the hostile politics -- as well as from their own anger over a bombing that many Chinese think was probably intentional or at the least extremely reckless.
The Chinese Government has suspended official collaboration in areas like military cooperation and human rights. Some private contacts, too, have at least temporarily followed suit. A reception for a Chinese version of Cosmopolitan magazine was canceled. A party to kick off a new master of business administration program offered by Beijing University in conjunction with a consortium of United States colleges was postponed.
"Last week we were going to host a delegation of American insurance managers, but they canceled," said Yang Qi, a 27-year-old woman in a denim jacket emblazoned with a small American flag, who works for a Chinese insurance company. "There has been tremendous outrage about the bombing, and of course it has created some bad feelings. I myself have stopped eating at McDonald's. But the outrage is directed at the American Government and not at the American people, and I think it will pass."
Many employees at joint venture companies said normal after-hours socializing -- the dinners and the badminton games -- had ground to a halt. "Everyone sits and does their work and nothing is said openly, but there's an extremely awkward feeling in the air," said a man wearing black jeans and aviator sunglasses who would give only his surname, Hou.
But in other cases the eruption of hostile politics barely intruded on plans.
The week that the American Embassy in Beijing was pelted with stones, angry Chinese were waiting outside the closed consulate in Guangzhou -- for visas. That same week, as the official press attacked American "barbarism," Li Peng's daughter called American officials here with an urgent request to get documents notarized.
Ten days after the embassy bombing in Belgrade, a 176-member delegation from Jiangsu Province arrived in California looking for investment and trade opportunities.
The Chinese leaders hope, perhaps unrealistically, that the economic ties they have cultivated to aid China's modernization can be insulated from political conflict. American business executives, now with millions at stake, tend to share that wish.
Many educated Chinese are likewise trying to wall off international politics from their own self-interest and their personal lives.
Guo Haobin, an affable 22-year-old who works for an international trading company, said he was "very angry and agitated about the bombing, like all Chinese." But he said he continued to treat American clients well, and when asked if he had been to the United States, he answered, "I haven't, but as soon as I have the opportunity, I really want to go."
Indeed, when the visa section of the American Embassy reopened on Monday, many of its shattered windows still covered with boards, there were hundreds of applicants, mostly students wanting to get back to American universities or parents eager to visit children in the United States.
"People have very contradictory points of view right now," said Shi Tao, a book editor who did graduate studies in Ohio. "Personally, they want their sons and daughters to go to the U.S. to get educated and have a better life. Or they have prospered and for that reason want to keep beneficial contacts going. At the same time, they think we should fight against the American imperialists.
"They think they can separate their personal needs from the national point of view. But I worry that it will make some people feel more angry and confused."
Chinese with their feet in both worlds insist that the social and economic relations are too strong to destroy and hope that the politics will ultimately be dragged along.
"I don't think the political tensions will affect all the other ties," said Xu Xiaonian, chief of research at China International Capital Corporation, the investment bank that is a joint venture with Morgan Stanley Dean Witter.
"The relationship between these two countries has never been easy, and I don't expect it to be smooth in the future," said Mr. Xu, who received a Ph.D. from the University of California at Davis, then taught at Amherst College in Massachusetts for many years. "But I believe that it will move in a direction that will benefit both countries."
Late last year, after spending 12 years in the United States, Mr. Xu returned to Beijing to take his current job. "I could have stayed in Amherst and led the good life," Mr Xu said. "But the other part of my life belongs in China."
Mr. Xu's wife and daughter have remained behind in Amherst, where his wife works in the public library. "It was just too beautiful for them to leave," he said.

HEADLINE: NEWS SUMMARY

 BODY:

INTERNATIONAL A3-13
 
Milosevic to Be Indicted By War Crimes Tribunal
The international war crimes tribunal in The Hague will announce the indictment of President Slobodan Milosevic of Yugoslavia today, Western officials said. A1 

The Clinton Administration has concluded that an indictment would not preclude direct negotiations with President Milosevic on Kosovo, senior American officials said. A1
 
The Pentagon ordered a broad suspension of retirements from the Air Force to insure that enough pilots and other personnel were available to continue waging the air war. A13
 
Kosovo Update A12
 
Indian Air Strikes in Kashmir
Indian combat jets and helicopter gunships attacked a large guerrilla force dug into the mountains of Kashmir. India said Pakistan was sponsoring Islamic militants in a planned land grab. Pakistan rejected the accusations. A1
 
Relations With China Examined
Members of Congress from both parties pressed for a pause in relations with China and a reversal of years of liberalized technology sales to Beijing, saying that evidence of Chinese espionage had undercut the Administration's policy of engagement. A6
 
Mission to North Korea
A special envoy, William Perry, delivered a letter from President Clinton to North Korea's leader, Kim Jong Il, the official North Korean news agency reported. Last week, White House officials said Mr. Perry would deliver a proposal that the economic embargo against North Korea be lifted gradually in exchange for major concessions. A3
 
Renewed Hope in Middle East
The Palestinian leader Yasir Arafat met in Gaza with King Abdullah of Jordan. The meeting was part of a flurry of contacts between the Palestinian Authority, Egypt and Jordan since the Israeli election. A5
 
American Sumo Champ in Japan
A second American has muscled his way into the top ranks of sumo. The wrestler, Musashimaru, a Hawaiian whose given name is Fiamalu Penitani, became the 67th yokozuna, or grand champion, in the sport's 300-year modern history. A9
 
World Briefing A8
 
NATIONAL A14-30
 
For Heart Attack Victims, All Hospitals Are Not Equal
New research indicates that getting heart attack victims to the right hospitals -- ones that treat many heart attack victims -- is more important than getting them to the closest ones, and may mean the difference between life and death. The report, published today in the New England Journal of Medicine, also calls for emergency medical systems to consider identifying certain hospitals as heart attack centers, just as they now designate trauma centers. A1
 
New Diabetes Treatment
The F.D.A. has approved the new drug Avandia to treat adult-onset diabetes, and its maker said it would be on the market in days. Avandia is thought to pose less risk to the liver than the popular drug Rezulin. A22
 
Abnormality Found in Clone
The scientists in Scotland who created Dolly, the sheep that was the first animal that was a clone of an adult, say the genetic material in her cells may show a sign of aging, appearing, in effect, older than Dolly herself. If confirmed, it would be the first sign of possible genetic abnormalities in cloned animals. A19
 
G.O.P. Infighting in House
Representative Christopher Shays, Republican of Connecticut, defied his party by signing a Democratic petition intended to force the issue of overhauling campaign finance laws to the House floor. Mr. Shays also accused Representative Tom Delay, the majority whip, of offering to finance a primary challenge against him. A28
 
House Acts to Shield Surplus
The House, voting 416 to 12, passed legislation that would put new hurdles in the way of spending any Social Security program surpluses. A18
 
Rebuff on Base-Closing Effort
The Senate rejected an Administration request to close more military bases to save money at a time when the Kosovo conflict has strained resources. A22
 
Redirecting Reform Judaism
The rabbinical leadership of Reform Judaism, the largest and most liberal group of American Jewry, approved guiding principles to encourage more use of traditional rituals like wearing skullcaps, keeping kosher and praying in Hebrew. A24
 
NEW YORK/REGION B1-10
 
U.S. Approves Request To Shift Medicaid Coverage
Federal officials said New York could begin shepherding hundreds of thousands of poor people in New York City into managed care health plans in July. Most people on Medicaid receive care under the traditional fee-for-service approach, in which medical providers are paid every time a patient is treated. A1
 
Guards Charged in Jail Death
Three guards at the Nassau County jail were charged with violating the civil rights of an inmate who prosecutors say died from a beating by two of the guards. B1

HEADLINE: CRISIS IN THE BALKANS: BELGRADE;
Word of Indictment Stuns Serbs and Blights Hopes

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER 

DATELINE: BELGRADE, Serbia, May 26

 BODY:
Word that President Slobodan Milosevic may be indicted for war crimes on Thursday, the day a Russian envoy is due here to discuss a peace settlement for Kosovo, stunned Serbs tonight, dampening hopes for an end to the war.
As the Government cracked down on antiwar dissent, charging an elected official with treason, a former confidant of Mr. Milosevic suggested that the Yugoslav leader might intensify the conflict by carrying it to Montenegro, Serbia's sister republic. 
 Its leader, Milo Djukanovic, has given shelter to democrats from Serbia. He is opposed to the ethnic purges in Kosovo and poses the greatest domestic challenge to Mr. Milosevic.
If Mr. Milosevic is indicted on Thursday by the tribunal in The Hague, it will greatly complicate the efforts of Viktor S. Chernomyrdin, the Russian special envoy who is expected here for talks.
"If the report is true, this will change everything," said a Serbian analyst. An indictment would confirm the suspicion of most Serbs that the tribunal, which operates under the United Nations, is prejudiced against them and is not an independent court. Serbs will conclude that NATO is not interested in peace, making Mr. Chernomyrdin's efforts all the more difficult.
Tonight, the Yugoslav delegate to the United Nations, Vladislav Jovanovic, attacked the pending indictment and its timing. "It is a politically motivated decision that renders the tribunal an accomplice to NATO as an aggressor," he told the BBC. "This is motivated to help turn the population against our President and Government."
Mr. Milosevic has always been suspicious of NATO's desire to introduce troops into Kosovo, fearing that the troops would arrest him for war crimes. He may now suspect that the indictment is a way to spurn any initiatives he makes toward a Kosovo settlement.
Mr. Milosevic is making clear that he will not tolerate antiwar dissent while the NATO bombing lasts, and may even intensify the conflict in the Balkans by mobilizing the general population to defend Kosovo and oust Mr. Djukanovic.
The elected Mayor of the southern town of Cacak, Velimir Ilic, a member of the opposition Democratic Party, is the latest to flee to Montenegro, according to residents of Cacak. Officially, Mr. Ilic is on vacation, but he is now thought to have joined the head of the Democratic Party, Zoran Djindjic, there. The army has issued an arrest warrant for Mr. Ilic for treason
Six prominent democrats in Cacak, including a journalist, have been fined a total of $2,800 for taking part in unsanctioned and thus illegal gatherings of a "civic parliament" that has called on Mr. Milosevic to negotiate a rapid settlement to the Kosovo conflict.
The army and police have also cracked down in Krusevac, where mothers have been demonstrating for their soldier sons to return from Kosovo. All roads into the town are being monitored, and there are police checks on anyone going in or out.
And in Belgrade today, the trial of two Australian aid workers for CARE and a Yugoslav colleague, imprisoned since March 31, on charges of espionage opened. The trial, after its ceremonial start, was closed on security grounds to all outsiders, including the Australian Ambassador to Belgrade and the mother of the Yugoslav on trial.
Thus far, there have been few severe punishments meted out to dissenters, even in Krusevac, where up to 3,000 people, many of them the mothers of conscripts, demonstrated for days and the soldiers themselves were allowed to return home, at least for a time.
In Cacak, a statement by the local branch of Mr. Milosevic's political party accused Mr. Ilic of giving interviews to Radio Free Europe, which was described as "under the direct patronage of the C.I.A."
"The present local authorities in Cacak have not shown even the minimum of patriotism, even after two months of war," the statement said.
The reported indictment of Mr. Milosevic complicates peace efforts. In the past the Clinton Administration refused to negotiate with people indicted for war crimes in Bosnia, including the former Bosnian Serb leader, Radovan Karadzic, while it did negotiate with Mr. Milosevic.
NATO is not negotiating with Mr. Milosevic now, doing it through Mr. Chernomyrdin and the Finnish President, Martti Ahtisaari, who is expected to accompany Mr. Chernomyrdin to Belgrade. Finland is not a member of NATO.
But even negotiations through intermediaries deal ultimately with Mr. Milosevic, who decides Belgrade's policy.
The trial of the aid workers before a military court took place despite protests from the United Nations and other international agencies. Those on trial are Steve Pratt and Peter Wallace of Australia and Branko Jelen, a Yugoslav.
On April 12, Yugoslavia displayed Mr. Pratt on television, apparently confessing to collecting information on Kosovo and the effects of the NATO bombing, but the confession, which Mr. Pratt's lawyer has said was made under duress, is not admissible.
Sergio Vieira de Mello, the United Nations Under Secretary General for Humanitarian Affairs and Emergency Relief, said he had appealed to Belgrade to "balance any evidence that might exist" against "the interest we all have in fostering support, sympathy, involvement and engagement" of international organizations and donors.
Mr. Vieira de Mello, ending an 11-day tour of Yugoslavia, including three days in Kosovo, also told the Government that no arguments could justify the magnitude of the displacement of people in Kosovo. "The extent of the damage is deeply disturbing," he said. "People were forced to leave with various degrees of coercion, intimidation or force."
Mr. Vieira de Mello also spoke of the suffering of civilians. Unemployment has reached crisis proportions, he said, and health, water, transport and heating have suffered.
"It is a combination of anxiety, humiliation, the lack of understanding of what is happening, and immense stress of a distant and powerful enemy that can strike at any moment," he said.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS;
Shares End Their4-Day Slump as the Dow Advances 171.07 Points

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Stocks rebounded from a four-day slump yesterday, as the banking and Internet shares that faltered earlier this week roared back.
The Dow Jones industrial average rose 171.07 points, or 1.62 percent, to close at 10,702.16. 
 In a single, volatile session, the index of blue-chip stocks wiped out more than half its losses since Friday. Four straight losses had pushed the Dow 5 percent below its May 13 record of 11,107.19. After yesterday's rally, the Dow is 3.6 percent off the all-time high.
Broader indicators rose, recovering early losses. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index gained 20.36, or 1.59 percent, to 1,304.76, and the technology-heavy Nasdaq composite index rose 46.28, or 1.94 percent, to 2,427.18.
Analysts said the market was ready for a bounce, as the nation's economy remained strong and the outlook for corporate profits remained rosy, at least for the next quarter.
"Conditions are quite good," said Tony Dwyer, chief equity strategist at Ladenburg Thalmann & Company. "I.B.M. is solid, and the financials are very strong."
I.B.M. contributed heavily to the Dow's gains, soaring 15 1/16 , to 236 1/4. The computer giant drew attention as a 2-for-1 split took effect after the market closed yesterday. Stock splits often prompt a rally, as investors expect the lower price to entice more buyers, but analysts were reluctant to pin I.B.M.'s gains solely on the split.
Instead, market watchers said, I.B.M.'s stock was unfairly battered in recent sessions, as the entire technology sector faltered.
Technology stocks were hit hard as concerns about potentially higher interest rates drew investors away from the high-priced shares. Through Tuesday, the Nasdaq index had tumbled 10 percent from its record of 2,652.05, set on April 26, meeting the conventional definition of a market correction.
Several industry leaders continued to languish yesterday. Intel, which unveiled a new chip design, fell 1 3/16 , to 51 11/16 .
But many Internet-related shares resumed their dizzying gains. Yahoo surged 13 15/16 , to 140 7/8, and America Online jumped 5 15/16 , to 120 1/4.
Banking and financial stocks, which have been hit by concerns over interest rates and the Year 2000 computer problem, surged as old merger rumors hit the market. Merrill Lynch, often mentioned as a takeover target, climbed 10 5/16 , to 78 11/16 . Chase Manhattan, the rumored suitor, rose 1 9/16 , to 75 3/8. Both companies denied the rumors.
A. Marshall Acuff, equity strategist at Salomon Smith Barney, said investors were merely revisiting the stocks that fed market gains earlier this year.
Yesterday's market took a measure of support from a Commerce Department report that factory orders for big-ticket manufactured goods fell an unexpectedly sharp 2.3 percent last month. The figure offered new evidence that the economy is not overheated.
Yet analysts were far from enthusiastic about the rally on Wall Street. Most Dow components were fairly flat. "It's not the healthiest of rallies," Mr. Acuff said.

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
Treasury Prices Fall
 (By Bloomberg News)
 Bond prices fell for the first time in four sessions because of lackluster demand at an auction of $15 billion of two-year notes.
Investors balked at the notes, among the most sensitive to Federal Reserve policy, because the central bank said last week that it was prepared to raise interest rates to slow growth and quell inflation.
The price of the benchmark 30-year bond fell 24/32 , to 92 8/32 . Its yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, rose to 5.80 percent from 5.74 percent. Yields on two-year notes rose to 5.33 percent from 5.26 percent.
Investors are concerned the central bank may soon raise rates after it warned last week that it was leaning in that direction. A report today is expected to show the economy grew at a 4.3 percent annual rate in the first quarter.

HEADLINE: Good News on Jobs, Or Just Good Times?

 BODY:

To the Editor:
The longest post-World War II recovery with declining unemployment rates and skilled labor shortages is finally benefiting those at the bottom of the economic ladder. Black male unemployment is declining (front page, May 23), and real wages are finally increasing after almost three decades of stagnation. 
 What is the response of Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, to this good news? Concern over barely discernible inflation and hints that the Fed should raise interest rates.
Tight monetary policy has been a major contributor to employment and wage gaps among the races. A pre-emptive strike against the possibility of inflation would undermine recent gains by African-American men and lead to a growth in inequality.
 
MICHAEL ROSEN
Milwaukee, May 24, 1999
 
The writer is the chairman of the economics department, Milwaukee Area Technical College.

HEADLINE: End of Commuter Tax May Cost Us All

 BODY:

To the Editor:
Gov. George E. Pataki, in his defense of eliminating New York City's commuter tax, writes that cutting taxes is good for the economy (Op-Ed, May 22). 
 But there is a difference between cutting taxes and eliminating them entirely, which, as conservative economists like to point out, is just as bad as overtaxation.
Let us not forget that the commuter tax was a central element in the state's regionalized tax structure, which allowed New Yorkers not residing or working in New York City to benefit from a lower tax rate.
Now that this part of our social compact has been broken, what will the State Legislature have to do when good economic times turn to bad? Simply impose a higher unified state income tax on all New Yorkers?
 
KENT K. GORDIS
Brooklyn, May 22, 1999

HEADLINE: U.S. LETS NEW YORK SHIFT MOST POOR TO MANAGED CARE

BYLINE: By RAYMOND HERNANDEZ 

DATELINE: ALBANY, May 26

 BODY:
Federal officials said today that New York can begin moving more than one million poor people in New York City into managed care health plans as early as July in one of the most ambitious efforts of its kind in the nation, according to state and Federal officials.
The state and the city have been seeking approval from Washington for two years to begin the mandatory managed care program in New York City, where most people on Medicaid receive medical coverage under the traditional fee-for-service approach in which medical providers are paid every time a patient is treated. 
 Under a waiver approved today by the United States Department of Health and Human Services, the state would be allowed to place as many as 1.1 million Medicaid recipients in New York City into managed care programs during the next two years, according to state and Federal officials. The Federal Government has approved similar waivers from the current Medicaid payment system in about a dozen other states.
People with chronic illnesses like AIDS or diabetes would be exempt from the mandatory program.
The administrations of both Gov. George E. Pataki and Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani have repeatedly praised managed care as a way to save taxpayer dollars, largely because managed care companies are paid a set rate for each patient and, in turn, hold down costs by limiting access to specialists and emphasizing preventive care. They also say care would be improved for the poor because they would be assigned a regular doctor and would rely less on emergency room visits for basic care.
But the effort has faced resistance by advocates for the poor and Democratic lawmakers who are concerned that it would reduce services to welfare recipients. Hospitals have also expressed concerns that the program could shortchange them on the cost of services because health maintenance organizations will try to negotiate low rates.
The cost of Medicaid in New York State has grown to about $28 billion a year, the most expensive Medicaid program in the nation. The state and local governments, primarily New York City, pay roughly half of the program's cost, with the rest covered by the Federal Government.
The Federal Government two years ago gave the state the authority to begin placing Medicaid recipients into manage care programs, but has required it to do so gradually. Today's ruling affects only New York City. The state has already been requiring Medicaid recipients in a dozen upstate counties to enroll in managed care plans.
In New York City, where the bulk of the state's Medicaid population resides, poor people have, until now, been given the option of enrolling in managed care plans.
There are 650,000 Medicaid recipients in managed care plans throughout the state, with nearly 400,000 of them residing in New York City, according to state officials.
The mandatory program in New York City will be phased in beginning in July, starting with lower Manhattan, parts of Brooklyn and all of Staten Island, affecting nearly 400,000 recipients. The program will be phased in in the rest of the city sometime later.
The decision by the Federal Government was warmly praised today by officials in both the Giuliani and the Pataki administrations, which have made managed care for Medicaid recipients one of the keys to their health care initiatives.
"This is a great victory for the Governor and for New York City," said Dennis P. Whalen, the Acting State Health Commissioner. "This waiver will give millions of medically needy New Yorkers the medical homes and the quality health care they need to live long healthy lives."
City and state officials said, however, that they needed to review some of the conditions that the Federal Government included in the approval. Federal officials said they would allow the state to move ahead with the managed care program in the city only after it established certain protections for recipients, like establishing an appeals process for decisions made by their managed care plans. Medicaid recipients will have a choice of managed care plans in which they can enroll.
The Federal Government is also requiring the state and the city to undertake an extensive public information campaign intended to alert people with AIDS or chronic illnesses that they are exempt from being placed into managed care because of the gravity of their illnesses.
Today's decision came after Federal investigators spent months reviewing whether the city had a sufficiently extensive network of health plans and doctors to absorb the hundreds of thousands of Medicaid recipients who will be sent into managed care. The extensive Federal review was in part prompted by concerns among advocates for the poor and the disabled, who worried that many managed care companies would try to cut corners and reduce services, particularly given the high cost of treating Medicaid patients, who on average are sicker than others.
Advocates for the poor and the disabled said today they were pleased that the Federal Government had placed conditions on the program. "The Federal Government isn't taking an 'anything goes' attitude," said Michael Kink, legislative counsel for Housing Works, a group serving people with AIDS. "They are being careful. And there are plenty of good reasons to be careful when the health care of more than a million poor and disabled New Yorkers is at stake."
The Government's decision comes as many of the city's economically beleaguered hospitals are raising concerns that the advent of managed care among the state's Medicaid population, particularly in New York City, will cause them greater financial distress and potential ruin.
Many hospital officials contend they are already facing huge financial headaches as they find themselves caught between increasingly powerful H.M.O.'s that demand reduced rates for their private customers, and state and Federal and state officials who are cutting billions of dollars in hospital payments.
The hospitals are concerned that health maintenance plans will also demand reduced rates for their Medicaid customers, particularly since the plans themselves have been complaining that the state is not adequately reimbursing them.
Some managed care companies have in recent years either scaled back their participation in the state's existing, voluntary Medicaid managed care program or dropped out because they were sustaining huge losses as a result of the state's relatively low reimbursements rates for Medicaid recipients.

SECTION: Section E; Part 2; Page 36; Column 1; Leisure/Weekend Desk 

LENGTH: 1883 words

HEADLINE: Bette Midler Was Here: A Park Gets a Second Act

BYLINE: By PETER HELLMAN 

 BODY:
What I'm smelling on a May afternoon, as I stand on an overlook in Highbridge Park at 179th Street, is not the noxious fumes from compacted traffic on the Alexander Hamilton and Washington Bridges looming overhead. It's a heady floral scent, as intriguing as it is unlikely. Just above on the slope is a grove of locust trees in full white blossom. Even on their best day, they would never smell as good as this. Walking beyond the locusts, I come upon a field where the secret is revealed. Here, where less than two years ago carcasses of stolen cars were heaped, are dozens of low-lying lilac bushes in pale purple flower. Bette Midler had struck again.
Actually, Ms. Midler's stealth campaign to resuscitate the 19th-century Highbridge Park began in November 1997, long before her much-publicized rescue of 51 community gardens this month. The lilac bushes were planted without fanfare by workers from her New York Restoration Project. Here at the midpoint of Highbridge Park, they symbolize the renewal of what had been, in Parks Commissioner Henry J. Stern's estimation, the city's "most damaged, most cluttered" major park. And yet under all that debris the spectacular contours of the park remained. Now, in a dramatic turnaround, it's ready again for visitors. Regular tours are being sponsored by the New York City Parks Department and the Urban Park Rangers. 
 The park runs a full two and a half miles from 155th to Dyckman Streets along the steep Harlem River bluff, also called the Manhattan Palisades. Though little known, Highbridge is nearly as long as Central Park, but narrower and steeper.
It is the ruggedness of this terrain that saved it from development but also provided seclusion for the car strippers, drug users and prostitutes who overran the park from its crest in Washington Heights as it deteriorated in the wake of the city's mid-1970's financial crisis. Highbridge had become so degraded that in an all-hands cleanup in 1986, 250 tons of garbage and 25 auto wrecks were hauled out. Within days, new wrecks littered the slopes.
"It was such a shock to have a newly cleaned park dumped on," Mr. Stern said back then, when he was in his first stint as Parks Commissioner, under Mayor Edward I. Koch.
Only the most intrepid or foolhardy visitors dared to explore Highbridge when Ms. Midler first threaded her way up a decrepit, poison ivy-choked stairway from the Harlem River Drive into the park in 1996. She was guided by Joseph Pupello, director of the New York Restoration Project, which she founded. Instead of lilacs, the 179th Street overlook smelled of fetid mattresses and rank pools of motor oil. Fragments of the overlook's original elegant wrought iron railing were strewn about. But the drama of the spot could not be denied.
Not that the Restoration Project does anything alone; it is a complement to the Parks Department. "They can come in with heavy equipment and pull out car wrecks, which is beyond our abilities," explains Amy Gavaris, the project's landscape architect. "But after the parks people leave, we're there with our own staff and Americorps volunteers to do hand cleaning and planting. And we stay on."
Over the last year, the project spent just over $1 million in Highbridge Park, about a quarter of its total budget. One of the easiest places to see its handiwork is at the approach to the Washington Bridge at 181st Street and Amsterdam Avenue. Here a path leads into the park, passing under an car access ramp that local residents once called "the tunnel of death." Now the area has been restored, its formerly overgrown outcroppings of grey schist exposed. The ground is alive with eight species of new plantings, from Montauk daisies to catmint.
As this path wends down toward the Harlem River, you'll come across Ms. Midler's sea of plantings. Farther along, hugging a giant stone arch of the Washington Bridge, is a once-ruined hillside that was covered with daffodils in April.
Highbridge Park, designed in 1888 by Samuel Parsons Jr. and Calvert Vaux, begins in a thin spit called Coogan's Bluff that rises abruptly at West 155th Street and Edgecombe Avenue. In the swamps below, abutting the Harlem River, were the Polo Grounds, once home to the baseball and football Giants. Children who didn't have the price of admission could perch on the bluff and see part of the field.
In Jeff Kisseloff's 1989 book "You Must Remember This," Lewis Michels, father of City Councilman Stanley Michels, reminisces that when he was a boy, "if a ball was hit over the fence and you retrieved it, you could bring it to the gatekeeper, and in exchange for the ball, he would let you in."
The southern zone of the park, stretching roughly 20 blocks uptown to the grand old High Bridge itself, was long impassable. But two years ago, as the Restoration Project was beginning work in the middle of the park, the Parks Department began the arduous work of opening a trail along the route of the buried Croton Aqueduct, paralleling Edgecombe Avenue. On a recent Thursday, I hiked this recovered trail with Jane Schachat, chief of northern Manhattan parks, and the department's senior naturalist, Michael Feller.
Mr. Feller quickly spotted examples of healthy woodland: fine high oak, tulip, hickory, black birch, American sycamore and maple trees with "a nice sapling layer" beneath. Ground cover stalwarts included Solomon's seal and plume, woodland goldenrod, maple leaf viburnum, and even a species that he hadn't spotted in city parks: zigzag goldenrod.
Around 162d Street we came upon a thin-soiled, rocky meadow overlooking the Harlem River. "This is how Central Park probably looked before it was engineered," Mr. Feller said.
Heading on, we came to a deep forest valley, or clove, dropping away from the trail. Down its center a tiny brook burbled over rocks, all but hidden by a sea of delicate, damp-loving jewelweed. So complete was the overhead leaf canopy that the late morning light was filtered to softness amid echoing bird sounds.
Ms. Schachat pointed out that despite the steepness of the valley, thick ground cover and an apparent absence of any fires had prevented erosion. "A good hunk of forest," she said. Only yards away, traffic rushed along the Harlem River Drive.
Back on the trail we suddenly saw, poking above the trees, one of the city's most prominent landmarks: the 200-foot-high, campanile-style stone tower rising from the promontory at the western end of the equally magnificent High Bridge. Both tower and bridge were elements of the Croton aqueduct system, which first brought drinking water to New York City from Westchester County in 1848. The origins of Highbridge Park are here, where promenaders who had crossed the bridge stopped to rest and gaze at the Harlem River valley vista.
While the High Bridge walkway has long been closed off, the tower is occasionally opened to visitors. The full-circle view from the top of the tower is exhilarating, but be prepared for a long climb up the tower's intricately patterned cast-iron stairway. A small reservoir had been behind High Bridge tower. In the 1930's, Robert Moses made it into one of the city's largest municipal swimming pools, which is still operating.
Remarkably, three bridges -- the Washington, the Hamilton and the pedestrian High Bridge -- traverse the midpoint of Highbridge Park within seven blocks -- one of the densest bridge massings in the city. Rather than being a fatal incursion, however, the spans with their warren of access ramps lend to this stretch of park beneath them an extra dimension of drama.
For years, the very grand double stairway into Highbridge Park from the little-known Laurel Hill Terrace at West 184th Street descended into a thicket of weeds battling for dominance with garbage pitched over the park walls, which bear an unmistakable familial resemblance to Vaux's perimeter walls in Central Park. Now, in a joint effort by the Restoration Project and the Parks Department, the charming path below Laurel Hill Terrace has been restored.
Ms. Midler's hand is evident here in the elaborate plantings around the stairway: compact holly, climbing hydrangea, two kinds of roses, cinquefoil, spirea, raspberry bushes, daffodils and flowering apple trees. What can't be changed, however, is Yeshiva University's egregiously bulky science tower, whose loading platforms are positioned directly in front of the grand stairway.
A bit farther on, at 190th Street, where the Raoul Wallenberg Playground has been newly landscaped, a paved path descends into the northern end of the park. The steep slope is contoured here by rugged rock terraces, apparently a W.P.A. project from the 1930's. Specimen fruit trees abound, along with pheasants in full throat.
The northern end of Highbridge is at Dyckman Street, where Mr. Stern, who grew up nearby on Post Avenue, fondly remembers finding a spring, one of many in the park.
While Highbridge is ready for visitors, word is not yet out. On the day I explored the park with Mr. Feller and Ms. Schachat, from 8 A.M. until almost noon, we saw not one other person on the trails.
If you have built up an appetite during a Highbridge hike, a dining opportunity beckons at the south end of the park, below Coogan's Bluff on the Harlem River Drive at 158th Street: Guagua Amarilla, or Yellow Bus, a school bus transformed into a roadside restaurant specializing in down-home Dominican fare. Car service drivers flock to it. Inside, the bus is startlingly clean, given the constant cooking, and multiple skylights let the heat out. A full range of meat stews, roast chicken, seafood and vegetarian dishes are available. With excellent lemonade, two can eat for under $10. Just ask for what you want; there is no menu and no place to eat on the bus. But the shade of the park is just outside the door.
 
On the Trails

SECTION: Section E; Part 1; Page 20; Column 5; Movies, Performing Arts/Weekend Desk

LENGTH: 2209 words

HEADLINE: MOVIE GUIDE

 BODY:

Here is a selective listing by critics of The Times of new or noteworthy movies and film series playing this weekend in New York City. * denotes a highly recommended film or series. Ratings and running times are in parentheses. An index of reviews of films opening today appears on page 7.
 
Now Playing
 
* "BESIEGED," starring Thandie Newton and David Thewlis. Directed by Bernardo Bertolucci (R, 92 minutes). Like all Bertolucci's films, the love story of an expatriate African woman (Ms. Newton) fleeing an oppressive military regime and the reclusive British pianist (Mr. Thewlis) for whom she works as a housekeeper in Rome is a visual jewel. Based on a short story by James Lasdun, "Besieged" is a film of few words and powerful images. (Especially of a military coup in an impoverished African country.) The love is sealed through an extraordinary act of self-sacrifice and a deepening appreciation of each other's tastes in music. If this elliptical movie leaves too much unsaid, it has strong understated performances by its two stars (Stephen Holden). 

* "COOKIE'S FORTUNE," starring Charles S. Dutton, Glenn Close, Patricia Neal, Julianne Moore and Liv Tyler. Directed by Robert Altman (PG-13, 118 minutes). What a treat. The sweet assurance and guerrilla wit of Mr. Altman's vintage ensemble films make a copacetic return with a Southern small-town black comedy. Seamlessly made and completely enveloping, it tells of the machinations set off when a wealthy old woman (Ms. Neal) outrages her diabolically genteel niece (Ms. Close) by committing suicide. As the old woman's dearest friend and the niece's unwitting nemesis, Mr. Dutton is rock solid and appealing. The entire cast works on a single wavelength as Mr. Altman makes the most of a well-woven screenplay by Anne Rapp (Janet Maslin).
* "THE DREAMLIFE OF ANGELS," starring Elodie Bouchez and Natacha Regnier. Directed by Erick Zonca (R, 113 minutes; in French with English subtitles). In this very special French film about two freewheeling young women, their turbulent friendship is frayed by sexual passion and economic realities. The weaker and more head-turning of the women (Ms. Regnier) falls for the advances of a rich nightclub owner, while her fiery friend, played with unforgettable immediacy by Ms. Bouchez, rages against what she views as imminent disaster. In the process, Mr. Zonca shows astonishing acuity in observing how the women's diverging values affect every aspect of their lives. A rare and wrenching drama (Maslin).
* "EDGE OF 17," starring Chris Stafford and Tina Holmes. Directed by David Moreton (not rated, 100 minutes). It's 1984 in Sandusky, Ohio, and Eric (Chris Stafford), a skinny, fresh-faced high school student who worships the Eurythmics and Culture Club, has his first homosexual experiences. This is not boy-meets-boy (Romeo meets Romeo and they fall madly in love) but boy is used and confused and tries to go straight by sleeping with his former girlfriend. The teen-age coming-out story is realistic but warmhearted, the best among the recent gay coming-out films (Holden).
* "ELECTION," starring Matthew Broderick and Reese Witherspoon. Directed by Alexander Payne (R, 105 minutes). From the acerbically funny director of "Citizen Ruth" comes this caustic satire of the American electoral process in a high school setting. Its leading candidate, Tracy Flick, played tartly by Ms. Witherspoon, has a mother who sought tactical advice for Tracy from Connie Chung and Elizabeth Dole. Mr. Broderick plays the hapless good-guy teacher who thinks this wicked little front-runner needs to be stopped. And the hunky newcomer Chris Klein plays Tracy's reluctant opponent in a style best described as early Keanu. With a lovely ear for hypocritical conversation, the screenplay specializes in keeping all the characters a few beats off what they actually mean (Maslin).
"ENTRAPMENT," starring Sean Connery and Catherine Zeta-Jones. Directed by Jon Amiel (PG-13, 105 minutes). This glossy caper summons the days when being a thief was the profession of choice among debonair movie heroes. And heroines: as the great-looking insurance investigator who is supposedly investigating Mr. Connery's glamorous super-burglar, Ms. Zeta-Jones makes a smashing gymnast and heist expert in her own right. No need to believe the outlandish details of the story; there's enough verve and charisma to the stars to make the escapade easy on the eyes. The action sequence, in which the stars hang off the side of the world's largest skyscraper, is a vertiginous show stopper. And here's a nice bonus: nobody gets killed (Maslin).
"THE LOVE LETTER," starring Kate Capshaw, Blythe Danner, Ellen DeGeneres, Tom Everett Scott and Tom Selleck. Directed by Peter No-sun Chan (R, 88 minutes). Cathleen Schine's whimsical romantic novel about a summer affair between a divorced bookstore owner (Ms. Capshaw) and a college student half her age (Mr. Scott) bleaches out pungent ruminations on May-September love in favor of a bland cuteness. The teeny-weeny story revolves around an unaddressed, unsigned love letter that floats into people's lives and recharges their romantic fantasies. The dialogue is flat, the level of the acting grade-B sitcom (Holden).
"THE MATRIX," starring Keanu Reeves, Laurence Fishburne and Carrie-Anne Moss. Directed by Andy and Larry Wachowski (R, 120 minutes). The time-traveling story steeped in virtual reality will be all but incomprehensible to anyone but techno freaks. And the film borrows unapologetically from other sci-fi extravaganzas, with a big debt to "The X-Files" in the paranoia department. But the storytelling is intricately bizarre and the special effects are indeed special, notably when it comes to midair martial arts with a gravity-defying gusto. And Mr. Reeves shows that you can look like a Prada model while behaving like an action hero. One of the main stars here is Bill Pope, an inventive cinematographer. He and the Wachowskis provide a couch-potato epiphany: while the characters seem to be performing marvelous feats, they're often lying stock-still in the grip of virtual reality (Maslin).
"A MIDSUMMER NIGHT'S DREAM," starring Rupert Everett, Calista Flockhart, Kevin Kline, Michelle Pfeiffer and Stanley Tucci. Directed by Michael Hoffman (PG-13, 115 minutes). This is potluck Shakespeare, with a wildly uneven range of performances and no clear style holding them together. Rather than wonder why the actors are wrestling with bicycles in 19th-century Tuscany (a setting used much less enchantingly than it might have been), it's best to focus on the most comfortably Shakespearean performances and not wonder what Ms. Flockhart is doing here. Mr. Kline makes a perfect Bottom, Mr. Everett slinks seductively through Oberon's role and Ms. Pfeiffer makes an appropriately gorgeous fairy queen. The younger cast members are largely used as window dressing for the Leo-Does-Romeo set (Maslin).
"THE MUMMY," starring Brendan Fraser, Rachel Weisz, Arnold Vosloo and John Hannah. Directed by Stephen Sommers (PG-13, 134 minutes). More like a video game than a movie, this incoherent spoof (very loosely based on the 1932 Boris Karloff horror classic), overloaded with cheesy special effects, suggests an unwieldy hybrid of "Raiders of the Lost Ark" and "Abbott and Costello Meet the Mummy" via "Night of the Living Dead." The intrepid adventurers who disturb an ancient Egyptian crypt that carries a fearful curse are Mr. Fraser (playing a swashbuckling American in the Foreign Legion), Ms. Weisz (as a plucky, wide-eyed English librarian) and John Hannah (as her twitty older brother). It's all played for laughs. The only scary moments involve swarming scarabs who burrow under the skin and do awful "Alien"-like things (Holden).
* "SHAKESPEARE IN LOVE" (the Oscar winner for best film, best original screenplay, best art direction, best costumes and best comedy score). Starring Joseph Fiennes, Gwyneth Paltrow (the best-actress winner) and Judi Dench (best supporting actress). Directed by John Madden (R, 113 minutes). A witty, sexy and merrily literate delight, with an exhilaratingly clever premise that only gets better as the film unfolds. The screenplay, originating as Marc Norman's brainstorm and turned by Tom Stoppard into razor-sharp dialogue reminiscent of his "Rosencrantz and Guildenstern Are Dead," dares to imagine whatever it likes about the link between Shakespeare's artistic passions and his mad yearning for a certain aristocratic beauty. Meanwhile, this tirelessly inventive comedy envisions an Elizabethan theater fraught with the same backbiting and conniving we enjoy today and has great fun presenting the creation of "Romeo and Juliet," glitches and all. Mr. Fiennes and Ms. Paltrow make a fine, tempestuous duo, and she gives the first great, fully realized starring performance of her career (Maslin).
* "STAR WARS: EPISODE I -- THE PHANTOM MENACE," starring Liam Neeson, Ewan McGregor, Natalie Portman and Jake Lloyd. Directed by George Lucas (PG-13, 132 minutes). Stripped of hype and breathless expectations, Mr. Lucas's first installment offers a happy surprise: it's up to snuff. It sustains the gee-whiz spirit of the series and offers a swashbuckling extra-galactic getaway, creating illusions that are even more plausible than the kitchen-raiding raptors of "Jurassic Park." While the human stars here are indeed reduced to playing action figures, they are upstaged by amazing backdrops and hordes of crazily lifelike space beings as the Lewis Carroll in Mr. Lucas is given free rein. The "Star Wars" franchise was funnier and scrappier when it was new. But it simply wasn't capable of this (Maslin).
"TEA WITH MUSSOLINI," starring Cher, Dame Judi Dench, Joan Plowright, Dame Maggie Smith and Lily Tomlin. Directed by Franco Zeffirelli (PG, 117 minutes). The screen divas (especially Dame Maggie) who give "Tea With Mussolini" a patina of class obviously relish chewing the scenery in this sudsy historical drama (adapted from a chapter of Mr. Zeffirelli's autobiography) about a circle of English expatriate women living in Florence on the eve of World War II. Dame Maggie, a quintessential British snob, is the leader of the pack, nicknamed the Scorpioni, whose comfortable lives are shattered when the Fascists rise to power and they find themselves carted off to another city where they are held under house arrest. Cher is her usual likable self as a free-spending, free-loving wealthy American heiress and art collector who befriends the group (Holden).
"THIS IS MY FATHER," starring Aidan Quinn, Moya Farrelly and James Caan. Directed by Paul Quinn (R, 120 minutes). Made with obvious sincerity, this story of a glum Chicago schoolteacher (Mr. Caan) in search of his more interesting Irish forebears (Mr. Quinn) involves a third Quinn brother as well. And Declan Quinn's cinematography adds warmth to an atmospheric, sentimental tale featuring stellar Irish actors (Stephen Rea, Brendan Gleeson, Colm Meaney) in cameo roles. Aidan Quinn's sturdy performance, as a lonely farmer in love with a fiery young lass from a prosperous family, does a lot to help the film override its hackneyed elements and keep it moving (Maslin).
* "THE WINSLOW BOY," starring Nigel Hawthorne, Rebecca Pidgeon and Jeremy Northam. Written and directed by David Mamet (G, 110 minutes). Terence Rattigan's play about the 1910 schoolboy's trial that created a furor in England has been artfully pared down to its essentials in this stately and deliberate adaptation. Played with unusually effective restraint by a fine, meticulous cast, it works as a precisely calibrated war of nerves in which the family of a cadet accused of theft is quietly staggered by the consequences of that accusation. Benoit Delhomme's cinematography is beautifully lucid and painterly. Ms. Pidgeon's defiantly arch acting style sets the film's tone and gives her suffragist character a sharp edge (Maslin).
 
Film Series
 
"COMING TIMES: NEW IRISH CINEMA." Six films by Neil Jordan serve as the centerpiece of this series opening today at the Walter Reade Theater. The series carries forward the Film Society of Lincoln Center's comprehensive survey of Irish cinema, which began five years ago with a series that spanned the years from 1916 to 1994. The Jordan films include "The Crying Game" (1992), "Michael Collins" (1996), "Angel" a k a "Danny Boy" (1982), "The Miracle" (1991), "The Company of Wolves" (1984) and "The Butcher Boy" (1998). The series consists of more than 20 feature films as well as shorts, and brims with outstanding Irish actors like Stephen Rea, Liam Neeson, Aidan Quinn, Brendan Gleason, Gabriel Byrne, Donal Donnelly, Richard Harris, Brenda Fricker and John Lynch. Opening day's attractions include two United States premieres, Mark Staunton's romantic comedy, "Separation Anxiety" (1997) at 2 P.M., and Colm Villa's post-millennial combination of parable and horror movie, "Sunset Heights" (1998) at 6:15 P.M. The series continues through June 10 at the Walter Reade Theater, 165 West 65th Street. Admission, $8.50; $5 for Film Society members; $4.50 for the elderly at weekday matinees. Information: (212) 875-5600 (Lawrence Van Gelder).

HEADLINE: OPERA REVIEW;
Two Familiar Tales in Unfamiliar Settings

BYLINE: By ANTHONY TOMMASINI 

 BODY:
Since 1982 the Center for Contemporary Opera, founded by the conductor Richard Marshall, has presented productions of 37 new or recent operas. No company large or small has a better record of service to the cause. On Tuesday night in Kaye Playhouse at Hunter College, Mr. Marshall and his forces presented two more, each in one act, each by an American: the world premiere of Jack Beeson's "Sorry, Wrong Number" and Elie Siegmeister's "Angel Levine," which received its premiere at the 92d Street Y in 1985 and has languished since.
Mr. Beeson prepared the libretto for "Sorry, Wrong Number" himself, basing it on the original radio play by Lucille Fletcher, not the 1948 film adaptation that starred Barbara Stanwyck. For this thriller to work, the audience must accept an improbable and hideous coincidence. An Upper East Side woman, confined to a wheelchair, tries frantically to call her wealthy husband, who told her he was working late at the office. Telephone lines accidentally cross, and she overhears two criminals plotting a contract murder for that night. Shaken, she tries to alert an operator, then the police. She herself, it winds up, is the intended victim, and her husband is party to the plot. 
 Mr. Beeson's most notable opera, "Lizzie Borden," was a popular success at the New York City Opera this season. And at 77 he is still at it. The dramatic pacing of this new 40-minute work is economical and sure; the orchestra of 17 players provides color and imaginative evocations of dial tones and busy signals.
That said, Mr. Beeson may be overly deferential to his conversational text. The music, grounded in tonality but spiked with dissonance, paces the story and highlights interchanges, but it only skirts the surface of the drama. Aspiring opera composers could learn much by studying the natural way he sets text, though in this he was helped by the strong cast, especially the soprano Patricia Dell, who was compelling and pitiable as the unwitting victim.
Bernard Malamud's story "The Angel Levine" was the basis for a 1970 film starring Zero Mostel as Nathan Manischevitz, a poor, elderly tailor in New York of the 1950's, coping with the fatal illness of his wife, and Harry Belafonte as the unlikely answer to his prayers: a black Jewish Harlem hipster who claims to be an angel. Siegmeister, who died in 1991, often composed in a rigorous, thickly chromatic language touched by 12-tone elements. Yet he was also versed in musical theater and loved indigenous American musical styles. These dimensions of his music come together, sometimes oddly, in "Angel Levine." The stern, atonal music gives some of the exchanges, like that between Nathan and his wife, a gritty intensity, though it was sometimes hard for the singers to project over the brassy, busy chamber orchestra.
When Nathan wanders into a Harlem nightclub searching for the angel, Siegmeister takes us there with jazzy music for sultry blues singer, though the lyrics are embarrassing. ("I'm gonna fry my man some chitlins.") The stage director, Charles Maryan, did not try to soften the stereotypes. Three black Jews intoning Hebrew prayers in a Harlem temple break into a soul-daddy dance, complete with palms outstretched to heaven, and one character with Coke-bottle-thick glasses blows like Gabriel on an imaginary trumpet.
Yet "Angel Levine" essentially works. The story is wistful and oddly affecting, and Siegmeister's eclectic score is always interesting. The performers were winning, too, especially Richard Frisch as Nathan and Sam McKelton as Angel.
Mr. Marshall conducted both works ably.

HEADLINE: The Military's Myopia

 BODY:

To the Editor:
Re "America's Promise, Found in the Army" (news article, May 23):
It only seems right that many of our new immigrants are taking advantage of the economic opportunities that the United States armed forces offer in exchange for a recruit's commitment to defend our country. 
 A young man or woman facing a life of limited earning power can turn his or her life around with the college tuition and vocational training provided by the military.
What a shame it is, then, that the armed forces and Congress have joined ranks to deny this economic opportunity to gay American citizens, while aggressively seeking to recruit immigrants, many of whom are not yet citizens. At a time of dangerously low enlistment rates, the military's anti-gay policy is inexcusable as well as morally bankrupt.
 
JEFFREY OEBERST

HEADLINE: Residential Real Estate;
Rental Complex Is Bronxville's First in 30 Years

BYLINE: By RACHELLE GARBARINE 

 BODY:
The first new rental complex in Bronxville, N.Y., in 30 years opened this week, with rents ranging from $2,100 to $8,000 a month. The 110-apartment complex has been built on a 1.7-acre site that had eluded development for nearly a decade, replacing a gas station and a small commercial building.
The $25 million project, called the Avalon, is being developed by Collins Enterprises, L.L.C., of Old Greenwich, Conn., and Avalon Bay Communities, a real estate investment trust based in Alexandria, Va. Its apartments have one to three bedrooms and range from 740 to 2,066 square feet. The apartments are in two buildings -- one has three stories and the other has five -- flanking an interior landscaped courtyard on the site at Parkway Road and Milburn Street across the street from a Metro-North train station. There is also underground parking for 166 cars. 
 Monthly rents for the smaller apartments, which predominate, are $2,100 to $4,200, while the 10 three-bedroom units rent for $6,000 to $8,000. The project is designed to appeal to prosperous empty-nesters and professional couples in the area who want to sell their homes and rent either for the maintenance-free life style or until they buy another home.
The developers said they also hoped to attract local renters looking to trade up, corporate nomads who know they will be moving in a few years and renters from Manhattan, including Wall Street executives, seeking a convenient, if not necessarily less-expensive, alternative.
In the two weeks since leasing began, tenants have signed leases for 21 of the apartments, the developers said. Initial occupancy begins next month.
Collins Enterprises got involved with the project in 1995, just over a decade after local developers had spent most of the 1980's obtaining preliminary approvals from the village for a condominium building. But by the time the approvals were granted in 1991, the real estate market had fallen into a deep recession, halting that project and forcing the developers to file for bankruptcy.
From 1995 to 1997, Collins, as part of a bankruptcy reorganization plan, took over the development site, obtained local approvals for its rental complex and joined with Avalon Bay to develop it. Construction started early last year.
"The market moved in our direction in the last three years and this is an exclusive site that can't be duplicated and is well suited for rental housing," said Arthur Collins 2d, president of Collins Enterprises.
Susan R. Bresler, development director at the Manhattan office of Avalon Bay, said that the project was "an opportunity to enter a high-quality area where there is a high shortage of rentals."
Avalon Bay, which builds only rentals, provided the financing for and will manage the Bronxville project, its seventh, together totaling 2,000 units, in the immediate New York, New Jersey and Connecticut area. Six other projects, including 2,500 more apartments, are under way or planned in the area.
Village officials wanted to see development on the site, which William T. Regan, Bronxville's administrator, said had become an eyesore. But he said there were community concerns over traffic and parking and overburdening municipal resources, especially schools.
To address those issues, the developers designed the Avalon to have 55 one-bedroom units and included a provision in the leases that sets a limit of two people per bedroom in each apartment. The project's garage is also large enough to accommodate tenants' parking needs.
Mr. Regan said the developers, as part of securing their approvals, were also required to make more than $100,000 worth of improvements to a nearby traffic circle to enhance pedestrian safety, as well as upgrade sidewalks and install new lighting in front of their project. That work, he added, is part of a larger improvement program by the village to make similar upgrades to Parkway Road and Palmer Avenue, a mixed commercial and residential area on the west side of the railroad tracks.
The rental buildings are faced with brick and stucco and have been designed in a Tudor style to blend with the character of the village. And though the complex is close to the train station, the use of two-inch-thick insulation behind the brick facade and double-pane windows "work as the best buffer against noise," Ms. Bresler said.
The project's design and convenience appealed to Sarah J. McCann, who with her husband, Wayne G. Bartlett, will move from a two-bedroom rental on Manhattan's Upper West Side to a one-bedroom unit with a den at the Avalon. The couple, both Australians, relocated to Manhattan one and a half years ago because of their jobs but may return to their native country by the end of next year.
"We looked at Bronxville when we first moved to Manhattan, but the apartments available then didn't appeal to us," Ms. McCann said. "At the Avalon, we're getting a new kitchen and bath."
Ms. McCann said that their new apartment was similar in size to their current one and that their rent would be slightly less than what they paid in Manhattan. "But," she added, "we are gaining because we have suburban living and access to the city as well as to upstate New York and Connecticut." She said the move would not add much time to her commute to her job in midtown Manhattan or to her husband's.
Ms. Bresler said rents at the Avalon were comparable to some of the condominium and cooperative apartments in prewar buildings in the village that are rented. And, she added, they are 25 percent lower than those in Manhattan, where apartments are smaller.
