HEADLINE: NYC;
Despite Holes, Good Enough For a City Plan

BYLINE: By Clyde Haberman 

 BODY:
LIFE is enriched by its little mysteries, like who first looked at a lobster and said, "Mmm, I want to eat that," and what was the greatest thing before sliced bread.
Municipal government is not nearly as compelling, but it does have mysteries of its own. One has come to the fore with a much-disputed plan for a rail link to Kennedy International Airport: 
 How is it that city agencies can study one of the most important public works projects in decades, conclude it has more holes than a harmonica and still tell its backers to go ahead?
That is essentially what happened early this month when the City Planning Commission helped clear the way for a so-called Airtrain to Kennedy, despite having almost nothing good to say about it. Next week, the City Council will take its turn, and few would be shocked if the same thing happened.
Council staff members have also studied this project, a $1.5 billion baby nurtured by the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey. They, too, have nothing but doubt, with a capital D. Mostly it is focused on the Port Authority's plans for an elevated light-rail line above the Van Wyck Expressway, running 3.1 miles from Kennedy to Jamaica, Queens. At Jamaica, travelers would switch to the Long Island Rail Road or to the E, J or Z subway lines, in most cases to continue the journey to mid-Manhattan.
In other words, New York would not get what other international cities have: a seamless "one seat" ride from the airport to the heart of the city.
Given that basic fact, and using the Port Authority's own projections, the planning commission strongly doubted that more than a few thousand travelers would use the Airtrain each day -- not nearly enough, most commissioners said with open disdain, to make it a success.
Even so, they held their noses and gave their approval, focusing on narrow land-use concerns. In large measure, they acted because the Mayor had already lent his support, however unenthusiastically. The desire to see something -- anything -- get built runs high in a city where grand public works programs are remembered only by citizens with the grayest hair.
Now, the question is whether the City Council will go the same route; it is effectively the Airtrain's final municipal stop. Ultimately, everything depends on Peter F. Vallone, the Council Speaker, who says through a spokesman that he is neutral for now.
You'd be hard pressed to find a room big enough to contain the many high-priced lobbyists, consultants and political musclemen hired by all parties to this highly contentious issue.
People living by the Van Wyck don't want the noise, traffic and air pollution that train construction would bring. They have an unusual ally in the Air Transport Association, an airline trade group unhappy that the project will be financed largely through airport passenger fees. It wants all that money to be spent only at Kennedy itself.
On the other side are politically powerful contractors and trade unions, salivating at the prospect of 4,000-plus construction jobs. With them are businesses and political leaders in southeast Queens who see an economic development offer they can't refuse. "If we lose this opportunity, we're not going to see it again in my lifetime or yours," warned Archie Spigner, a senior Councilman from Queens.
Y OU would have to look far and wide to find a city planner opposed to the concept of a train to the plane. New York is close to being strangled by traffic jams on the highways leading to its airports. Salvation, the consensus holds, lies in a train ride that is swift and reliable.
The question is whether the Port Authority plan does the trick.
Absolutely not, critics say. They cannot imagine many tourists or business travelers taking the subway in Manhattan, getting off at Jamaica and then lugging their bags up and down stairs, or handing them to planned airline check-in counters there. The word "schlep" comes up a lot.
The Port Authority replies that this is, by far, the best that can be done because tunnels under the East River rule out a one-seat ride for now. Some day that ride will come, the authority promises.
Who will win this critical fight? Stay tuned. About the only thing not a mystery, said Walter L. McCaffrey, another Queens Councilman, is that no lobbyist or consultant is about to go hungry.

HEADLINE: An Eastern Alternative to Blockbusterwood

BYLINE: By Neal Gabler; Neal Gabler is the author, most recently, of "Life the Movie: How Entertainment Conquered Reality."

DATELINE: AMAGANSETT, N.Y.

 BODY:
Call it Brooklyn's revenge for decades of indignities suffered at the hands of Hollywood. That is one thing that came to mind when Robert De Niro and Miramax Films announced a tentative agreement with New York City on a $150 million plan to develop the abandoned Brooklyn Navy Yard as a film-making complex.
Given the financial and political obstacles still to be hurdled, no one knows if the project will ever be built -- but whatever happens, there may be something more at work here than economics, civic pride or the desire of New York-based actors for a production complex closer to home. There is a recognition that America's movie culture is changing -- a change that is reflected in the difference between Hollywood and Brooklyn. 
 For two decades, Hollywood has been relying primarily on special-effects-driven blockbusters. Not only are they the pictures most likely to appeal to young males (still the largest demographic cohort among filmgoers); they are also most likely to appeal to viewers overseas, a more profitable venue now than the domestic market.
Yes, there have been big-budget disasters like "Waterworld" and "Godzilla," and some low-budget films like "The Full Monty" and "Shakespeare in Love" have become modest hits. But Hollywood is like a casino. To win really big you have to wager really big. "Shakespeare in Love" has grossed roughly $100 million domestically. "Star Wars: Part One -- The Phantom Menace" exceeded that in its first five days.
While the Hollywood blockbuster still rules the box office, its esthetic hegemony faces a new challenge. The competitors are smaller, less bombastic films often called independents even though they are likely to have been made or distributed by divisions of major studios. They tend to emphasize words over action, nuance over size, emotion over sensation. These emphases don't necessarily make independent films better than the Hollywood blockbusters. But they do make them different.
The Hollywood blockbuster testifies to the tyranny of the young over popular culture -- television shows, music, magazines and advertising. The independent film testifies to a backlash by baby boomers hoping to retake a small portion of what they had ceded.
Still, if the big money is elsewhere, why does Hollywood care about boomers? Because in an industry racked by an overwhelming sense of intellectual inferiority, the "indies" provide prestige: one film can instantly turn a producer from a mercenary into an esthete. As the critic Richard Schickel once summed it up to me, "In Hollywood, pictures are either made for teen-agers or for Oscars."
Which brings us back to Brooklyn. Where a movie is made may not seem to be of much consequence, yet it can actually make all the difference in the world.
Early in this century New York City was the center of the film industry, and many films had a certain New Yorkishness about them, which usually manifested itself as either urban or urbane. Urban films dealt with the struggles of city life: immigrant assimilation, labor strife, disease, crime and delinquency, prostitution. Urbane films dealt with the problems of the wealthy and sophisticated: infidelity, passion, ennui, social climbing. It is a tradition that survives in the urban films of Martin Scorsese and Sidney Lumet and the urbane films of Woody Allen.
The shift to Hollywood changed all that. From the time the first movie moguls decamped from the East around 1910 and arrived in California, they found a tabula rasa on which they could write their dreams.
Inevitably, the movies were cast in the expansive image of the place. Everything in Hollywood, from the swashbucklers of Douglas Fairbanks to the Biblical spectacles of Cecil B. DeMille to the science fiction epics of George Lucas, suggests escape. Escapism worked: by 1922 only 12 percent of American films were being produced in New York; 84 percent were being produced in Hollywood.
The recent surge in independent production, however, may redress things a bit. Just as New York could never have sustained the fantasies promulgated by the Hollywood film, Hollywood may not be able to sustain the maturity of the so-called independent film. The hyperkinetic Hollywood ethos just isn't congenial to that kind of picture. It needs a new venue -- an anti-Hollywood.
England and other outposts across North America have been performing that function, but there may be no better candidate for the job than Brooklyn. In old Hollywood movies, Brooklynites -- always ferret-faced, with their nasal accents, their fractured syntax of "dese, dems and dose," and their hands flapping like semaphore flags -- were aliens.
Set them down in the small-town, middle-class domain of those films, say, as an Army buddy, and the locals were in absolute wonderment that anyone actually behaved this way. It was the difference between the Brooklyn Dodgers, with No. 1 fan Hilda Chester rattling her cowbell, and the Los Angeles Dodgers, cheered on by Frank Sinatra and Cary Grant.
Yes, it's difficult to imagine prosaic Brooklyn as a Hollywood East. But with American film and American film audiences increasingly divided between the blockbuster, which reaps the bulk of profits, and the mature film, which has been reaping the bulk of the Oscars, one can hope that American film making may someday be divided between Hollywood and Brooklyn.
As they say in Flatbush, "Who wudda thunk it?"

SECTION: Section B; Page 9; Column 2; Arts & Ideas/Cultural Desk 

LENGTH: 1499 words

HEADLINE: What Murder Says About the Society It Exists In

BYLINE: By PETER APPLEBOME 

 BODY:
At the heart of the analysis of the killings last month at Columbine High School in Colorado has been a deceptively simple question: Were the killers chillingly aberrant monsters or were they a part, however perverse, of something in the culture as a whole?
But long before Columbine, this question and others were being asked by professors as homicide became an increasingly conspicuous slice of American scholarship.
Recent books have surveyed murder in the United States and the world, explored the meaning of murder narratives and unearthed vast detail and sociological significance in celebrated 19th-century cases. There is a three-year-old journal, "Homicide Studies: An Interdisciplinary Journal." These and other projects suggest that just as "true crime" has become a permanent fixture in publishing, murder may be establishing itself as a valid course of study in academia. 
 "Homicide has always been the backbone of history -- 'legitimate' homicide, which is war," said Roger Lane, a Haverford College historian and author of "Murder in America" (Ohio State University Press, 1998). "It has only been in the past generation that social historians have discovered how much homicide has to tell us about the society in which it exists."
Historians have been drawn to celebrated murder cases in the past, and criminologists and sociologists have long studied the incidence of homicide and how to reduce it.
But only recently have scholars begun to focus so intensely on murder as a window on society and a revealing subject for social or intellectual historians. Dr. Lane's book, for example, tracks the incidence of murder and the culture's response to it from medieval England to modern times, using murder -- and which murders are deemed to matter -- as a way of taking the social temperature on issues of race and class and attitudes toward children and the sexes.
Patricia Cline Cohen of the University of California at Santa Barbara and Amy Gilman Srebnick of Montclair State University each examined different 19th-century cases of murdered women in New York to analyze attitudes about women, sexuality and class.
"Murder Most Foul: The Killer and the American Gothic Imagination" (Harvard University Press, 1998) by Karen Halttunen of the University of California at Davis tracks the way the culture has imagined and portrayed murder. She argues that the depictions of murder reveal a shift from values based in the Puritan religion to secular ones. In the first case, murderers are seen as sinners in a community of sinners, whose fall is a warning to everyone. In the second, the killer is seen as an alien monster whose crimes reflect his separation from the rest of society. She calls this the Gothic homicide narrative.
M. Dwayne Smith, a sociologist at the University of North Carolina at Charlotte and editor of the Journal of Homicide Studies, said that it was natural for scholars to be drawn to murder.
"There's a joke that if a total alien came and did a content analysis of American literature and pop culture, he'd conclude that we kill one another and we fall in love," he said. "The culture has such a tremendous fascination with homicide, you would expect to find academic interest as well."
But until recently homicide was largely left to the criminologists and sociologists, whose research tends toward the pieces in Homicide Studies on topics like "Suicide Following Homicide in Canada" or "Murder as Self-Help: Women and Intimate Partner Homicide."
Daniel A. Cohen, a historian at Florida International University who wrote a survey of crime literature, cited two developments that made homicide more appealing to historians and other scholars.
The first, he said, was the development of the new social history in the 1970's, which argued for depictions of the poor, minorities, women, religious and ethnic minorities and others who had been often overlooked in histories oriented toward the rich and powerful. The second was a renewed respect for narrative rather than quantitative or technical analysis in recent years.
Thus "The Murder of Helen Jewett" (Knopf, 1998) by Ms. Cohen and "The Mysterious Death of Mary Rogers" (Oxford University Press, 1997) by Ms. Srebnick take murders of women, the first a prostitute killed in 1836, the second a 20-year-old descendant of prominent New England settlers, killed in 1841, and combine compelling storytelling with bottom-up social analysis. (Edgar Allan Poe based "The Mystery of Marie Roget" on the Rogers case.) Ms. Cohen said newspaper accounts and trial transcripts -- usually kept unofficially by reporters rather than by the courts -- provide much material about the lives of ordinary and marginalized groups that would otherwise be extremely hard to find.
"It's one of the best ways to hear from people who don't normally publish books or get their words written down," she said. "There were 20 different court reporters putting out transcripts in the Helen Jewett trial. Normally you would not hear about the life of a prostitute except through the eyes of moral reformers."
For some scholars homicide is most interesting not as a reflection of cultural forces but as a subject in itself, particularly given the consistently high murder rates in the United States.
Eric Monkkonen, a historian at the University of California at Los Angeles, has spent nearly 12 years studying 200 years' worth of homicide in New York City. Mr. Monkkonen is part of a loosely knit group of about 250 researchers from various disciplines called the Homicide Research Working Group.
"We don't usually get much of a long view of homicide," he said. "We're always thinking of the past 10 years and very seldom looking at the past 200. If someone were to say to me: 'Why 200 years? It's not enough. It should be 300?" I'd say, 'I agree.' "
Mr. Monkkonen said his research more than anything convinces him that the shifts in homicide rates reflect a web of social, economic and technological factors ranging from changes in policing and weapons to shifts in employment that may take a generation to take hold, rather than any single government policy. That leads him to be wary of attributing credit for a current decline in the city's homicide rate or expecting lower crime levels to be permanent.
Some aspects of homicide seem eternal, experts say.
Cases that get the most attention, like those of O. J. Simpson and JonBenet Ramsey now or those of Mary Rogers and Helen Jewett, often include salacious elements of passion and mystery that also cross into the tense territory of race or sexuality.
And despite widespread worries about the toxic effects of tabloid news coverage, murder has always sold. The sensational murders of the 19th century were perhaps the greatest sales tools for fledgling newspapers of the time reaching for a mass audience. Before there was Geraldo Rivera pumping up ratings with daily coverage of the Simpson case, there was Cotton Mather, the famous preacher of Colonial America.
"As early as the 1680's you had ministers who realized that murder cases provided them with a much larger audience than they normally had," Mr. Cohen said. "Usually sermons were reprinted with maybe 200 or 300 copies. Cotton Mather used to brag how he could print 1,200 or 1,500 copies of execution sermons, and they would sell out."
An element that seems new to many scholars, however, is the nation's wave of school shootings. The age of the killers and the way they strike at schools and schoolchildren defy most of the common conventions of American homicide.
Ms. Halttunen's book argues that some recent murder accounts, like the book and film "Dead Man Walking," veer back toward the earlier Puritan sense of the killer who reflected society's sins rather than an alien ogre. Yet she admits that the recent school killings have elements of both.
"We're very torn in the way we view them," she said. "Something about these school shootings may be pushing us through a wall. It's almost as if we are searching for a new language with which to discuss them."
Mr. Cohen says that this has always been the case. In his 1993 book, "Pillars of Salt, Monuments of Grace: New England Crime Literature and the Origins of American Popular Culture," he argues that accounts of reactions to homicide show that killers have traditionally been seen as insiders and outsiders simultaneously.
Much of the increase in scholarship began when murder rates were soaring. Though they are plummeting, Mr. Lane said homicide was unlikely to disappear, either as a crime or a subject of scholarship.
"I gave a talk to the Mystery Writers of America in January, and I told them not to worry, murder isn't going away," he said. "The factors that appear to be driving down homicide, I think, are short-term with clear downside boundaries, and homicide is going to rebound.
"Sex and violence have always sold. The interest in murder doesn't flag. We academics are finally catching up with it."

HEADLINE: Voting at Long Last, Nigeria Pinches Itself

BYLINE: By NORIMITSU ONISHI 

DATELINE: ABUJA, Nigeria, May 28

 BODY:
At the U-Fine Barbers Shop in the slum of Nyanya here, Nigeria's final day under military rule registered with a mixture of great hope for the future and last-minute jitters about a return to civilian government -- long promised, yet often postponed.
"It is a day we have been looking forward to," said Thomas Idoko, a 29-year-old teacher, who was getting a trim. "We thought it would never take place, but now I am expecting great changes both in the economy and society." 
 The barber, John Agbo, 21, wished that the long-awaited day would bring a more personal change. "I pray to God that I can get a richer shop," he said, mentioning air conditioning, sharper clippers, a bigger waiting area. He said he would settle, though, for reliable electricity from the Government so that his old clippers would not stop working in mid-hum.
Nigeria, whose 122 million people make it Africa's most populous nation, changes governments on Saturday; the generals who have ruled it since 1983 are giving way to a civilian Government elected in February. Since the departing President, Gen. Abdulsalami Abubakar, announced last July that a civilian government would take over on May 29, the date has loomed before Nigerians as the finishing line of a political marathon.
And only 24 hours before the day, there were doubts in Nyanya, amid the expressions of optimism, that the line would be crossed.
"I'm highly elated that the handover will take place," said Friday Nathaniel, 18, who was waiting for his turn at the barbershop. "We are praying that it should happen as it has been promised. I expect the incoming administration will do something in the area of job opportunities. I am now just like a beggar with no choice."
Nyanya is on the outskirts of this capital's center, a world away from the five-star international hotels, where Nigerian and foreign lobbyists were furiously slapping backs this week, and a dinner was held to honor Stella Obasanjo, the wife of the President-elect, Olusegun Obasanjo, the retired general and onetime military ruler.
Nigeria, ruled by the military for all but 10 years since independence from Britain in 1960, teetered on the edge of collapse under the extremely repressive and corrupt Government of Gen. Sani Abacha. But after five years in power, General Abacha died suddenly last June, setting off a series of political reforms that is culminating in the inauguration on Saturday. The Rev. Jesse Jackson, President Clinton's special envoy to Africa, is to lead the American delegation at the inauguration.
This morning, the dirt road outside U-Fine Barbers was flooded after a night of heavy rains. A block away, in a heap of uncollected garbage, a baby goat could be seen butting a rooster over an apparently tasty morsel.
Nyanya came into existence in the 1980's after Nigeria, its coffers flush from the oil boom, decided to move its capital to Abuja from Lagos. Over the years, Nigerians from seemingly every corner of this country gravitated to Nyanya, divided by religion and ethnicity but seemingly united in their concept of a better life.
"I want the new Government to increase the salaries of the workers and to help the poor," said Ezekiel Oyeleru, 13, who was milling around with other children outside the barbershop. The children have not attended school in months, since unpaid teachers began striking.
"To repair our roads," said Charles Jeremiah, 17.
"Our homes," Ezekiel said.
"To repair our schools and to reduce our school fees," Charles said.
Lazarus Mohammed, 26, struggled to push his wheelbarrow around water-filled potholes in front of the barbershop. It was laden with plastic containers that he had filled with water from a tap and that he was going to sell in a remote corner of Nyanya for 5 cents each.
"Mai ruwa!" a woman cried to him in Hausa, the dominant language and ethnic group in northern Nigeria. "Water boy! Your jerrycans are leaking."
The water boy stopped. "Nigeria will be great a year from now," he said before going on. "We are suffering now. But after suffering is enjoyment."
Elegant Ukpong, 35, was not ready to celebrate yet. Mr. Ukpong was one of the hundreds of men in Nyanya, called okada-men, who ferry passengers on their motorcycles for a living.
"People still have doubts that the handover will take place," the okada-man said. "They are afraid of all the rumors."
"Last week we heard that Obasanjo was killed," he said, referring to rumors that suddenly swept the country and caused riots in Lagos. "So we still have doubts. Until I see the new Government here -- 24 hours from now -- I will not go out and jubilate."
But then, the okada-man said, he will party. "Anything my hand can reach," he said, "I will eat to make my mind happy."

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS;
Friday Rally Can't Reverse Rough Week

BYLINE: By EDWARD WYATT 

 BODY:
After rising relentlessly for more than two months, the Dow Jones industrial average has given up a portion of its gains the last three weeks as investors begin to worry that cyclical stocks that seemed underpriced at the end of winter might be heading into the summer having risen too far or too fast.
The prospect that the Federal Reserve might raise interest rates in a pre-emptory move against an overheating economy, and the inflation that it might bring, has also put a crimp in some investors' hopes for industrial companies like Alcoa, International Paper and Caterpillar. 
 Despite a gain of 92.81 points yesterday, the Dow Jones industrial average lost 2.5 percent of its value this week, finishing at 10,559.74. That was the steepest one-week decline in the Dow since mid-January, and it left the industrial average roughly 5 percent below its peak of 11,107.19, reached on May 13.
Even after those declines, however, the bellwether market index was still 15 percent ahead of where it started the year. Trading was relatively slow yesterday on the New York Stock Exchange, with 657.8 million shares changing hands, among the lowest totals this year.
Other market indexes, which did not benefit as much from investors' spring obsession with cyclical stocks, also fell this week. The Nasdaq composite index, which gained 51.37, to 2,470.52 yesterday, nevertheless dropped 2 percent of its value this week as Internet-related stocks continued their recent declines. The Nasdaq index is up 12.67 percent so far this year.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index rose 20.43, to 1,301.84, yesterday but also lost 2.1 percent for the week. And the Russell 2000 index, which gained 5.76 points, to 438.68 yesterday and which had risen 14 percent the previous two months, fell 2.3 percent this week. Those indexes have gained 5.9 percent and 4.5 percent, respectively, since Dec. 31.
"We've had a fairly remarkable run over the last eight months," said Michael Clark, head of United States equity trading at Credit Suisse First Boston. "I always find it a little peculiar when people then try to search for an answer when we're off 5 percent. The market has been very efficient over the last three to four years at backing off when things got a little too far ahead of themselves."
Mr. Clark acknowledged that several factors seemed to be weighing heavily enough on investors that they were not eager to go into the three-day Memorial Day weekend without some hedges against potential losses. Among them is continued uncertainty about the progress of the battles in Yugoslavia, including the possibility of a ground war involving American troops.
In addition, a plentiful supply of new stock offerings has continued to come to market, tipping the balance for some investors to an oversupply of shares and not enough buyers, Mr. Clark said.
The recent declines in the Dow industrials and most other indexes retrace only a portion of recent gains. After rising in 9 of 10 weeks from the end of February through mid-May, the Dow industrial average has fallen in each of the last three weeks. Those declines date to the May 13 announcement that Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin would resign and include last week's announcement by the Federal Reserve that it might raise interest rates if it continues to see signs of inflation.
The list of Dow stocks that gained the most this spring almost perfectly matches the list of losers in the last three weeks. Alcoa, for example, rose from $40.50 to $63.875 from Feb. 28 through May 7, a gain of more than 57 percent; in the last three weeks, the shares have fallen back to $55, a 14 percent decline. Shares of Allied Signal, DuPont and Goodyear Tire and Rubber have followed similar trajectories.
Interest rates, which have generally been climbing since the beginning of the year, have risen more sharply in recent weeks. Yesterday, the price of the 30-year Treasury bond rose 9/32 , to 91 29/32 . The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction, fell slightly, to 5.82 percent from 5.84 percent on Thursday.
Among the most active stocks on American markets yesterday, Microsoft gained 2 5/16 , to 80 11/16 , making it the single biggest contributor to the day's gain in the S.& P. 500.
Yahoo gained 14 5/8, to 148, with most of the gain coming after the company announced that in completing its purchase of Geocities, it had dismissed about 200 of that company's employees, or 63 percent of the Geocities total. Yahoo will take a $68 million pretax charge in the second quarter related to the acquisition.
Paine Webber, which will become part of the S.& P. 500 index after the close of trading next Friday, rose 2 9/16 , to 47. Paine Webber will replace Bankers Trust, which is being acquired by Deutsche Bank, in the index. The announcement of its addition to the popular index usually bolsters a company's stock price, as buyers and speculators rush to buy the shares in the hope that more buying will come from large investors who track the index.
The hottest stock in American markets continued to be Starmedia, which gained 13 3/8, to 58 7/8, yesterday. Starmedia, an on-line network with content aimed at Spanish and Portuguese speakers in Latin America, completed an initial public offering of seven million shares on Wednesday. The shares, offered at $15, finished the first day at just over $26. On Thursday, the shares gained another 75 percent, to $45.50.

HEADLINE: Tenuous Trickle-Down

BYLINE: By Glenn C. Loury; Glenn C. Loury is an economics professor and director of the Institute on Race and Social Division at Boston University.

DATELINE: BOSTON

 BODY:
Certainly it is good news that the benefits of a vigorous economy have started to trickle down toward young black men in the inner cities. According to a recent study by the economists Richard Freeman and William Rodgers, even black males ages 16 to 24 are working more often, earning better wages and committing fewer crimes than was the case during the recession of the early 1990's.
But before concluding that a booming economy has succeeded where government job training and affirmative action programs have failed, we should recall some elementary facts of economic history. 
 We have been here before. The most recent data, for April 1999, show an unemployment rate of 17.6 percent for black men ages 20 to 24. Believe it or not, this is quite low by historical standards, though the rate for white men of the same age is only 6.2 percent.
Exactly one decade ago, in the boom year of 1989, unemployment among 20- to 24-year-old black men was 17.9 percent, about the same as today. And yet, the onset of the early 1990's recession saw the jobless rate climb steeply for young black men, reaching 24.6 percent during 1992.
There is good reason to think that a similar reversal will happen again once the current expansion ends.
Two laws of economic nature are at work here, neither of which can we safely presume has been repealed. The first holds that, in good times and bad, unemployment among young black men is two to three times as great as it is among whites. The second law is that expansions cannot last forever, and when they end, unemployment rates rise across the board.
The unfortunate reality is that race-based barriers to job access are a seemingly permanent feature of the economy. Sociologists and anthropologists studying labor markets in New York, Chicago and Los Angeles have consistently found young African-Americans to rank behind nonwhite immigrants as well as whites in the urban job queue.
One thing is certain. When Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve, and his colleagues sit down to gauge whether the risk of renewed inflation requires them to slow an overheating economy, the job prospects of poor urban youth will not weigh heavily in their calculations.
So, with the benefit of a strong economy at hand, now is the time to invest in the education and job skills of the urban poor. And with crime rates at historic lows, we should look for ways to ratchet down the rates of incarceration that have so severely affected urban black men.
By all means, let's celebrate the fruits of our economic success. And let's keep it going as long as we can. But we would do well to remember that even a fast-rising tide won't lift the sunken boats. The only way to keep them afloat is to do the hard work of rebuilding them.

HEADLINE: Rise in Chicago Manufacturing Index Slows

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: CHICAGO, May 28

 BODY:
Manufacturing in the Chicago region expanded at a slower-than-expected pace in May, held down by smaller gains in new orders and production. Prices rose at a more subdued pace in May.
The National Association of Purchasing Management-Chicago said today that its monthly index of regional manufacturing fell to 57.9 in May from 63.3. Analysts expected a reading of 60.1. Index readings above 50 mean the number of manufacturers saying business had improved was greater than the number saying it had deteriorated. 
 The index of prices paid fell to 52.5 from 56.7 in April. That halted five consecutive months of increases in this inflation-related index.
"It doesn't necessarily signal a slowdown," Brian Wesbury, chief economist at Griffin, Kubik, Stephens & Thompson Inc. of Chicago, said before the report. "Two factors led to the big jump in April: increased steel production and increased auto production, which impact the Midwest area dramatically."
The Chicago purchasers index of new orders fell to 59.6 in May from 68.7 in April.
In another report, the Government said personal income rose half a percent in April, up from a gain of three-tenths of a percent in March, and spending rose four-tenths of a percent on top of an increase of half a percent in March. That suggests consumers are not pulling back in the second quarter after the first quarter had the fastest rate of spending in 11 years.
In another report, the University of Michigan said today that its final index of consumer sentiment for May rose to 106.8 from 104.6 in April. The index measuring expectations for future economic growth also rose, to 97.6 from 97.4, suggesting consumers will continue spending.
Backing up that point, the Commerce Department said the formula it used to calculate the savings rate showed that it was a minus seven-tenths of a percent in April, the lowest on record and the same as for the two previous months.
While the Chicago purchasing managers report measures only activity in the Chicago area, investors watch it because in the past it has foreshadowed the National Association of Purchasing Management's factory index, which measures conditions nationwide.
The Chicago manufacturing report follows several recent reports that suggest if the American economy is slowing, it is not doing so by much.
Production at factories, mines and utilities rose a stronger-than-expected six-tenths of a percent in April, the biggest increase since last August, when the General Motors Corporation resumed production after a labor strike.
Rising mortgage rates did not dissuade consumers from buying homes in April, as sales of existing homes rose at a faster-than-expected annual rate of 5.24 million.
Orders for big-ticket factory goods unexpectedly dropped 2.3 percent in April, though the declines reflected falling orders for aircraft and other transportation equipment. There was a 3.5 percent gain in orders for industrial machinery and a 1.3 percent gain in primary metals.

HEADLINE: Clean Cars Get Real;
Makers of Hybrid Vehicles Add Fuel to Electric Mix

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD 

DATELINE: ALBANY, May 27

 BODY:
The annual showcase for electric cars, the Tour de Sol race, was so crowded this year that organizers were forced to turn some entrants away. But the crowded field was not so much a result of electric vehicles finally catching on as it was the new emphasis on another class of vehicles invited to join. These cars, known as hybrids, use batteries and electric motors, but also depend on gasoline or diesel engines as well.
In a change that might have horrified the purists who ran in the first race 11 years ago -- when there were only five participants and not all of them could finish the course -- the event was held in conjunction with a conference on hybrid vehicles, organized with the Society of Automotive Engineers. 
 Hybrids are now the subject of intense efforts by almost every leading auto maker. Toyota and Honda even plan to begin selling them from dealer showrooms in this country within the next 12 months.
While electric cars have so far failed to catch on, burdened by high cost and weak performance, a genuine market is about to emerge for clean, peppy hybrids that can go twice as far as a conventional car on a gallon of gasoline.
Bill Van Amburg, a spokesman for Calstart, a private nonprofit group that is trying to develop a clean-vehicle industry, contrasted the choice facing buyers interested in helping clean up the environment: The Toyota Motor Company's Prius, a hybrid already available in Japan and scheduled to go on sale in the United States next year versus the General Motors Corporation's battery-electric model, the EV-1, which is available in California.
"Toyota is selling a hybrid for $16,000 in Japan, a four-seater with an 800-mile range, or you can buy a two-seat EV-1 for $32,000, with a 60-mile range," Mr. Van Amburg said in a telephone interview. "That's an easy choice for most consumers, I think."
As a result, electric car advocates are going through something of a reality check. Nancy Hazard, organizer of the Tour de Sol, told an audience of more than 200 experts gathered here that during the first race 11 years ago, "we debated which electric technologies would be the first to receive market acceptance."
"Hybrids," she said, "were not at the top of the list. They seemed too complex and too costly."
Now, she said, people who have refined electric drivetrains, including motor controllers and charging systems, "need to embrace the hybrid at this point," because it will give them a mass market for their components. "We are on the brink of an automotive revolution," she said.
Electric cars have always been hand-built, often by hobbyists, and expensive. But Toyota is selling 2,000 of its assembly-line-produced Prius model every month in Japan, with a gasoline engine that turns the wheels and charges the batteries for an electric motor, which also turns the wheels. Moreover, when the driver wants to slow down, the motor absorbs energy from the wheels and puts it back into the battery. The car drew plenty of stares running through the streets of Albany.
So did DaimlerChrysler A.G.'s slick-looking Dodge Intrepid ESX 2, with a three-cylinder diesel engine, 133 pounds of lead-acid batteries and an electric motor. Chrysler has not committed itself to selling that car, but has other hybrids in the works, as do nearly all the big manufacturers.
Last month, the Honda Motor Company said it was giving up production of its EV Plus, a battery-powered electric, after selling only a few hundred. But from the same factory in Japan, it plans to build a two-seat, 70-mile-per-gallon hybrid for sale later this year; it hopes to sell 5,000 a year in the United States.
The New York Metropolitan Transportation Authority, meanwhile, is running six diesel-electric hybrid buses on city streets and is contemplating the purchase of hundreds more, because they seem better suited than straight diesel models for stop-and-go traffic. Plus, they are a lot cleaner; the diesel-electric bus has an engine that runs at a nearly steady state, with none of the moments familiar to every pedestrian near a traffic intersection, when straining diesel engines dump clouds of soot.
Officials of Allison Transmission, a G.M. division that makes one of the buses now in use in the city, told experts at a conference in Indianapolis recently that preliminary tests on that bus, which is equipped with a trap to capture soot, showed no measurable release of the smelly and dirty substance.
The New York agency is "very, very near" to being comfortable enough with the vehicles for a large order, Millard L. Seay, the Transit Authority vice president for buses, said. "We believe hybrids are very close to being a production-available bus," he said.
This is not unalloyed good news for the people who have championed electric vehicles. Among their customers, the small group of affluent environmentalists and "early adopters" who like to have the newest gizmo first, hybrids loom large. Consequently, they are holding off buying electric cars.
"Boy, has it been frustrating," said James D. Worden, president of the Solectria Corporation, a Wilmington, Mass., company that for several years bought Geo Metros, junked their engines, and converted them to electricity. Now the company has a deal with G.M. to buy the cars without engines at substantial cost savings, but the potential buyers, he said, are sitting on their hands waiting for the Toyota and Honda hybrids.
Mr. Worden said Solectria now did as much business selling electric drive-trains for hybrid buses, trucks and cars as selling pure electric cars.
Mr. Worden is a perennial winner of the Tour de Sol, but this year, for the first time, he sent others to drive his company's entries. Instead, he attended the conference on hybrids.
Still, engineers are far from a consensus about the best hybrid design. DaimlerChrysler's have big conventional engines with small electric motors that help on acceleration, giving a modest increase in fuel economy. Their plus is acceleration that resembles that of a sports car. Another design has the gasoline engine run the back wheels and an electric motor turn the front wheels, giving four-wheel-drive as a bonus.
The more experimental models in the race are far more fuel efficient, using big electric motors and small gasoline engines to gradually recharge a battery pack. One entrant, the New Jersey Venturer, used a combination of a hydrogen fuel cell and batteries instead of an engine and batteries. And while the major auto makers are leaning toward diesel, environmentalists -- who comprise the backbone of the racers -- say diesel will never be clean enough.
Cleanliness is a big motivator. California dropped its 1998 requirement that 2 percent of new cars sold in the state should produce zero emissions. But it still has a 10 percent quota on the books for 2003. The California Air Resources Board's chief deputy executive officer, Thomas A. Cackette, told conference attendees that the state would give partial credit toward the electric car quota for hybrids.
But the benefits of hybrids to the environment could be small, warned James A. McCargar, a senior policy adviser at the United States Environmental Protection Agency.
The auto industry, he said, is "caught up in a size-and-performance arms race."
"Will hybrid technology prompt manufacturers to downsize engines and translate that into fuel-efficiency gains," Mr. McCargar said, "or will they use the hybrid technology to get even more performance than we've had in the past?"
The range of options was clear during the race, which is run in stages by managers with clipboards and stopwatches. Organized by the Northeast Sustainable Energy Association, of Greenfield, Mass., with a variety of corporate and government sponsors, race results are available at www.nesea.org.
This year, it was run along a 250-mile route on streets, highways and country roads from Waterbury, Conn., to Lake George, N.Y. Everything from single-seat solar vehicles to two New York City buses, 46 vehicles in all, participated. They competed on many factors, including performance and energy use. Some zoomed, some cruised, but organizers insisted on no speeding beyond the limit.
During the race, high school and college teams rubbed shoulders with corporate entrants. Some vehicles were home built and others were extensively modified production models. All were covered with decals advertising component manufacturers and other sponsors.
Race teams carried conventional car tools but the device of choice for monitoring their vehicles and making last-minute tweaks was the laptop computer, jacked into a port on the dashboard or under the hood.
In the next few years, it could become a more frequent sight in the garages of America.

HEADLINE: Hoop Dreams, Not War Games;
From Montenegro to the N.B.A., Radojevic Has High Hopes

BYLINE: By JUDY BATTISTA 

 BODY:
There is just one more month left in Aleksandar Radojevic's hurried odyssey to the National Basketball Association -- a month filled with tryouts in one city after another, with four-hour training sessions and with thousands of calories in protein shakes. And the pace of the final push is wearing on the gangly 22-year-old, who did not start playing basketball in his native Montenegro until he was 16, and who did not take the sport seriously until he sprouted to nearly 7 feet by 19.
Now, when the 7-foot-3-inch Radojevic lopes into a room, shoulders hunched, he looks weary. This week alone, Radojevic, with just two years of junior college experience to his credit, has worked out in front of officials of the Knicks and Nets, and there are at least seven more auditions to go, including one this weekend for the Chicago Bulls. 
 But the effort, Radojevic said, is more than worth it, for there is much within his grasp and much to at last put behind him. Radojevic's skills are rated so highly that he is expected to be selected in the first round of the N.B.A. draft June 30, and he may well become the highest-drafted Yugoslav player. And with the riches that will come, Radojevic will be able to pluck his family from a part of the world that is once again convulsed by war.
"His skill level is off the charts," said Leo Papile, the director of scouting for the Boston Celtics. Papile, figuring it to be a thin year for big men, said Radojevic, who weighs 244 pounds, could be one of the first two centers taken. "He has a high basketball I.Q. He can move all the way up to the top half-dozen. He is no secret."
Still, his availability in the draft is a surprise, to Radojevic as much as anyone. As one of the nation's top junior college prospects after averaging 15.4 points and blocking 153 shots last season at Barton County Community College in Kansas, Radojevic had signed a letter of intent to play for Ohio State next season. But on May 11, the National Collegiate Athletic Association ruled that because Radojevic received $13,000 for playing a total of 19 minutes for a Yugoslav club team two years ago, he was ineligible to play at Ohio State.
While speculation had swirled even before the ruling that Radojevic's draft status was climbing so high he would forgo the rest of his college eligibility, Radojevic says he was disappointed by the decision.
"If I was eligible for Ohio State, I wouldn't have entered the draft," Radojevic said. "In your life, you've got your goal. You don't jump from one step to the last step."
Radojevic has taken many steps very quickly over the last several years. He did not give up his first passion, water polo, until he was 16 and stood 6-5. And while the Balkans have long produced outstanding basketball players -- Vlade Divac, Drazen Petrovic and Toni Kukoc -- Radojevic didn't take his prospects seriously until he grew another five inches by the time he was 19.
By then, Semi Pajovic, a family friend and a former Yugoslav national team player who helps other Yugoslavs find places to play in the United States, had sized up Radojevic's potential. For someone who had begun playing the game so late, Radojevic displayed natural gifts.
"He was one tall, big kid," said Pajovic, who lives on Long Island and is the host for Radojevic while he prepares for the draft. "He's unbelievably agile."
And as he advanced, coming to play for a club team, Radojevic managed to avoid being drafted into the military. Montenegro is a republic of Yugoslavia, and Pajovic suspects a sympathetic basketball coach helped Radojevic stay out of the Yugoslav Army until he could go to the United States. The decision to leave, in the end, was simple.
After nearly a decade of war in the Balkans, Montenegro's economy is a shambles. Radojevic's late father was a security guard, and his mother worked in the fields. Together, they earned about $2,400 a year.
Radojevic, who was 20 when he left Montenegro, spoke no English, making junior college his only option. Barton County, one of the nation's premier junior college teams, already had a Yugoslav player who had transferred from Connecticut. That player, Predrag Materic, introduced Steve Forbes, then Barton's coach, to Pajovic. Pajovic told Forbes he wanted to bring a 7-3 player over. Forbes offered Radojevic a scholarship without even having met him.
"He comes walking into this airport in Kansas and says, 'Hello, how are you?' and that's all he could say," Forbes recalled. "We drive to Burger King, and he just pointed to chicken sandwiches and orange juice. You could talk to him and he could understand it, but there was no way he could talk to you.
"He was just a tall, tall skinny kid with a great demeanor. We're having a barbecue and he's out in the backyard chasing my son, who's probably 2, playing cowboys and Indians. I have a picture of him holding my son straight up in the air, and my son is dunking on a 10-foot hoop and Alek is standing flat-footed."
The former St. John's coach Fran Fraschilla was one of the first coaches to see Radojevic in the United States when the center briefly stayed with Pajovic before leaving for Kansas. Now, Fraschilla predicts he will be taken around 15th but won't make an immediate impact because of his slight build.
"I was amazed that a kid that big and that young had such a good skill level," Fraschilla said.
Because of the war in the Balkans, Radojevic has not gone home since coming to the United States, and he stayed in Kansas even when his father died last season. When the N.C.A.A. ruled him ineligible, the draft was the only viable option because a return home would have surely meant being conscripted into the army and perhaps being sent to fight in Kosovo.
"I would probably be the first on the front to be shot," he said, alluding to his height. "Going there would be jeopardizing everything I built here. I'm focusing on something I'm going to be doing for the next 15 years."
The war has left his small hometown on the Adriatic, Herceg Novi, intact, but Radojevic, who learned his now-flawless English from classes and television, worries that the scene of the conflict could shift at any moment. He tries his best to block out reports of bombings, and their attendant misery. As long as his mother keeps assuring him that his family is fine, he said, he will continue to focus on basketball. But as soon as he can after the draft, he will move his mother, his twin brother and sister here.
Where Radojevic will be drafted is still a question. Jeff Bower, the Charlotte Hornets' scouting director, is less certain than Papile of the Celtics. He said Radojevic is still something of an unknown because he has faced only junior college competition, which infrequently features the play of anyone over 6-8.
Radojevic, for his part, does not like to discuss his own talent, saying he will let the scouts decide. There are seven tryouts and a pre-draft camp in Chicago before this last, hectic chapter in his journey ends.
"I never thought it would be this close," he said of his shot at the N.B.A. "My friends told me, 'You're tall, you have to bend over to take your money.' "

HEADLINE: Abroad at Home;
Which Side Are We On?

BYLINE: By ANTHONY LEWIS 

 BODY:
The indictment of Slobodan Milosevic by the International War Crimes Tribunal told us nothing new about his character or his record. He has been carrying out crimes against humanity since 1991, when his forces leveled the Croatian city of Vukovar and executed 260 men in a Vukovar hospital.
But the indictment does make a difference, a profound one. It clarifies the nature of NATO's war against Mr. Milosevic over Kosovo. And it requires those who have been critical of that war to think again about which side they are on. 
 Just as Mr. Milosevic is the first serving head of state to be charged with war crimes, so this war is a first. The reason was eloquently stated by President Vaclav Havel of the Czech Republic last month in a speech to the Canadian Parliament, reprinted in The New York Review of Books.
"This is probably the first war that has not been waged in the name of 'national interests,' " President Havel said, "but rather in the name of principles and values. . . .
"Kosovo has no oilfields to be coveted; no member nation in the alliance NATO has any territorial demands on Kosovo. . . . It is fighting out of concern for the fate of others. It is fighting because no decent person can stand by and watch the systematic state-directed murder of other people."
NATO air attacks have killed Serbian civilians. That is regrettable. But it is a price that has to be paid when a nation falls in behind a criminal leader. It happened in Germany. And it has happened again in Serbia.
How can a young man, a Serbian paramilitary, demand money from an Albanian family for a child's life -- and, when they have none to give, knife the child? That, and other savageries, could take place only because an evil leader had infected his people with the notion that others are less human -- are untermenschen.
Even Serbs distant from the atrocities have been affected. Reports from Belgrade say most people see themselves as victims. They are oblivious to what Serbian forces have done to other human beings over the last eight years -- oblivious to Vukovar, to the rape camps and ethnic cleansing in Bosnia, to the massacres in Kosovo.
In 1992 the Serbian commander in Bosnia, Ratko Mladic, told his gunners in the hills around Sarajevo, "Burn it all." And they did: hospitals, universities, mosques, homes. That should be remembered when Serbs today describe themselves as victims.
That NATO's purpose is just does not, of course, mean that it has fought the war wisely. It has not. Serious military analysts are agreed that the alliance should not have forsworn the use of ground troops at the start of its campaign, and should not have begun the air war so tepidly.
Nor was the war inevitable. American diplomacy went in fits and starts before the final crisis, seeming to lose its way in dealing with Mr. Milosevic's broken promises on Kosovo. In the critical months beginning in January 1998 President Clinton was persistently distracted by a special prosecutor obsessed by sex. And Mr. Milosevic well knew that.
But we are in the war now, and for the most urgent political as well as moral reasons we must win. Even achieving NATO's declared objectives in Kosovo will not necessarily end the menace of Slobodan Milosevic. Unless he is captured, he will no doubt make more trouble in Montenegro and elsewhere. But victory, limited though it be, is essential.
The indictment of Mr. Milosevic may change the politics of the war in this country. Many Republicans have tried to use the war politically. That was the strategy of Representative Tom DeLay, the G.O.P. whip, when a motion to support the war came before the House. He urged Republicans to vote no, making it Bill Clinton's war -- an astonishing position for a party that usually touts its patriotism. The motion lost on a tie vote.
The President should go to the country now, on the strength of the Milosevic indictment, to tell people what this war is about. He needs stronger public support as a moment of truth approaches.
A summit meeting of the seven leading economic powers and Russia will be held in Cologne on June 18. If the air war has not brought Mr. Milosevic to accept NATO's demands by then, the prospect of winter means that the unthinkable will have to be confronted: a ground war.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Consumer Spending in April Adds 0.4% to March Rise
Consumer spending on goods and services rose four-tenths of a percent in April on top of an increase of half a percent in March, the Commerce Department reported. And, personal income rose half a percent in April, up from a gain of three-tenths of a percent in March. 
 In another report, the National Association of Purchasing Management said its index of regional manufacturing fell to 57.9 in May from 63.3. Analysts expected a reading of 60.1. Index readings above 50 mean the number of manufacturers saying business had improved was greater than the number saying it had deteriorated. Page C15.
 
NEC Lost $1.3 Billion in Fiscal Year
NEC, one of the world's largest makers of semiconductors, lost 157.9 billion yen, or $1.3 billion at current exchange rates, in the fiscal year that ended March 31, compared with a profit of 41.3 billion yen the year before. C2.
 
Hackers Disrupt 2 U.S. Web Sites
Hackers disrupted the Internet sites of the Federal Bureau of Investigation and the United States Senate this week, forcing them to shut down. The sites serve a public relations function, so no classified, confidential or personal material was compromised. A1.
 
KPMG Accused in Medicare Lawsuit
The accounting firm KPMG was accused in a Federal whistle-blower lawsuit unsealed yesterday of aiding some hospitals in Florida in a scheme to defraud Medicare through the use of bogus expense claims. C5.
 
A Pact With Sweepstakes Promoter
American Family Publishers, one of the two largest sweepstakes promotion companies, agreed to changes in its practices. It also agreed to a $4 million settlement with four states that accused it of deception. A9.
 
Italian Banks Find Mergers Difficult
Italian banks, like those across Europe, are struggling to merge, to cut costs and create the larger capital bases they will need to finance customers on the euro market. Yet recent efforts have failed. Earlier this month, Banca Commerciale Italiana, the No. 4 bank in Italy, rejected a $14 billion takeover offer from Unicredito Italiano, the No. 3 bank, after having concluded that the nation's central bank would block the bid. It did so because the central bank's president, Antonio Fazio, said he would not approve hostile bids for banks. C2.
 
A Microsoft Defender Is Questioned
Gordon Eubanks, a longtime software developer, was questioned by the Justice Department's chief trial lawyer in the Government's antitrust suit against Microsoft about whether he had benefited as a result of his decision to defend the software publisher and to testify in its behalf. The Microsoft trial is expected to resume Tuesday in Washington. C5.

Airlines Agree on Accident Liability
The world's major airlines have agreed to provide better protection and compensation to victims of international air accidents. B2.

ARM Financial Hires an Adviser
ARM Financial Services Group, a provider of insurance-based retirement saving plans whose stock has languished, hired Morgan Stanley Dean Witter Inc. to "evaluate strategic alternatives." C4.

Concern Raised About Falling Euro
Germany's top central banker expressed alarm at the depressed value of the euro against the dollar, hinting at a shift in the hands-off policy toward exchange rates. C2.
 
Markets Closed Memorial Day
United States financial markets, government offices and banks will be closed Monday in observance of Memorial Day. Financial markets will be open in other countries.

HEADLINE: Failed Business Zone

 BODY:

To the Editor:
Re "Thin Support and Red Tape Mar New York City Development Zone" (front page, May 24): I am president of a small South Bronx apparel manufacturing company that grew out of a defunct contract shop, developed a designer line and now sells to 700 retailers. 
 Having mortgaged ourselves to the hilt and maxed out our credit cards, my associates and I needed financial assistance to take advantage of our fast growth, which had in turn created badly needed jobs. We had numerous promising-sounding meetings with South Bronx development people, Bronx Borough President people, city economic development people, industrial assistance people, New York State people and people from banks supposedly ready to assist development zone companies.
In the end it boiled down to the usual bank credit criteria: if you have enough money not to need us, we will gladly lend to you, but if you don't, forget it. The entire process was a waste of time.
 
GEORGE WOLF
Bronx, May 24, 1999
 
http://www.nytimes.com

COUNTRY: UNITED STATES (95%);

And Bear in Mind
(Editors' choices of other recent books of particular interest)
 
ANOTHER LIFE: A Memoir of Other People, by Michael Korda. (Random House, $26.95.) A book editor recounts his life in publishing and how what he thought would be a gentlemanly pursuit of erudition was in reality the entertainment business.
 
ANOTHER WORLD, by Pat Barker. (Farrar, Straus & Giroux, $24.) Old hatreds, wounds and guilt, simmering from World War I and beyond, haunt a family in a novel set in modern Newcastle.
 
CLOSE RANGE: Wyoming Stories, by Annie Proulx. (Scribner, $25.) A collection that depicts the New West within the framework of the Wild West, and discovers life hasn't changed all that much for the cowboys and cowgirls who inhabit it.
 
A DANGEROUS FRIEND, by Ward Just. (Peter Davison/Houghton Mifflin, $23.) As its well-meaning civilian hero attempts to find an American officer captured in Vietnam, this novel emerges as an allegory for our country's place in the increasingly deranged world of the mid-1960's.
 
FREDY NEPTUNE, by Les Murray. (Farrar, Straus & Giroux, $27.50.) This verse novel follows an itinerant Everyman from Australia afflicted with an unusual power as he travels through the horrors of the 20th century.
 
FREEDOM FROM FEAR: The American People in Depression and War, 1929-1945, by David M. Kennedy. (Oxford University, $39.95.) A history of the Roosevelt era, from the New Deal to V-E Day, and the America he taught to believe that "the only thing we have to fear is fear itself."
 
LOVE IN A DEAD LANGUAGE: A Romance, by Lee Siegel. (University of Chicago, $25.) An ambitious and imaginative novel masquerading as a commentary on the Kama Sutra that becomes a literary puzzle in which Nabokov, Joyce and Philip Roth provide the clues.
 
THE PITY OF WAR, by Niall Ferguson. (Basic Books, $30.) The traditional views on the causes of World War I are called into question, and the author suggests that had England stayed at home, the world might be a different place today.
 
TURN OF THE CENTURY, by Kurt Andersen. (Random House, $24.95.) A novel about a "yoga-practicing Jew" and a "lapsed Unitarian" who are married and competing in the silicon rat race of the new millennium, where life is only slightly more crazy than the old one.
 
WOMAN: An Intimate Geography, by Natalie Angier. (Houghton Mifflin, $25.) A personal celebration of the female of the species -- the glory, the rapture, the mystery of being a woman -- written from the point of view of a member of that sex but which may prove instructive for the other one as well.

SECTION: Section 7; Page 2; Column 4; Book Review Desk 

LENGTH: 164 words

HEADLINE: 'False Dawn'

 BODY:
To the Editor:
In his criticism of John Gray's "False Dawn" (April 25), Fareed Zakaria dazzles us with handpicked statistics while ignoring the bigger picture. Missing are the glaring reminders of economic imperialism. 
 Yes, we've controlled infectious diseases with modern medicine, but we're replacing them with chronic degenerative diseases of polluted air, water and soil. Subsistence farmers are not commuting to filthy, overcrowded, crime-ridden cities for the good life, but they are trying to survive the intrusion of modern agribusiness and its pressure on global prices for crops.
In the long run, the most insidious damage to poorer countries may come from stealing their human resources. Congress recently passed a rider making the issuance of permanent visas for high-tech workers more expedient. We should be training high-tech people in other countries. Instead, the "free global market" is taking away their best minds.
 
Fred F. Caruso
Burlington, Vt. 
http://www.nytimes.com

HEADLINE: The Dominican National Pastime

BYLINE: By Nicholas Dawidoff; Nicholas Dawidoff is the author of "The Catcher Was a Spy: The Mysterious Life of Moe Berg" and "In the Country of Country: A Journey to the Roots of American Music."

 BODY:

Away Games
 The Life and Times
 of a Latin Baseball Player.
 By Marcos Breton
 and Jose Luis Villegas.
 Illustrated. 272 pp. New York:
 Simon & Schuster. $23. 
 ON rutted baseball diamonds in the shantytowns of the Dominican Republic, burros graze in the outfield and lithe little boys fight to stand in the stretches of dust between second and third base. That is the shortstop's territory. Over the past 20 years, an unusual number of Dominicans have eluded the sugar cane fields, the textile mills and the enervating poverty of their small Caribbean island by coming north to the United States to play shortstop in the major leagues. Yet for every Alfredo Griffin or Julio Franco or Tony Fernandez who makes it to las grandes ligas, there are tens of thousands who will fail, who will end up like men Marcos Breton and Jose Luis Villegas describe in their book "Away Games": "half-drunk, unemployed," bragging about "how good they once were."
"Away Games" is the story of one of the nominally lucky ones. Miguel Tejada grew up in Los Barrancones, a barrio outside the city of Bani. By the time he was 13, his mother had died and he had long since quit school to shine shoes. At day's end he came home to a shack where the toilet was a hole in the ground, the wall to his room was a soiled curtain and his bed was a mattress with such a large hole in the center that he was completely obscured while he slept. But Tejada had natural riches. His legs were sturdy, his wiry body could propel a baseball bat with stunning aggression, and his will was strong, too. Soon baseball men began to notice him.
From this point, "Away Games" becomes the breathless story of Tejada's rise from life as a wild kid from a defeated place to a poised, acrobatic athlete. There are a series of wonderful, almost filmic scenes, including the practice sessions he attends run by a local sandlot youth coach, Enrique Soto, who imposes discipline upon his young charges by shrugging when they misbehave and saying: "To hell with you. I'll see you out begging on the street." Tejada attracts the attention of the Oakland Athletics and is sent to their baseball development complex in the Dominican jungle.
Eventually he shows enough promise to receive a minor league contract. Breton and Villegas consider Tejada to be a person of rare character, and we hope for him as he learns English (and how to use a toilet), suffers injuries, avoids seductive women, adjusts to minor league towns like Medford, Ore., and Modesto, Calif., and meets up with young American ballplayers, including Ben Grieve, an outfielder who was the Athletics' top prospect. At the end of a season, Grieve and Tejada are talking about how it will be to spend the winter as incipient baseball stars. "You know, when I go home, I'm going to have all these people asking me for balls or autographs," Grieve complains.
"Yeah," Tejada replies, "but Ben, when I go home, I'm going to have all these people saying, 'Miguel, can I have some money? Miguel, can I have some shoes? Miguel, can I have some food?' "
As long as "Away Games" concentrates on Tejada, it is truly engrossing. But Breton, a senior writer at The Sacramento Bee, and Villegas, a photographer at the same newspaper, have broader ambitions. They also want to use Tejada's story as a means of reflecting upon the more general plight of Latin ballplayers in the United States. It's a worthy subject, an important and, in many ways, bitter story. Generations of Latin ballplayers have left behind unspeakable poverty and arrived in this country only to be subjected to racism and exploitation. To this day, teams like the Athletics save money by filling out their minor league rosters with young Dominicans who can be had for a meager wage that a young American would never accept. (Tejada signed for $2,000. The average American signing bonus is $50,000.) It's a throw-back-the-little-ones approach; within a year or two, most of these players have either been shipped back to the Dominican or, if they manage to escape their handlers, are roaming the streets of cities like New York as illegal immigrants.
Unfortunately, Breton and Villegas make very little effort to weave this broader story into Tejada's particular narrative, with the result that the book sputters and drags every time the protagonist leaves the scene. And, alas, he sometimes vanishes for entire chapters or more. Breton and Villegas's prose is potholed with cliches ("pint-sized," "blank slate," "broke the mold"), with peculiar images -- what is a "blistering rain"? -- and with wooden sentences. These can be ignored when we're absorbed in all the fascinating things they have to tell us about Tejada. On a tangent, they are less tolerable.
It's a familiar story in many ways, the intersection of poverty, athletics and capitalism. "Away Games," like its subject, Miguel Tejada, is very raw. Still, it's not exactly a missed opportunity. For, like Tejada as well, it's also powerful and, at times, it really moves.
 
http://www.nytimes.com
