HEADLINE: MUSIC;
Once Rivals, Two Jazz Impresarios Agree to Disagree

BYLINE: By BEN RATLIFF 

 BODY:
GEORGE WEIN and Michael Dorf -- impresarios of this year's JVC Jazz Festival and the Bell Atlantic Jazz Festival respectively -- have been butting heads for 11 years. In 1954 Mr. Wein started the Newport Jazz Festival, which became the paradigm for outdoor music events. After moving through several different sponsors, it became the JVC festival in 1984. Mr. Dorf put on his first jazz festival (called "What Is Jazz?") at the Knitting Factory, his club in lower Manhattan, in 1988, scheduling it to compete with the JVC concerts uptown. A cordial relationship developed, but it broke down in 1997, when Mr. Dorf expanded, found a major sponsor (Texaco), and called his giant festival the New York Jazz Festival. This was the student rather stridently pulling rank on the teacher, and a period of bad blood followed.
Jazz is too small for a brand war like this, and detente was probably inevitable. Friendly again, they're both committed to making New York a city overrun with jazz each June, resolving that too much at once is the best way to draw fans to the concerts. This year, Bell Atlantic Jazz (the festival has a new sponsor) runs from June 3 to 13 and JVC Jazz from June 14 to 27. 
 As the head of Festival Productions, Mr. Wein, 73, produced 37 music festivals around the world in 1998, including the New Orleans Jazz and Heritage Festival; he almost sold the company to Black Entertainment Television last year, but the deal did not go through. As the C.E.O. of Knit Media, Mr. Dorf, 37, runs a club and a record label, and plans to open new branches of the Knitting Factory in Los Angeles and Berlin. In a joint interview, at The New York Times, the first they had ever done, they discussed a range of issues, including their different theories on how to reach the current jazz audience. Following are excerpts from their conversation.
 BEN RATLIFF
BEN RATLIFF. George, you've been a concert producer for nearly 50 years. Is there anything you could possibly have learned from the ascension of Michael Dorf?
GEORGE WEIN. There are things happening every day and every year that I cannot keep up with it for many reasons. I can't understand the new technology and things like that, and I watch what he's done with the Internet. I've given instructions now to my people about getting sponsors for a festival, tell them we will get the artist to sign for an appearance on the Internet as part of the deal. And give them a national image. I mean, that's something I never did before.
MICHAEL DORF. Jazz is perfectly suited for the Internet because it is a little more intelligent. It's not a mass-pop type of music. The Internet allows us to find those niches, and when you can aggregate all those audiences together, 75 people in Memphis and 32 of them in Stuttgart and 100 in Singapore, now you start building up a really large audience that can come to jazz.
WEIN. But I would never put a whole concert on the Internet. Because, conceivably, it would get economically unfeasible with programs that I do. If I ask Herbie Hancock to give me an Internet program, he's going to want to be paid double, but we can't afford that unless you got a sponsor paying for the whole thing. The basic difference between what Michael and I do is economics. He has a place where he can put people in; he uses -- and I don't mean this as derogatory -- non-union musicians, young musicians. Every time I present something I have to rent a hall. That affects the total economic structure, and when I'm using Carnegie and Avery Fisher, I have no freedom. I have to charge $40, $50, $60 for tickets because the costs of hall and advertising is $50,000 before you pay your artist. But last year was one of the best years we've had. This year we're way ahead of last year. Ticket sales took off.
Q. How do you account for that?
WEIN. I think there's a lot of money around. People are making money in the market. We broke all records at New Orleans this year. There's an economic spirit in the air with the middle upper class. They're spending money and going out. Restaurants are packed. Everybody's doing more business.
Q. In theory, your festivals reflect different attitudes about what jazz is. People in general think of JVC as the mainstream jazz festival and Bell Atlantic as the alternative. Is that still a fair assumption?
DORF. We certainly have had a much more open definition of what jazz is. Most of that is about trying to expand the audience. When a band like Medeski, Martin and Wood or any of that "Grateful Jazz" genre is able to turn people on to Ornette Coleman or Sonny Rollins -- because they cover a particular song or they invite those older musicians on stage the way the Grateful Dead used to have Ornette or David Murray come on to the stage -- to me that's accomplishing a really great objective, which is turning a lot of young people on to the music.
WEIN. See, newness is something I don't think you can create. New things happen in spite of everything. The minute you say I have to be different, you have a problem. You are different if you are a great artist.
Q. And yet, Michael, your only concert at Town Hall this year is Grover Washington Jr., who is more mainstream than mainstream. How do you square that with any sort of alternative profile?
DORF. Opening up for Grover is Bill Ware, a great downtown vibraphone player who never will have an opportunity, unless we provided this one, to play in front of 1,000 or 1,400 more conservative jazz fans. So, getting the Grover fans turned on to some of the stuff that we're doing downtown fits our objective. We want to have both ends of the spectrum. All this stuff will go into a jazz bin at Tower Records.
WEIN. I went down to see John Zorn and Dave Douglas, and they said, "Well, what do you think -- isn't it something different?" I said, "It's nothing different, it's music." I said, "What is different about music?" I wanted to play them this year, uptown.
Q. George, you've said that The New York Times made Michael Dorf -- and that the paper made him at your expense. But a lot of musicians feel that they made Michael Dorf, or at least that they made what's widely regarded as a "Knitting Factory esthetic." In 1997, a gathering of musicians, under the banner of the Noise Action Coalition, voiced their concern that they weren't being paid enough in the festival. So, Michael, you negotiated with the N.A.C., and last year the fee went up to $100 per musician for a group of up to six players; this year it's $200 per musician. This is a great victory for musicians; does it make it harder to put on a festival?
DORF. Yes, it does. It makes it harder to put on as much music as we used to. We have a budget, we have a realistic pie to work from, and you can only cut the pie so many ways.
WEIN. I can't accept that. Because I'm a musician, and I know what it is to go out and earn a living playing music. I don't set a price for the musicians when I pay them. I negotiate with each musician on each individual deal. If I can't pay him and he wants ridiculous money, I don't pay him.
DORF. We had a difficult situation. The Knitting Factory has a very symbiotic relationship with the music community. They all use it as a place to improve their profile. And we always had a deal in which the artist made 75 percent of the money that came in the door. It was a relationship that worked very well and continues to work well at the club. If 10 people come and pay 10 bucks, it's $100 at the door, they get $75. And they understand I shouldn't be going into my pocket to pay them $1,000.
WEIN. Michael, it was the best thing that could happen.
Q. That the musicians complained?
WEIN. Yeah, it was the best thing. It brought you into a reality of a situation that you're going to have to live with the rest of your life. Because that's the reality: musicians have to make a living, have to get paid.
DORF. I don't think we're going to have the financial complaints from the musicians anymore. I think that was a hiccup in our growth. In terms of having this community problem, I don't think we'll see that. It is a partnership, and if there's not pure open communication, whether it's a one-on-one relationship with your wife or significant other or in this case a partnership with the whole community --
WEIN. Michael, you are not a partner with the musicians. You are the enemy. You are the exploiter. As you grow, that is the image you will have with musicians. You are the Man. And the Man can never be a partner.
Q. You both have extremely difficult tasks: trying to sell tickets to New York jazz audiences, who have seen everything and regularly get a chance to see most jazz groups. Even though this is the jazz capital of the world, is it harder to put on a successful jazz festival in New York than in Montreal and Umbria, to name two particularly good ones?
WEIN. You know our problem? Not that the New York audience has seen everything. You've seen everything. How many times can you review Sonny Rollins? But the only time people won't come to see Sonny Rollins is when they read that he's not doing anything new.
DORF. I see it as a challenge for us. To put on something that the critics haven't seen before.
Q. Leaving the question of innovation aside, because it's so hard to define, should jazz festivals offer a reflection of the current state of jazz?
WEIN. I think they should offer a reflection of jazz, which includes the current, which includes the past. Why should my people, who are 50, 60, 70 years old, be denied hearing Benny Goodman's music and things like that because they don't reflect the current state of jazz?
Q. Every year you're competing with each other to book artists.
WEIN. The only competition Michael gives me is in the press. I've no feeling of competition with him for my concert stuff.
DORF. But there's overlap on some programming.
Q. Cassandra Wilson or Medeski, Martin and Wood, these are acts that you could both play successfully.
WEIN. I think we will get closer as we go along. For instance, last year, I didn't want my artists appearing on his award program the Jazz Awards, produced by Knit Media . This year, I don't care.
Q. As I remember, part of the problem was that you were going to receive one of the awards, and you didn't want to receive an award from Michael.
WEIN. I didn't think Michael was in the position to give me an award. I should be giving Michael an award. I still don't think Knit Media's sponsorship of the Jazz Awards is right. You know, I'll go along with it because you're doing it. But I think it should be done by an institution of some sort, the Smithsonian or something.
DORF. And I wouldn't disagree with that. The only reason we decided to do it was because no one else was doing it.
Q. Michael, the message I've been hearing from you is that unless jazz can combine with other forms of music, it's not really going to find an audience.
DORF. No, I think you need to bring new people into the room to listen to it. We have to somehow figure out what that 13-year-old or 12-year-old is listening to and how can they make a connection between Chuck D and Ornette Coleman or Don Byron or what Steve Coleman or Greg Osby's been doing with hip-hop.
WEIN. I agree with Michael in this respect. I have always believed that you must put on more than just pure jazz. And the only way jazz will ever die is if the musicians stop playing jazz. Jazz can never be more than a minority music. Everybody cannot like the highest quality things. But if we cultivate that minority -- we're a country of 250 million people. If you have 2 percent of them that like jazz, you've got 5 million jazz fans. You've got to cultivate those 5 million jazz fans. You can't look for the 98 percent. Forget them, they're going to go out for the Rolling Stones or the Beatles, or whatever it is. But we need a Miles Davis. We need a Dave Brubeck. We need somebody that sells records.
Q. Is there anybody on the horizon that could do that?
WEIN. That I don't know. I tell all groups, all musicians: Stop making a different record every time you play. Get a group and work on a sound. It's all about a sound that people remember, not how great you blow. And that's the way you must establish identity. The Modern Jazz Quartet stayed selling out concerts, right until the time they quit. That group sound is what sells jazz; the greatness of the music has to be within the structure of a group.
DORF. There were 35,000 new CD's released in 1998, and that was not the case four years ago, so there's limited shelf space in retail. There's limited air space to play these on the radio. Why I'm pushing on-line is that you now have virtual shelf space. You have virtual dials, and you can program your jazz to your computer and listen to whatever you want. And if you look at the sales on Amazon.com or CD Now, you'll see jazz is now represented by about 10 percent of sales. So when you really can democratize the ability of people to listen to the music that they want to, jazz is going to have a much higher market share than it currently does.
WEIN. He's absolutely right. I don't know what he's talking about, but he's right. If I knew what he was talking about, he might be wrong.
Q. Michael, George has criticized certain aspects of your festival.
WEIN. I haven't criticized; I've commented on it.
Q. Is there anything about George's festival that you would do differently?
DORF. The only suggestion I've made in the past was to have more unknown opening acts on the stage.
WEIN. It keeps people away.
DORF. It's hard, it's a lot easier to say it than it is to actually implement it. And I see that in my own event. But I really force that. I try and have one unknown name on every big bill, and give that artist an opportunity for exposure.
WEIN. There is no question that he would take a lot of things I do and keep them and take a lot of things I do and change them. Because he is what he is. And that's why he and I have built up an affinity. Because I recognize that this kid doesn't stop. Look, I think Michael and I are going to be doing a lot of things together in the future if I last long enough. We understand each other.

HEADLINE: POSTINGS: $62 Million for 57th Street Structure and Lease on Land;
Steinway Buys Its Building

 BODY:
Since 1925, Steinway & Sons has sold pianos and played host to great pianists in its elegant store at Steinway Hall, at 111 West 57th Street, between the Avenue of the Americas and Seventh Avenue.
But for 40 years, as a result of a 1958 decision to sell the land and the building, the company had been only a tenant in the building it put up. 
 Now, however, Steinway & Sons has reacquired the structure, paying $62 million to a group of investors including Wexford Management and Bear Stearns for the 217,000-square-foot building and a 99-year lease on the land under it.
"Steinway Hall has set the tone for the company's revenue growth by producing record-breaking sales results for the last several years," said Bruce A. Stevens, the president of Steinway & Sons. "We can now enter the new millennium with this valuable asset safely where it belongs -- at Steinway."
The building is also home to the American operations of The Economist magazine and the architectural firm of Kohn Pedersen Fox Associates.
Francis A. Coco, a director of Cushman & Wakefield, the real estate services company that advised Steinway, said that the purchase, which was completed earlier this month, had been designed to assist the company financially.
"The time was right and the market was right for Steinway to regain ownership of this renowned building," Mr. Coco said. "We are extremely pleased with the results, as we were able to structure the transaction to increase Steinway's cash flow and produce an attractive rate of return on their investment."
Dennis Hanson, the chief financial officer of Steinway, said the decision to lease rather than purchase the land under the building reduced the purchase price and eased the financial impact on the company.

SECTION: Section 14CN; Page 1; Column 1; Connecticut Weekly Desk

HEADLINE: Help Wanted: Low Salary, No Benefits, Little Respect

BYLINE: By JULIE MILLER 

 BODY:
THE labor shortage created by the strong economy has done more than send Connecticut companies searching for skilled workers. It has also created a labor squeeze in classrooms, forcing schools around the state to scramble for substitute teachers, who can often make more money doing just about anything else.
In Norwich, the school system typically had to hire about 15 substitutes a day, but still fell one or two short. So principals or other school staff members took over classes. In Waterford, the schools have needed substitutes 2,725 times this year, but were able to fill only 2,484 positions. New Haven schools needed from 100 to 150 substitutes a day, but often fell 25 to 50 short. The situation has gotten so bad that schools have had to become creative. 
 Some districts are recruiting parents and recalling retired teachers. New Haven got about 20 graduate students to sign on as substitutes by agreeing to pay some of their college tuition. The city has even called on firefighters and police officers to teach during their off-duty hours.
"We're trying every technique, method and strategy we can think of," said Starlet Wilder, director of personnel and labor relations for the New Haven schools.
Kyle Daniels, 24, who finished almost four years of college, is a New Haven firefighter and substitutes at one of the city's schools. The job pays him $50 per day, and although he would like to find a second job that pays more, he enjoys the students and said teaching fits well with his rotating shifts as a firefighter.
"I enjoy what I'm doing, although I wish I could get more money," he said.
The low pay forced Terrence Rountree, 25, of New Haven, to stop substitute teaching for the city in January after six years. Instead, he took a better-paying job running a job-training program.
"Any job you get will pay more than substitute teaching does," he said. "When you're a substitute teacher, you're treated like a substitute. The kids automatically think it's a free day. It's almost babysitting."
Substitute teachers usually earn between $50 and $70 per day and that's with no benefits. That's not much of an incentive in a state that has an unemployment rate hovering around 3 percent and that recently was one of only five states in the nation to report a dramatic drop in unemployment claims. Worsening the shortage is the competition among schools for capable substitutes, many of whom register to work with several school systems. The shortage of substitutes has forced schools to cancel special classes like art or music, combine other classes and call upon principals to teach.
A bill in Hartford that addressed the substitute shortage by lowering the qualifications for the job failed this year in the Legislature's Education Committee.
"A concern among many members of the Education Committee was that we not lower the standards for substitute teachers," said State Representative Cameron Staples, the House chairman of the committee.
He said some committee members felt the shortage of substitutes was due more to the low pay than a shortage of qualified people. A proposal to offset some costs, like health insurance for retired teachers who substitute, was still something the committee was interested in. The committee plans to study the substitute issue this summer, and Mr. Staples said he expects a revised bill to be introduced in the Legislature's next session.
 The state Department of Education said it is against lowering substitute standards. Currently, schools are required to follow the following selection process in descending order: Substitutes should be certified in the subject area they teach. If the school cannot get someone to meet that standard, it should seek a teacher with certification in any subject. If not that, the minimum standard is a bachelor's degree.
Thomas Murphy, the department spokesman, said the proposed bill is unnecessary because the department already grants waivers of the bachelor's degree requirement on a case-by-case basis for people with experience in the classroom, such as a teacher's aide. The department has granted 635 waivers this year.
Some schools have been hiring permanent substitutes and paying them more than casual ones. "More and more, we hear schools talking about hiring certified teachers as permanent substitutes," said Daria Plummer, president of the Connecticut Education Association, the union that represents teachers. "It worked well in the past, and it's an idea whose usefulness has come back."
The Waterford Board of Education approved hiring five permanent substitutes at $80 per day. If no substitutes are needed in the classroom, they will be assigned other duties in the schools.
Waterford also raised the pay for substitutes this year from $60 to $70 per day and adjusted the schedules of full-time teachers to reduce the need for substitutes.
"We stopped calling in substitutes for half a day," said Randall Collins, the Waterford School Superintendent. "We were losing people to towns that could hire them for a full day."
The one thing Mr. Collins said he does not want to do is lower the standards for substitutes. "We're hoping these measures will make the job more attractive," he said. "We should maintain the requirement of at least a college degree as long as we can."
Virginia Seccombe, director of Learn, the education service center in Southeastern Connecticut, said school districts are considering banding together and contracting with a temporary employment agency that would hire full-time teachers, provide benefits and place teachers in the school districts. Substitutes who were not needed in one district would be assigned to another. "Districts that struggle to hire substitutes would have help," she said. "The full-time work and benefits would be the attractions." .
Mr. Collins said he doubts that the plan will save money, although the precise costs have not been worked out. "It will cost more than doing it on our own," he said. "Right now, I'm not on board. But if every town joins, Waterford may be forced into it."
Gary Gelmini, the principal of Buckingham School in Norwich, recruits substitutes for the school from Sacred Heart University in Lisbon, where he teaches education courses. Mr. Gelmini said he supports the proposed bill allowing undergraduates to work as substitutes. "I have some wonderful undergraduates who are going to make excellent teachers," he said.
Mr. Gelmini added that there are people in the community who, with some training, would also be able to handle substitute teaching. "There's a wealth of people out there, like parents, who have associate's degrees and experience with kids," he said. "I think they could do a really good job."
Students from the University of New Haven and Quinnipiac College in Hamden make up a portion of the substitute force in the New Haven schools. Seventeen students, most of whom are in master's degree programs, work in New Haven's classrooms. The school district pays part of the students' tuition plus a stipend, Ms. Wilder said. Parents and people who have worked or volunteered in the community are also hired upon completion of 20 hours of training in substitute teaching.
"To tell you the truth, they are better prepared than some of the people with degrees," said Ms. Wilder, who had obtained state Department of Education waivers for the parents and students. "They have a realistic view of what a teacher needs.
"We're trying to be very careful that we get good people," she said.
Some may be reluctant to make a commitment to substitute teaching, but once they hear that they will receive training and support, they are more apt to be interested, Ms. Wilder said. And although only eight people attended a recent orientation session for prospective substitutes in New Haven, there were two certified special education teachers in the group.
Bruce Royce, 58, of Montville is a retired teacher who returned to his old school, Teachers' Memorial Middle School in Norwich, to teach an eighth-grade American history class after its regular teacher went on maternity leave. The assignment will last for four months.
"I was starting to get itchy," Mr. Royce said. "I enjoy being back in the classroom, and I know I'll be through in June. I can look forward to time off again."
Mr. Royce said he prefers long-term assignments to working in a different classroom every day.
"It's not like going into a different class every day, where the kids say, 'Oh, boy! A day off. Let's give the teacher a hard time" ' he said. "I don't run into that. The kids know I'll be there the next day."

LENGTH: 974 words

HEADLINE: Q & A/Marge Hiller;
Group Uses Grants To Inspire Teachers

BYLINE: By NANCY POLK 

 BODY:
THE Bridgeport Public Education Fund makes education mountains out of cash molehills. The fund awards modest grants to Bridgeport teachers for classroom projects and provides seed money for other innovative school projects. The founding executive director is an energetic former librarian, Marge Hiller. "There isn't a community in the country that couldn't use an education fund," she said, "a partnership between people who care about education and educators." "The principle of bringing people together around education issues is very sound, very valuable. It makes the system stronger."
Recently Ms. Hiller discussed some of the accomplishments and plans for the fund in her office, in a stately blue Victorian house in a quiet University of Bridgeport neighborhood. Following are excerpts from that conversation: 
 Q. What are public education funds?
A. There are a bunch of ed funds, around 50, in the United States. They were created in 1983 by the Ford Foundation, which provided seed money to replicate something they had seen in Pennsylvania that was a partnership among county government, school districts and parents. They thought if public education was to move forward, the community should come together in urban and rural areas where there was a preponderance of really poor children. They started an organization called the Public Education Fund. Their mission was to take this money from the Ford Foundation and create local education funds around the country.
Q. How did the fund get started in Bridgeport?
A. In the case of Bridgeport, the head of the school volunteers association came back with the idea 15 years ago. They were just about to start an Adopt-A-School program. This was a nice fit. Superintendent Jim Connelly assigned someone to pull together a group of people to see if there was interest in the business community; there was. This happened rather quickly.
David Carson had just been hired by People's Bank and he had a history of being involved in public education by serving on the West Hartford school board. He agreed to spearhead this movement. He's been our chairman for 15 years.
Q. Where does the money come from?
A. Our money comes to us from local sources. We raise all our money from small businesses, corporations, individuals. One of our biggest donor bases is our teachers. They contribute through payroll deductions. We get no Federal money and no state money.
We have a very small endowment, $80,000, started by some of the original founders. It's at the Greater Bridgeport Community Foundation. Q. What kinds of programs do you support?
A. The whole premise of this organization is to build community support for public education, where people understand that everybody is responsible for what happens in the schools. Education isn't just about school buildings and teachers and what happens in those six hours. It is about pre-natal care, economics, where children live, how their families live, air quality, what the economic plan for the city is, how that impacts on children. Part of our mission is to bring people into the schools to see firsthand what's happening.
We've used our grant program as a way for people to meet teachers personally and to see what goes on in their classrooms, to travel around the city and see what's happening in the schools, where the schools are and what the neighborhoods look like. These are community people. The biggest draw for getting people into the schools is our mini-grants for teachers program.
Q. What is the mini-grants program?
A. Teachers apply for money for classroom projects, innovative things that they would like to do for classrooms that they normally pay for out of their own pockets. They can apply for $500. We get anywhere from 150 to 200 teachers applying for that money. We have about 80 people from the community who make up our allocations committee. They are assigned one or two grants from one or two teachers. They go to the schools and talk about their project. They become the authority on whether the project is going to be funded or not. The hard part here is that they want to fund everything because they never meet someone who doesn't have a good idea or isn't going to be good at what they do. The hard part is deciding how we're going to split up our $45,000.
Q. What do you do for high school students?
A. We have a college bound mentoring program called Maccs, Motivation for Academic Achievement in College Study. It was started by a board member. He gave money for an assessment which found a definite need for kids with college potential to have more support. We found that guidance counselors and career counselors were busy doing critical work and didn't have the time.
Our program was designed to hire work-study students paid by a Federal aid program. We use students from the University of Bridgeport, Fairfield University and Sacred Heart University.
Q. Do you have something focused on younger students?
A. Another board member brought a reading program called "First Day." We give a brand new book to each first-grade student on the first day of school. We have a hundred volunteers who go into the classrooms to hand them out. They have a little nameplate with their name on it.
We hoped that it would help families get their kids to school on the first day of school, not the second day, but the first day. We distribute fliers. Last year there were 2,300 first-grade students.
Q. Do you support teachers?
A. Some teachers are kind of tired. They have been in the system a long time. They have lost their enthusiasm for teaching. All over the country there are huge numbers of teachers retiring.
We want the people who are coming out of education schools to be highly qualified to teach in urban areas. We want them to like kids and want to be in our schools.

SECTION: Section 11; Page 9; Column 1; Real Estate Desk 

LENGTH: 1323 words

HEADLINE: Commercial Property /Lexington and Third Avenues, 42d and 43d Streets;
The Making of the Chrysler Center

BYLINE: By JOHN HOLUSHA 

 BODY:
WHEN Tishman Speyer Properties acquired the landmarked Chrysler Building, with its stainless steel peak, gargoyles and elaborate lobby, it also took possession of a far less glamorous structure at the other end of the block on 42d Street, an office building with the address of 666 Third Avenue.
Unlike the Chrysler building, which was built as a prestige project from the start (and was part of a race in the early 30's to build the world's tallest building), 666 Third Avenue was relentlessly average, with a tiny lobby, plain brick walls and an inefficient layout. 
 Named the Kent Building after its former owner, the late Jack Kent Cooke, the structure was generally seen as an annex to the Chrysler Building. Indeed some tenants occupied space in both buildings at lower levels on the 43d Street side, although the floor levels do not match.
Although most attention focused on the Chrysler Building, the purchase included the entire block between Lexington and Third Avenues and 42d and 43d Streets, with some smaller buildings occupying the mid-block area on 42d Street.
The new owners decided that with suitable alterations they could brand the entire block as the Chrysler Center, and promptly put up signs proclaiming the new name.
"We thought we could create a new neighborhood there, an eastern anchor for the new 42d Street," said Valerie Peltier, an official of Tishman Speyer who is the project manager for the Chrysler Center. Tishman Speyer officials say the budget for the entire Chrysler Center project is on the order of $100 million.
Working with the architect Philip Johnson, company executives decided to demolish the small mid-block buildings, use some of the air rights to add 130,000 square feet to the west side of what is now being called the Chrysler East building, expand and embellish the lobby and clad the entire building with a new metal and glass facade.
The remaining midblock space will be occupied by 85-foot-tall triangular glass structures that Mr. Johnson calls trylons, that are meant to evoke the distinctive shape of the top of the Chrysler Building. Tishman Speyer officials say they expect to sign up a first-class restaurant to occupy the space under the trylons. The addition to the east building was critical, said Ms. Peltier. "It was a side core building and it didn't work," she said, referring to the section of a building that contains the elevators, stairs and basic utilities that serve the adjacent office space.
By adding on to the western side of the building the core area is now in the middle and occupants of offices there can walk easily from one side of a floor to another without bumping into the core. Existing tenants have inquired about leasing space in the new section of the building to unify their operations, said Ms. Peltier, who is a daughter of Tishman Speyer's chief executive, Jerry I. Speyer.
The new space is column free, so operations that require open space, such as financial trading floors, can be accommodated.
Although the project is far from complete, Tishman Speyer has got an important vote of confidence. Credit Agricole Indosuez, a French-based bank that is the product of the merger two years ago of Credit Agricole and Banque Indosuez is leaving two other locations to occupy 130,000 square feet at Chrysler East.
AS a result they get to hang their name on the building, which will become the Credit Agricole Indosuez building, its third name in just a few years.
Mitchell S. Steir, a broker at Julien J. Studley who represented the bank, said the decision to move to the Third Avenue site from locations on the Avenue of the Amercas and Madison Avenue was made after an extensive study of real estate options, including the possibility of moving out of the city.
Because operating in two locations made daily operations and post-merger integration difficult, the bank wanted to consolidate its operations in one location. And by moving the a new location, the bank avoided the problems that might arise if one company was to move into the other's home office.
"You could see this as a trail blazing location for an international bank," Mr. Steir said. "But when you examine the quality of the renovation, the efficiency of the 32,000-square-foot floorplates and the ability to expand over time and the economics, the deal makes sense."
By moving to that location, brokers said, the bank can save as much as $10 a square foot in rent compared with prime locations in the upper 40's and mid-50's. The bank will initially occupy the seventh through 10th floors with expansion rights for the fifth and sixth floors available for growth.
And by signing up while the project is under way, the bank was able to exert influence over the design and save money by avoiding a duplication of efforts.
Like most financial institutions with elaborate-record keeping systems and trading operations, the bank wanted to install back up generators to insure a continued supply of electrical power in case of a blackout. Since the building owners wanted generators for other sections of the building, the prospective tenant and landlord worked together on the installation.
"You have to open the sidewalk to lower in the generators, so you don't want to have to do that twice," Mr. Steir said. "They can also share some things, like air shafts for the exhaust pipes. In general, a tenant can significantly lower the cost of installing power and telecommunications if it is done before the renovation is finished."
The bank expects to move in next April. Its move was delayed over complications involved with converting to the new European currency, the euro, and preparations for dealing with the year 2000 computer problem.
Credit Agricole Indosuez's metal sign will hang over a greatly revised and enlarged lobby. "There really wasn't a lobby there before, just three hallways from street entrances to the elevators," Ms. Peltier said.
The enlarged lobby will be faced with white granite and graced with art works and other Class A building touches. "It is going to look like a Park Avenue office building," she said.
The building's other systems, the heating and ventilating, the power and telecommunications are all being improved, she said, but the elevators turned out not to be a problem. "They had been redone in the 90's," she said.
Although the most obvious changes are being made to the east building, there is plenty of work to on the Chrysler Building itself.
Much of it involves the facade which, though protected as a landmark, must be brought into compliance with city regulations intended to prevent pieces from falling off buildings.
"We had to replace bricks in the facade due to deterioration," Ms. Peltier said. "So we had some bricks made up and took them to landmarks for permission to use them on the building. The bricks that were in storage were useless." In addition to replacing bricks, contractors are replacing lintels and repointing the mortar on much of the building.
Mistakes of the past are being repaired in the lobby of the Chrysler Building, where temporary lights now help illuminate a mural on the ceiling.
The mural had been hard to view, both because it had been poorly coated in the past and because holes had been cut in it in the past to accept recessed light fixtures. "We had to spend a couple of hundred thousand dollars repairing the holes in the ceiling that had been cut for light cans," Ms. Peltier said. She said the temporary lighting would eventually be replaced by fixtures that produce subdued, but adequate light, similar to the lighting at Rockefeller Center, which Tishman Speyer also manages.
One issue still under discussion is how to actually build Mr. Johnson's trylons, which will tilt at odd angles to each other. "It has taken months to do the engineering for them,"she said. "The concept is cool, but the trick is to figure out how to make them stand up."

PUB-DATE: May 30, 1999,

SECTION: Section 11; Page 1; Column 2; Real Estate Desk 

LENGTH: 3297 words

HEADLINE: For Class of '99, A Real-Life 'Rent'

BYLINE: By TRISH HALL 

 BODY:
WITH all the scarcity, hysteria, back-stabbing and astronomical prices in the real estate market, it would hardly seem possible for young people just out of college to find apartments.
But while apartments go quickly, they can be found, even for those more interested in what they do than in what they earn. Regardless of whether they are making $20,000 a year as editorial assistants or $45,000 (before bonuses) as starting investment bankers, this year's class of graduates will find ways to live in New York, even in Manhattan. But they have to be flexible about where they will live and ruthlessly efficient in their approach. There is no time to think things over, or to get to the bank for a certified check. 
 "I tell people to bring a briefcase with cash," said John P. Burke Jr., a realtor at John P. Burke Realty on Seventh Avenue in Brooklyn who specializes in Park Slope and neighboring areas.
Although brokers say the first or second person to see an apartment nearly always takes it, they also say new places are continually coming on the market, and that any new college graduate with a job -- and without a dog -- who is also relatively presentable as well as realistic about prices can find an apartment. The market has even loosened a bit, because of new buildings going up and people leaving rentals for co-ops and condos, making it slightly easier than last year.
"It isn't quite as hysterical this year," said Soozy Katzen, director of relocation services for Fox Residential Group. "If somebody is looking for a one-bedroom, you can actually keep them busy for a day, show them eight to 10 properties." Although it has never been easy or cheap to find places in New York, it was only 12 years ago, she said, that there was a surplus of apartments and clients could be offered dozens in every part of Manhattan.
Although brokers say that many young people want to live on the Upper West Side or in the West Village, those are among the most expensive areas. And while they probably associate the Upper East Side with the un-young and the un-hip, it is the neighborhood with the largest supply of bargains. They tend to be rent-stabilized places in old walkup buildings around York and First and Second Avenues.
A few weeks ago, agreeing to a request to show his best picks in Manhattan for less-expensive apartments, Gordon Golub, a manager at Citi Habitats, showed a small but sunny one-bedroom apartment in the 70's near York Avenue, for $1,015; a one-bedroom in the same neighborhood on the sixth floor of a walkup building for $1,400 that could be shared by two people; a basement studio apartment on 32d Street between Fifth and Madison Avenues for $1,250 in an elevator building; a minuscule apartment with no kitchen and a shared bath, at Broadway in the 20's, for $595 a month, and a large and airy studio apartment on West 34th Street between Ninth and 10th Avenues, in a doorman building with elevator, for $1,200.
That neighborhood, he said, has some relative bargains, just like the Upper East Side. He said he had nothing he considered well-priced for typical new graduates either downtown or on the Upper West Side.
The Hecht Group, a brokerage company that specializes in rent-stabilized apartments on the Upper East and Upper West Sides, recently could list a number of apartments available on the Upper East Side, including a studio at Lexington Avenue and 96th Street for $899, a one-bedroom that could be shared for $1,350 at 91st Street and Second Avenue, and a two-bedroom at York Avenue and 84th Street for $1,550.
Amnon Hecht, an immigrant from Israel who started the company three years ago with his brother Oded, said the company has access to 300 apartments on any given day. Deciding who gets them, he said, often involves a lot of intuition. "When I'm sitting with a person," he said, "I see if they're a nice human being."
In Manhattan, brokers say, people get the most modern apartments for the least money in the financial district, where a number of buildings have been converted from commercial to residential use. A two-bedroom apartment that would be $4,000 on the Upper West Side might rent for $2,600 in the financial district.
Most new graduates share apartments with friends from college or home. Studios, brokers say, are more likely to be rented to people in their mid to late 20's. One-bedroom apartments in Manhattan newly occupied by only one person have become the province of the well-heeled, of a single person making more than $100,000 a year.
The sharing of apartments never meant to be shared has spawned its own language, including the elegant and totally mystifying "one-bedroom convertible." What that means is that a temporary wall costing about $800 can be used to divide the living room, creating another bedroom. It's really a continuation of dorm life, because the living room almost disappears and becomes a place holding little more than a couch and a television.
Judy Kekesi, a Corcoran Group broker, recently rented an apartment in the financial district to three men who will divide a two-bedroom renting for $2,600. "They would rather sacrifice space than location," Ms. Kekesi said. "I had somebody who was willing to put bunk beds into a one-bedroom."
DORIAN THOMPSON, who works at Macy's as an assistant buyer, found a temporary sublet when she moved here last year after graduating from Pennsylvania State University with a major in marketing. Recently, she and a friend found a one-bedroom apartment at 63d Street and First Avenue and put up a wall in the living room. Each woman pays $800 a month. Although she considers herself a downtown kind of person, Ms. Thompson likes the neighborhood.
"It's really pretty and there's a lot of young people in my building," she said. She never seriously considered living in Queens or Brooklyn, in part because she figured any savings on rent would be wiped out by the cost of cabs. Although Astoria is only 15 minutes away on the subway, it's a big psychological distance, and she couldn't imagine that she would visit people living there. "At this age," she said, "you want to be in New York. I guess I'm a Manhattan snob in that way."
Christine Ball, who just graduated from Trinity College in Hartford and is starting work as a publicist in the book business, was able to rent in one day, through the Hecht Group, a two-bedroom apartment in the 90's near York Avenue that she will share with a friend from college, for $1,350 a month.
She said she had dealt with some other brokers who tried to push her into paying a higher rent. Hecht showed her several apartments, but there was only one she could afford. She liked it and took it immediately, using her parents to guarantee the rent.
Landlords usually require either that the tenant's weekly pretax salary be equal to or greater than the monthly rent, or that the monthly rent amount to one-fortieth of the annual salary. So, depending on the landlord's requirements, a person renting an apartment for $900 a month would have to have an income of either at least $36,000 or $46,800.
For the many college graduates who are lured by far less lucrative jobs in publishing, media, retail and the arts, rent needs to be about $600 a month, or they need to find a landlord who will accept their parents as guarantors who will pay the rent if necessary.
But that system helps only the children of the affluent who live in the tristate region, because most landlords will not accept guarantors unless the guarantors live in New York, New Jersey or Connecticut and have sizable incomes of their own. Very few will accept guarantors who live in, say, Missouri, where distance makes it too hard to collect any money due.
Although some graduating seniors have already started looking for apartments, the real rush comes in July and August, brokers say. Wanting to get settled early, Bing Tien and Torun Mathias, undergraduate majors in economics who are both moving here for jobs in investment banking after they graduate from the University of Chicago, came here recently for two days to look for an apartment. They had already contacted a Citi Habitats broker and studied the company's Web site, printing out listings of apartments that seemed suitable. They had also answered ads for "no fee" apartments but never managed to find one that wasn't already taken.
They also contacted Mr. Tien's father, an engineer who lives in Cherry Hill, N.J., and he agreed to meet them here and serve as a guarantor if necessary. The group met with a broker from Citi Habitats, and from a list of a few dozen possibilities chose three. After looking at two, they knew that the third, a one-bedroom duplex apartment on 32d Street between Park and Madison, was the right one.
The rent, $2,000, meant they would pay $1,000 each, an amount higher than their salaries justified by the landlord's calculation. So, despite being among the best paid of new graduates, they needed Mr. Tien's father as a guarantor. The senior Mr. Tien had arrived with his income-tax and bank statements. And it was a good thing, because they had to move quickly.
"Every time we looked at a place, an agent from the same firm or another firm was showing the place," the younger Mr. Tien said. When he and his roommate agreed they wanted that apartment, their agent called the landlord and said they would be over immediately. The students were also prepared with their bank statements, letters certifying employment, and photo identification. And, Mr. Tien's father had brought some money orders.
"Time is everything," the younger Mr. Tien said. If his father hadn't come, and the paperwork had taken time, he is certain that someone else would have got the apartment. "It's a gorgeous apartment with a lot of space," he said.
Young people who can't afford Manhattan rents, or who prefer low-rise neighborhoods, go to Brooklyn, Queens, Jersey City and Hoboken. Although some obviously choose to live in the Bronx, or Staten Island, the majority go where their peers go, to Park Slope, to Astoria, to Williamsburg, to Greenpoint.
Mr. Burke, the broker in Park Slope, said that the "overflow" who cannot find or afford apartments in Park Slope, where there are dozens of shops, restaurants and bars, go to Kensington or Windsor Terrace, nearby neighborhoods that are another stop or two on the subway.
"Usually what I tell people is you can get 10 percent more apartment for 10 percent less money," he said. The least expensive one-bedroom he can show in Park Slope is $1,100, which few young people can afford on their own.
However, on Prospect Avenue in Windsor Terrace, which is near the Fort Hamilton stop on the F train, he has available a two-bedroom apartment in a small building for $1,400, soon to be vacated by several young men in the insurance business. And, while apartments go quickly, he says he is "loaded" with listings right now.
In Kensington, he could offer a one-bedroom apartment in a house for $900. In addition to urging that aspiring renters arrive with cash so they can immediately pay the first month's rent and security deposit, he suggests they have a letter verifying their employment date and salary, a copy of their drivers' license, and a copy of their credit report if possible.
"It's like the old West," he said. "You're either quick, or you're dead in the street."
He also suggests bringing postal money orders and an ATM card, as alternatives to cash. "For a hot apartment, it could make all the difference," he said. "I've seen people lose apartments if they have to go to the bank."
While it is his job to provide landlords with cooperative tenants who can pay the rent, there are two categories of people who have a lot of trouble no matter how wonderful or how rich they are: lawyers, and people with dogs. Mr. Burke said he had been trying for six months to get an apartment for a young woman with a dog, and even has mug shots of the dog from various angles to show potential landlords.
"I tell people to bring pictures of the dog lying down," he said. "Half asleep is good. Docile, fat, old and half asleep is really good."
Lawyers have a difficult time because landlords tend to find them combative and litigious.
Less expensive than Park Slope and very attractive at the moment to people in their 20's are Williamsburg and Greenpoint, which are both less than 30 minutes from Manhattan on the subway. In Greenpoint, an apartment with four rooms and hardwood floors can be found for $1,200; a spacious two-bedroom that can easily be shared, without putting up any walls can be found for $1,400, and a three-bedroom would be $1,800.
"People from all over the country are moving here," said Ursula Doukhowetzky, the owner of Ula's Real Estate in Greenpoint. "They hear about it in Texas," she added, with a touch of amazement.
For the longtime Polish residents of Greenpoint, the invasion is welcome if they are property owners, but not so welcome if they are renters. "Local people cannot afford this rent," she said, and so they are moving farther out, to Queens.
Mrs. Doukhowetzky said that the minute she gets a listing, she rents it. The first person who sees it, she said, usually takes it. "They just say, 'Wow,' " she said. "They are surprised by what they are getting. And they go straight to the bank." Greenpoint seems particularly popular now, she said, with young fashion designers.
In Queens, young professionals are moving in droves to Astoria, which has long been a Greek-American stronghold. It's a working class neighborhood, like Greenpoint, with a number of old walkup apartment buildings and apartments in two- and three-family houses.
Grace O'Sullivan, a manager at Aamen Realty on Broadway in Astoria, said that many young people arrive having heard of Astoria but knowing little about it. They sometimes come with unrealistic expectations, she said, thinking they can find elevator buildings with health clubs.
They can't. But they can find very sunny apartments that have been cleanly renovated, often with great views of the Manhattan skyline, about two or three blocks from the Broadway stop on the N train, about 15 minutes from Manhattan.
She can show one-bedroom apartments with big eat-in kitchens for $900, or a two-bedroom apartment for $1,200 that could be shared. Most of the apartments in the larger buildings are rent-stabilized.
Although there is enormous demand in Astoria, she said, she can always find something for someone "when I commit to working with them." She said her exclusive listings with landlords rest on her ability to find responsible tenants, a process that many brokers mention. They use their instincts to figure out whether a prospective client will be a good tenant.
"We try to get a good feeling from them," she said. "We really observe their behavior in the office." A young man who was snooping around her desk would be in for some deeper than usual scrutiny, she said. What turns off landlords, she added, is someone who comes in and starts asking too many questions in a negative way, about things like cockroaches.
And some landlords care deeply about appearances, she said, so sometimes she has to tell a potential tenant to leave the nose rings at home.
Recently, two men aspiring to be Astorians walked in looking for help: Ben Stillman, who recently graduated from the University of Virginia and is planning to start graduate school in social work at Columbia University in December, and Hans Dempsey, who is graduating from Mary Washington College in Virginia with a major in French and planning to find work with an international company.
They had decided on Astoria after looking at the cost of apartments in Manhattan. "We looked at Manhattan and realized we can live far more lavishly if we live 10 minutes away," said Mr. Dempsey. Apparently judging them good prospects, despite their lack of jobs, an agent in the office, Monique Peron, led them away to see a two-bedroom apartment in a two-family house.
Although they didn't take that apartment, after leaving New York they agreed to take a place in Astoria sight unseen because their broker assured them they would like it.
Brokers, of course, are too expensive for many people, but working without them can be arduous. There are a few other alternatives. Megan Dineen, who graduated from college a year ago and moved to Hoboken, would prefer to be in Manhattan, and recently paid $150 to use a Web site, www.mlx.com, that provides lists of available apartments. She said it seems to offer useful information, although she hasn't yet found a place she likes.
Although some college graduates arrive with roommates, others look for them when they get here. Holly Policy, who graduated in December from the University of Florida, came to New York with a friend and $1,200. Just as she was down to her last $20, she found an entry level job in a media company. Since her arrival, she has been living in the attic of a house in Jamaica, Queens, with a friend for $200 a month, and making a one-hour commute.
So recently she paid $265 to sign up with Roommate Finders, which gives her names of people who might be good matches. Miss Policy never even tried going through brokers to find a place because their fees, 10 to 15 percent of a year's rent, are too high.
MOST of the places she has seen through Roommate Finders have not appealed to her, but recently she found a place, and a person, she liked, in a railroad flat on the Upper West Side. Her share would be $615 a month, and she would get her own bedroom, although her roommate would have to pass through it to get to her bedroom. So far, they are in the questioning stage, finding out about each other, and considering the match.
Although some about-to-be-graduates have already started looking, the rush really begins in June and accelerates from there. Some apartment seekers who have been arriving in the last week or so have learned that they are too early. Lauren Levitt, who just graduated from the University of Pennsylvania and is planning to share a place with Boris Bogatin and David Cotterman, fellow Penn graduates, was looking at apartments recently with a Citi Habitats broker.
Because they don't need the apartment until July, she will have to come back in mid-June, when her roommates will be out of the country, and try to find a place for the three of them.
Ms. Katzen of Fox Real Estate, who deals with a lot of bankers and lawyers, said that July and August are so busy "I never see my house in the country."
After graduation, she said, they all "take their last exotic trip around the world." And then they come to her, looking for a place. Sometimes, she tells them not to worry so much about what their apartments look like. "I tell them, you'll never be there," she said. "You're going to go home to sleep and send out for Chinese. They're really worked to death."
Indeed. Alan Kelly, who is moving to New York after he graduates from Williams College in Massachusetts, will share an apartment with two friends, and they will all work in investment banking. He said he knows he'll be working "80 or 90" hours a week, but the three men have decided that the West Village is the place for them, because it's so convenient.
The other day, they were on their way to New York to look at a place on West 10th Street with a doorman and three bedrooms for $3,500 a month. And the broker told them to hurry, because three guys were coming down from Yale to see the same place.
"Basically, it's entirely a scare tactic, I'm sure," said Larsen Fusco, who will share the apartment with Mr. Kelly. But nevertheless, they were planning to give it a look, before going off to Hilton Head on vacation.

HEADLINE: In the Region / New Jersey;
Jersey City Seeing Riverfronts Activity

BYLINE: By RACHELLE GARBARINE 

 BODY:
TWO speculative office buildings now rising on Jersey City's Hudson River waterfront have been or will soon be fully leased in less than a year, prompting their developers to start new office towers and other developers to start their own projects.
The buildings, one with 434,000 square feet at Newport Center and the other with 422,000 square feet at 90 Hudson Street, will be completed this fall. 
The additional office buildings on which work is now under way or about to begin total almost 1.7 million square feet of new or renovated space, much of it to be built on speculation.
In this round of construction developers are betting lightning will strike twice, given what they say is an unmet demand for new space at the water's edge. Indeed, there are users in the market, including PricewaterhouseCoopers and Thompson Financial, known to be shopping for more than 2 million square feet of space, commercial brokers report.
Last week plans were announced to add a three-story, 185,000-square-foot office building atop a seven-story, 1,100-car parking garage as well as a 350-room hotel at the Harborside Financial Center. The office building and garage are to be developed by the center's owner, the Mack-Cali Realty Corporation; the hotel is a joint venture with the Hyatt Hotels Corporation. The office building is being designed to the requirements of Waterhouse Securities, a discount brokerage concern, which will be its sole occupant.
With the dearth of new construction in Manhattan as well as in the other boroughs of New York City and in its suburbs, brokers and developers add, Jersey City's waterfront is well positioned to continue to draw tenants from across the river in search of larger and less expensive space.
Earlier this month, after having successfully wooed PaineWebber, Cigna and U.S Trust to its Newport Office Center III building, which is now nearing completion, the Lefrak Organization of Rego Park, Queens, broke ground for an 800,000-square-foot, 22-story speculative tower.
"This market is developing because there not only is a need for space now but a need for space at an affordable price," said Richard S. LeFrak, the company's president.
Lefrak is using its own money to finance the new building, known as Newport Office Center IV, which is expected to cost more than $100 million. It will rise north of Newport Office Center III, which has been renamed the PaineWebber building after its principal tenant.
The office towers are within the 600-acre Newport mixed-use development at the northern end of the city's waterfront, which will be connected to other riverfront developments by the light-rail system now under construction. The first phase, from Bayonne to Hoboken and including Newport, is expected to be completed by mid-2001.
About a mile south, Hartz Mountain Industries, of Secaucus has signed on Lord, Abbett & Company and National Discount Brokers Group as tenants at 90 Hudson Street, its 422,000-square-foot speculative building. And commercial brokers report that American Express is expected to sign on for the building's remaining 176,000 square feet as soon as next month.
Emanuel Stern, president of Hartz, would neither confirm or deny the interest of American Express in the building. But he did say that once the first building is leased his company will move forward with a second building, 70 Hudson Street, which will be similar in size to the first one and could also be built on speculation. It will rise next to the first building on the 20-acre site that once held Colgate Palmolive's toothpaste factory, which Hartz now controls.
AT the same time SJP Properties of Parsippany plans by early next year to transform into modern office space a 300,000-square-foot former manufacturing building at 74 Grand Street, behind the former Colgate site and inland from the river.
The rapid pace of leasing at the two speculative riverfront buildings, and the plans for more projects, testify to the strength of the office market along Jersey City's waterfront. Like Manhattan, the waterfront continues to be driven by financial services firms, said Robert Rudin, an executive managing director at the Saddle Brook office of Insignia/ESG, the national commercial brokerage firm. As long as Wall Street remains healthy, the next round of waterfront office construction should do well, he said.
There has been tremendous leasing activity in Manhattan in the last year," added Kenneth Krasnow, director of business development at Cushman & Wakefield, another national commercial brokerage. "That has pushed midtown firms downtown, and now that that space is filling the next natural progression" is the waterfront on the New Jersey side of the Hudson, which he called "an extension of downtown."
In today's tight employment market large companies tied to Manhattan can move part of their operations across the river and "still be close enough not to lose their workforce," said Mr. Krasnow.
Robert Freedman, vice chairman of the board at GVA Williams, a commercial brokerage in Manhattan, noted that downtown Manhattan executives also can actually see buildings on the New Jersey waterfront from their windows, which is a psychological benefit. Added to that, he said, is the fact that in the last two and a half years "Jersey City's waterfront has emerged as the low-cost provider of space."
As New York rents increase Jersey City's economic advantages have become more attractive. There is no corporate occupancy tax and while asking rents in the best building have inched up to an average of $33 a square foot a year they are still lower than those in Manhattan, which average $47 a square foot, but climb to $61 in prime midtown locations.
In addition, operating costs and real estate taxes are well below those in New York and the waterfront area is in a state-designated Urban Enterprise zone, where most corporate tenants pay no sales tax.
Some brokers said that an added factor was that while New York has provided tax incentives to retain major companies, primarily in Manhattan, little has been done to lure tenants to the other boroughs. On the other hand, New Jersey has helped developers with a state program that offers financial incentives to companies relocating or expanding anywhere in the state.
UNDER that program, initiated two and a half years ago, up to 80 percent of the personal income tax revenue generated by each new employee brought on the tax rolls can be returned to the companies for up to 10 years.
"It's a compelling program because it could shave an additional $3 to $5 a square foot per year off rents," Mr. Freedman said. As a result, he added, rents on the waterfront could be 25 to 40 percent lower than those for comparable space in Manhattan.
Along with accessibility and affordability Jersey City's waterfront is now also a mature market. Over the last two and a half years some 1,500 new riverside residential units have been added to the thousands that already exist, bolstering the office sector by providing housing to employees and bringing life to the area after the offices close. And the two office towers started last year increased the amount of offices built there to 7.4 million square feet, with a vacancy rate of just 2.3 percent.
"There is a greater critical mass and people like go where there are people," said Edwin H. Cohen, an executive director at the East Rutherford office of Cushman & Wakefield, who is handling the leasing at the Newport buildings. "Most of the users we are talking to are not reluctant brides."
With the two speculative buildings now rising basically spoken for, available space is shrinking elsewhere along the waterfront.
At the same time, space is tightening along the entire Hudson County portion of the riverfront, which had a vacancy rate of 5.9 percent as of the first quarter of this year. The same is true for midtown and downtown Manhattan, which registered overall vacancy rates 8.3 percent and 9.7 percent, respectively, in the first quarter, according to Cushman & Wakefield.
Just as Manhattan's space squeeze is a boost for Jersey City's waterfront so is the lack of new speculative construction anywhere is the region. While financing to start new buildings is available it is still dependent on finding a tenant. "This is one of those markets in which cash is king," said Mr. Krasnow.
"There are no alternatives, and we have the wherewithal to start a building without tenants," said Mr. LeFrak of the Lefrak Organization. "Jersey City, is a little bit of a field of dreams in that our philosophy is 'build it and they will come.' "
The market is strong enough to fill another 800,000-square-foot building, he said, adding: "We're building into an established community and providing a very good mousetrap."
Asking rents at the Newport Center IV are $30 a square foot a year, plus utilities. Occupancy is scheduled for September 2000.
"Speculative construction is a game of musical chairs," said Mr. Stern of Hartz. "It has to be done at the right time of the real estate cycle and right now we're in the middle of a good cycle." He said construction of 70 Hudson Street was expected to begin well before the end of the year.

HEADLINE: NEIGHBORHOOD REPORT: WASHINGTON HEIGHTS;
Bodegas Closed. Owners Cry Foul.

BYLINE: By NINA SIEGAL 

 BODY:
The Dume-Checo Grocery on the southeast corner of 162d Street and Fort Washington Avenue is boarded up and padlocked. The bodega had been operating on the ground floor of a residential building for 12 years before the city's Department of Buildings shut it down last month, said the president of the Dominican-Hispanic Chamber of Commerce, Jose D. Marte.
Across the street, another bodega has also been shuttered. That one, Mr. Marte said, had been operating for eight years. 
 The city has been closing down businesses illegally operating out of residential buildings as it steps up enforcement of zoning laws, said a Buildings Department spokesman, Paul Wein. But because many of the businesses are in upper Manhattan and are owned by Dominican shopkeepers, Dominican political and business leaders say they are concerned that the city is unfairly singling out their neighborhoods to pave the way for future development.
The Consul General of the Dominican Republic, Bienvenido Perez, said he was worried that the closings will create unemployment and that people will lose services. "Most of these owners who are being closed out have invested most of their resources into these businesses," he said. "The closures will affect their families and all the Dominicans in the surrounding neighborhood."
The Buildings Department has shut seven illegal stores in Manhattan this year, Mr. Wein said. He said he did not know how many were in Washington Heights. All were shut in response to complaints from neighborhood residents, he said.
Mr. Marte estimated that 15 stores had been been closed or given notice in the last five months. On Post Avenue near 204th Street, a meat market, a grocery and an international money transfer office are padlocked on one side of the block. Mr. Marte said the owners of a travel agency, a grocery and a barbershop across the street told him that the city had told them to close. He said shop owners usually leased their spaces and were unaware that operating a business in a residential building is illegal.
Mr. Marte is an accountant who worked in a bodega when he first came to the United States in 1969 from the Dominican Republic, and later opened his own bodega on 170th Street. "When Spanish people buy a store like that, you get your mother, your sister, everyone gives you a little money so you can open the store," he said. "At least the city should give them a couple of years so they can relocate."
The president of the Federation of Dominico-Hispano Chambers of Commerce, Agustin Garcia, said the city should re-evaluate its zoning to accommodate the businesses. "We took areas that were dilapidated and falling apart and made this into a vibrant neighborhood," he said. "That type of contribution makes you entitled to some support." NINA SIEGAL

HEADLINE: ECONOMIC VIEW;
A New Kind of Fed Watch: The Changing of the Guard

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
DURING a House Banking Committee hearing earlier this month, Representative Spencer Bachus, Republican of Alabama, warmly praised Robert E. Rubin, the outgoing Treasury Secretary. Then he turned to Alan Greenspan, the Federal Reserve chairman, who was sitting beside Mr. Rubin at the witness table.
"We ought to put you under protective custody," Mr. Bachus told Mr. Greenspan. "I hope you're well guarded and have good doctors advising you." 
 Now, Mr. Greenspan does not have many enemies these days, and at 73 he is apparently healthy and shows no signs of decamping to a retirement condo. But Mr. Bachus had a point.
Especially with Mr. Rubin's resignation, Mr. Greenspan is the embodiment of economic policy in the United States. His departure, under any circumstances, would create a crisis of confidence. And it is hard to see an obvious replacement waiting in the wings.
Mr. Greenspan's third four-year term as Fed chairman will expire in just over a year. He has not given any public hint about his plans. The betting in Washington is that he will choose to stay for another term, that President Clinton will be happy to nominate him and that the Senate will confirm him expeditiously.
But Mr. Greenspan could still prove to be less out of the Cal Ripken mold -- an Iron Man soldiering on, but at risk of playing past his prime -- and more of a Michael Jordan, choosing to retire while on top of his game. For an economic policy maker, on top might be defined as next year, when the current expansion will become the longest in the nation's history.
IN any case, since Mr. Greenspan's willingness and ability to serve another term are not sure things, speculation has begun about who will be tapped to succeed him, whenever he goes. Here is a handicapper's guide to likely entries in the race. (The list would look very different if that moment comes after a new President is elected, especially a Republican President.)
* William J. McDonough, the president of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York, is on nearly everyone's list. He is the leading candidate from within the Federal Reserve, if only because of the key role the New York Fed plays in monitoring the financial system at home and abroad. A former banker and diplomat, he played an important part in orchestrating the rescue of Long-Term Capital Management last year and is active in efforts to strengthen global banking standards. But while he is well known on Wall Street, he is little known in Washington. And although he votes loyally with Mr. Greenspan on interest rate policy, he has never established a strong public reputation as an inflation fighter. Early line: 4-1.
* As vice chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, Alice M. Rivlin is an obvious candidate, and within the White House the notion of appointing a woman to the post has some appeal. But while she is a well-respected economist, she is viewed with some suspicion on Wall Street, and she may have diminished her influence within the Fed by spending much of her time helping the District of Columbia clean up its finances.
Early line: 10-1.
* Janet L. Yellen, chairman of the White House Council of Economic Advisers, is also seen by Administration insiders as a viable candidate. A former Fed governor, she has a strong reputation among academic economists as well as Washington experience. But some analysts question whether she has the leadership qualities a Fed chief needs.
Early line: 15-1.
* Other long shot candidates include Laurence H. Meyer, who was appointed a Fed governor by Mr. Clinton and is the board's most outspoken proponent of orthodox inflation fighting (20-1). Outside of the Fed, Mr. Clinton could turn to a corps of executives who move between the worlds of Washington and finance. Among them is Franklin D. Raines, the former White House budget chief, who is now chief executive at Fannie Mae (25-1).
* Of course, Mr. Clinton has two other candidates he can call on in a pinch. One is Lawrence H. Summers, the Deputy Treasury Secretary and Mr. Clinton's nominee to succeed Mr. Rubin. A brilliant economist and a forceful personality, Mr. Summers has all the gravitas the Fed job requires and seems to be learning the art of keeping his mouth closed when circumstances dictate, a requirement for any Fed chairman. Among his fans is Mr. Greenspan, who publicly endorsed his nomination for Treasury Secretary. Should Mr. Clinton need him more at the Fed than at Treasury, Mr. Summers would be the clear favorite (2-1).
* Then there is Bob Rubin. The outgoing Treasury Secretary shows no signs of wanting to come back to Washington. Moreover, he is not an economist and could find the intricacies of monetary policy heavy going. But his reputation on Wall Street is second only to that of Mr. Greenspan. And if there were a crisis, and the President called . . . .
(A lead-pipe cinch, but only under those unlikely circumstances).
In the meantime, is there a second to that motion for protective custody?
