HEADLINE: PRIVATE SECTOR;
From Brazil to Wall St. and Back

BYLINE: By SIMON ROMERO 

DATELINE: RIO DE JANEIRO

 BODY:
THE United States has had only two central bank chiefs in the last two decades, but boom-and-bust Brazil has had 20, an average of one a year.
Now, three months into the term of the newest head of Brazil's central bank, Arminio Fraga Neto, a soft-spoken former hedge fund manager for the billionaire investor George Soros, Brazilians hope that the roller-coaster ride is smoothing out. They also hope that Mr. Fraga, like the United States' departing Treasury Secretary, Robert E. Rubin, can use his experience in the high echelons of finance to advance his government's goals. 
 "The market was in need of a respected trader, and Fraga filled this gap," said Fernao Bracher, a former president of the central bank who is now the head of Banco BBA Creditanstalt, an investment bank here.
But Mr. Fraga, who is 41, brings more than trading experience to his new post. While overseeing emerging-markets investments for Mr. Soros, he also taught part-time at Columbia University. Now, back in Brazil, he has been credited as the driving force in restoring Brazil's credibility with foreign investors in the wake of a currency crisis earlier this year.
Mr. Fraga's course in bringing the country back from the economic brink was relatively straightforward:
Knowing that the devaluation of Brazil's currency would make imports more expensive, he sought to pare consumer demand by raising interest rates to 45 percent. The high rates also helped attract foreign capital. Mr. Fraga, who holds both Brazilian and American citizenship, then traveled to the United States, where he convinced large banks to renew credit lines to Brazil. Since then, interest rates have declined rapidly, to 23.5 percent, and Mr. Fraga has signaled that they have further to fall. But after a 10.9 percent stock market tumble in the last two weeks, more work may be needed first.
"Arminio was very bright and very tough, and he had a hard time choosing between university and the private sector," said Winston Fritsch, his former colleague on the faculty of Pontifical Catholic University here and now president of the Brazilian operations of Dresdner Kleinwort Benson, an investment bank.
In fact, Mr. Fraga has kept a foot in both camps since receiving a Ph.D. in economics from Princeton University in 1985. Before working for Mr. Soros, he was at Salomon Brothers and Banco Garantia, a leading Brazilian investment bank now owned by Credit Suisse First Boston. In 1984, he worked for the Federal Reserve's research division in Washington.
The international finance course Mr. Fraga taught at Columbia illustrates how his varied experience helped him prepare for leading the central bank, where he worked in a lower-level job in the early 1990's. Students wrote a a hypothetical memo to the finance minister of a country of their choice, diagnosing the country's problems and proposing solutions. Next, they wrote an investment report about a country.
"Factoring in this whole globalization thing helped people to think of how markets would react," Mr. Fraga said in an interview at the central bank's branch here, his relaxed manner and careful choice of words seeming more suitable to a professor than a power broker.
"Approaching problems from both sides of the fence is necessary," he added. In Brazil, Mr. Fraga hopes to continue teaching, with a similar course planned for later this year at the Fundacao Getulio Vargas, a prestigious local economics institute.
Although Mr. Fraga has captured the public imagination in a way his 19 recent predecessors never did, he has also faced challenges. When he appeared before a congressional committee considering his nomination, an issue was made of his dual citizenship. Mr. Fraga, who grew up in Rio in a family of doctors, responded that it was because his mother was an American, which had little relevance to his ability to protect Brazil's economic interests.
More troubling, an opposition politician compared him to a fox guarding the chicken coop, because of his previous work for the type of hedge fund that can wreak havoc on emerging-market currencies. During later testimony, he often appeared tired while explaining the workings of the central bank to senators with little knowledge of the institution. "I'm not used to the public speaking and the politics and all that," he said.
And last week, his aides were denying a critical report in the nation's leading news magazine, Veja, that he had been dismissed by Mr. Soros because of bad bets on Asia. They said there were bad bets, but no dismissal.
In another congressional appearance, Mr. Fraga had to rebut accusations that the central bank, under his predecessor Francisco Lopes, acted improperly during the sharp shift in exchange-rate policy in January. Nevertheless, his greatest political strength may well be that he was not involved in that change.
Politics notwithstanding, Mr. Fraga is settling back into life in Brazil. Like many high-ranking officials, he chose to live away from Brasilia, the capital city deep in the interior.
Instead, he moved his family to Rio and takes an hour-and-a-half flight each Monday and Friday. In Brasilia, he stays in an apartment and tries to take walks before work -- an activity that he says helps him think and sleep better.
During weekends in Rio, he has become a fixture at the Gavea Golf Club, where he plays golf with his 13-year-old son, Sylvio. As for his staying power at the central bank, Mr. Fraga remains cautious. "We're playing the third hole in a 72-hole tourney," he said. "It's a good start, but that's all it is."

HEADLINE: BEHIND THE WHEEL/Chevrolet Silverado;
The Cover Is Familiar But the Book Is All New

BYLINE: By JAMES G. COBB 

DATELINE: HOLTON, Kan.

 BODY:
HOW important are General Motors' full-size pickups? Check out Wall Street's numbers or Main Street's traffic. These are the company's most popular products, with some 700,000 sold annually. They are lucrative, returning several thousand dollars in profit for each one sold. They also serve as the basis for a fleet of big sport utilities that account for another 400,000 even more profitable sales.
Furthermore, with demand exceeding supply, G.M. hopes to ramp up production this year to build an additional 300,000 trucks and sport utilities. If it succeeds, it could end up with $30 billion in annual sales from this platform -- enough to rank in the top 35 of the Fortune 500, exceeding the value of all the microchips from Intel, all the drugs from Merck, all the sodas and snacks from Pepsico. G.M. can use a runaway hit, particularly as it has lost its ranking as the world's largest industrial company. Ford claimed that title on Friday when G.M. spun off its huge Delphi parts operation. 
 Or, if you prefer anecdotal evidence to raw numbers, look at the streets of Middle America. In this town of 4,000 a half-hour from Topeka, where the economic focus shifted long ago from farming to commuting, one thing has not changed. A lot of folks here, where I grew up, like Chevy pickups. You see them in many of the parking spaces around the monumental county courthouse, and they are lined up at the Dairy Queen drive-through. Along some roads there seems to be a pickup wearing a Chevy bow tie in almost every driveway.
Trucks account for 6 of every 10 sales at Clark Chevrolet Oldsmobile Pontiac Buick, said Bernie Clark, co-owner of the dealership with his two brothers, and 90 percent of those trucks are full-sized Silverados. Once relegated to the farm or job site, they now routinely provide family transportation.
Like most truck owners, Mr. Clark's customers are stubbornly loyal; about 80 percent are repeat buyers. Over the years -- the family's ties to the dealership date to 1947 -- some customers have driven away in as many as 15 trucks.
Such loyalty helps to explain why G.M. has changed everything about its full-size pickups except the essential way they look. The styling is new, yes, but even a trained eye must look twice to tell a '99 from a '98. G.M. says this is by design, and it marshals surveys indicating that many truck shoppers find the Ford F-Series too cute and the Dodge Ram too brutish -- never mind that the F-Series still outsells Chevy's truck, and that the latest Ram has grabbed a much bigger market share. But one has to believe that other buyers, after plunking down as much as $33,000, want the neighbors to know they've sprung for something new.
And under the skin, the Silverado certainly is new. After a 10-year run for the C/K series, G.M. put all its might into making the replacements a success. It came up with new engines, a new frame, new brakes. The crowning touch is a classy cabin that makes the competition's look, well, truck-like.
The Silverado isn't perfect -- it lacks the fourth door that many buyers now consider essential -- but it is genuinely pleasant in other important ways. Not that the C/K is bad -- it remains, despite its dated design, one of the easiest-driving pickups -- but the Silverado handles more securely, accelerates more briskly, consumes less fuel and provides far more comfort and convenience. (The C/K is still built in two plants, alongside Silverados, to fill fleet orders.)
There are five engine choices: The 4.3-liter V6 is carried over, but the 4.8- and 5.3-liter V8's are new, though based on the aluminum Corvette engine. There is also a new 6-liter V8 for three-quarter-ton trucks. And, for those who want to move mountains, there is a 6.5-liter turbo-diesel V8 producing 440 foot-pounds of peak torque.
 Prices start around $16,000 for a plain two-wheel-drive model. The truck I drove in Kansas, a two-wheel-drive LS 1500 extended cab, had a list price of $26,692; in New York, I had a four-wheel-drive LS priced at $32,203. I also tested an extended-cab GMC Sierra SLT -- except for its nose, the Sierra is much the same -- with a flared Sportside bed; its sticker was just over $33,000.
 All three test vehicles were equipped the way most of these trucks will be, with the 5.3-liter V8. It is a honey, another example of how G.M. has put new life in old pushrod designs. Ford, meanwhile, has added more modern overhead-cam V8's that will allow it to cut truck emissions next year to the stricter level required of cars.
The Chevy engine is smooth and gutsy, with 270 horsepower and 315 foot-pounds of torque, and it ought to please everyone from hockey mom to cattle hauler. The torque curve is flatter for more pull over a wider speed range. The four-speed automatic transmission (a five-speed manual is standard) felt rugged and workmanlike. It offers a towing mode that shifts at higher speeds.
Four-wheel-drive pickups are available with a transfer case that engages all four wheels automatically when it senses a loss of traction, along with a standard four-wheel setting and a low range for serious muck.
Silverados now come with either of two types of steering. Lightweight two-wheel-drive models get a nimble rack-and-pinion system, while four-wheel-drive trucks have a conventional recirculating ball that can stand up to heavy use. The latter system can seem numb at times, but I prefer it to the nervous feel of the Ford.
The Silverado still handles like a pickup, but it is honest and balanced. The chassis keeps you aware of its limits, giving you confidence in how it will behave in any maneuver. A dignified highway cruiser, it is maneuverable enough in town and unruffled on back roads. Some cars should do so well.
 And so it is with the brakes, never before anything to brag about. There are disks front and back, and the pads are 40 percent larger. A four-wheel anti-lock function is standard. Stops are sure and straight and the pedal is firm now, not spongy.
 The ride, too, is car-like, refined and quiet on all but the worst roads. The optional firm-ride suspension indeed stiffens the ride, but it adds little to the handling.
Of all the improvements, the cabin stands out; a lot of thought went into the needs of pickup owners. The outside mirrors are large and handsome and the inside door handles are big and easy to grip. The glove box is split into compartments. (Even the cargo box can be divided by thirds.) The gauges are large and legible, and there is an information center that relays up to 18 messages, from "cargo lamp on" to "transmission fluid hot." The knobs are durable and the buttons are big and chunky. Window and lock controls are lighted at night, just like those in a nice sedan. Power is retained after the key is switched off, letting you roll up the windows or catch the end of a song.
 There are sour notes, but they are minor. The dash and console comprise so many pieces that there seems to be seams everywhere; imagine trying to clean up a spilled Coke! The optional CD player is down near the console, far from the radio. The ashtray, lighter and two auxiliary outlets reside under a top-hinged lid that hides them even when open. As usual, the G.M. cruise-control switches on the turn signal stalk are clunky.
One design oddity: The most prominent place on the overhead console is a blank panel. Buttons for G.M.'s Onstar driver service may eventually go there; meanwhile, it is a waste of prime real estate.
And, while I'm at it, some more gripes: The door locks engage as the truck moves, but when you stop you have to unlock them manually; once parked, a handle pull should suffice. The console box is big and deep, but the lid won't stay open. And, as usual with G.M. vehicles, the combination of daytime running lights -- I'm not a fan -- and an automatic light control can be baffling: Are the headlights on, or just the driving lights? Do I need to switch the lights off, or will they go out on their own? Why do the lights have to be on if I want to idle inconspicuously?
My favorite thing about the Silverado may be the optional six-way power seats, which just could be the best G.M. has ever put in an American vehicle. Accommodating for anyone up to World Wrestling Federation size, they are highly adjustable and attractive. The headrests are good and the lumbar adjustments are great, taking the lower backrest from pillow-soft to rock-hard. Safety belts are mounted to the seats, aiding access to the rear.
Until now, the back seats in extended cabs have ranged from unpleasant to ridiculous. But here finally is a rear seat where adults can sit in reasonable comfort. The bottom cushion is 2.4 inches longer, and there are four more inches of leg room. The backrest is slanted, not bolt-upright, and there are even two headrests, a cup holder and ventilation ducts. There is a price to pay for this spaciousness, though; the cab's longer roofline looks a bit ungainly.
If the Silverado sets new standards for pickups, as I think it does, is there anything about it to give one pause? Well, aside from the conservative styling, there's that lack of a fourth door, which will arrive in the fall. This is no small oversight: Buyers of extended-cab pickups have expressed a clear preference for two small rear-hinged doors. The Silverado's third door, on the passenger side, always seemed to be where the cargo and passengers were not.
The company says it wanted to be sure it had a safe, solid design for a four-door Silverado. That is commendable. But after Dodge brought out its Ram Quad Cab, Ford quickly matched it, then offered four doors for all its trucks. G.M.'s tardiness points up, again, how slow reaction times force the company to keep playing catch-up.
Still, G.M. has done a remarkable job of fundamentally remaking the full-size truck. But it rests at its peril; Toyota is just out with its full-size Tundra. It replaces the T-100, an uncharacteristic disappointment from a company that doesn't make the same mistakes twice. And by the time Ford and Dodge remake their big trucks in a few years, you can bet they'll have dissected the Silverado down to the last screw.
 
INSIDE TRACK: A truck just one door short of stardom.

HEADLINE: INVESTING;
For Ad AgencyGiants, The Weather Is Just Right

BYLINE: By JOANNE LEGOMSKY 

 BODY:
BENEATH the patina of economic abundance, business competition is intensifying. Hard-pressed to fatten profits by cutting costs or raising prices, companies are pursuing a time-honored alternative -- trying to bolster sales by shoveling money into advertising.
"Businesses hate sinking money into advertising," said Mark Greenberg, manager of the Invesco Leisure fund, which counts some big ad agency companies among its core holdings. "But they can't afford to underspend the other guy." 
 Last year was the fifth in a row that the growth in advertising spending in the United States outpaced the growth of the economy, rising 7.1 percent, to $200.8 billion, against a 3.9 percent increase in the gross domestic product.
That story has not eluded the stock market: The average total return of the Big Four global agency holding companies -- Omnicom Group, Interpublic Group, Young & Rubicam and WPP Group -- was 37.7 percent for the 12 months ending Friday, versus 18.6 percent for the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index.
Analysts say there is reason to believe that the stocks haven't finished their climb. "Ad stocks have a consistent record of expanded earnings outperformance late in the economic cycle," said William Bird, an analyst at Salomon Smith Barney.
Mr. Bird attributes the outperformance to several factors, including the big agencies' global scope, which limits exposure to any single country's economy. He also cites flexible cost structures -- meaning that with staff representing about 60 percent of costs, the big companies can readily economize by trimming their work forces -- and their ability to grow through acquisitions.
Indeed, he thinks some of the stocks are relatively cheap, as illustrated by their P.E.G. ratios -- the projected price-to-earnings multiple for 2000, divided by longer-term projected earnings growth rates. Interpublic, Omnicom and Young & Rubicam have P.E.G. ratios of 1.8, 2.0 and 1.2, respectively, versus 5.4 for the S. & P. 500, Mr. Bird said -- much greater discounts than usual. He expects Omnicom, which closed on Friday at $70, to rise to $83 in 12 to 18 months; Interpublic, now at $75.75, to hit $88, and Y. & R., now at $38.25, to hit $50.
There are worries, certainly. Lauren Rich Fine, an analyst at Merrill Lynch, said that any weakening of the global economy could pose a big risk to ad agency stocks, because corporate advertising budgets are tied to sales forecasts.
But what some investors do not realize, she added, is that more agency revenue is coming from recession-resistant services like public relations and direct marketing. Those businesses now account for $200 billion of the $417 billion in industry revenue worldwide, she said.
The economic downturn of 1990-91, which contributed to the worst advertising recession ever, was an aberration, according to Robert J. Coen, senior vice president and forecasting director at McCann-Erickson U.S.A., which is owned by Interpublic. "Companies abandoned marginal brands and waged a no-win strategy of slashing prices to move inventory," he said.
Ms. Fine is especially bullish on Omnicom. Excluding its growing portfolio of Internet agencies, she expects the company's earnings to keep rising 15 to 20 percent a year.
Mr. Greenberg, the Invesco manager, also expects agency stocks to keep generating double-digit earnings growth for the foreseeable future. Omnicom represents 4 percent of his fund's assets; WPP, 3.3 percent, and Y. & R., 1 percent.
Whether marketers favor cable, billboards or the Internet, he said, "advertising will always be important."

HEADLINE: INVESTING WITH: Charles Albers;
Oppenheimer Main Street Growth and Income Fund

BYLINE: By VIRGINIA MUNGER KAHN 

 BODY:
IN April 1998, Charles E. Albers moved his computer models from Park Avenue to Main Street. Or, to be more exact, from the Guardian Park Avenue fund, where he had been manager for 26 years, to Main Street Income and Growth, managed by the Oppenheimer Funds.
Having beaten the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index by an average of 3.7 percentage points a year at Guardian, where he was a pioneer among fund managers in using quantitative methods to pick stocks, Mr. Albers was hired by Oppenheimer to turn around Income and Growth, which had fallen on hard times. 
 How is he doing? So far, the results from his Oppenheimer office in the World Trade Center in Manhattan are encouraging, if not overwhelming. In the 13 months since he and his associate, Nikolaos Monoyios, have been in charge, the fund is up 18.2 percent, substantially ahead of the 13.9 percent average for its peer group of large-company blend funds.
But the fund -- whose name has been reconfigured to Main Street Growth and Income, to reflect the managers' emphasis on total return, not just income -- has trailed the S. & P. by 1.4 percentage points. Performance has weakened in 1999; the fund is up 6.3 percent through May 21, versus 7.2 percent for its peers and 8.7 percent for the S. & P 500.
Mr. Albers said the gains slowed because he had positioned the fund to take advantage of the historically strong first-quarter performance of value-oriented stocks, a phenomenon that failed to develop this year. "Although our models are good, they are not infallible," he said.
When Mr. Albers started managing money in the 1960's, most stock pickers had no data-based system, he said. But after studying econometrics at Columbia University and working at Value Line, he developed a ranking system that, while primitive, imposed a logic on his moves.
"I felt like a one-eyed man in the land of the blind," the professorial-looking manager told a recent Morningstar conference in Chicago.
That initial model ranked stocks on three factors; he now considers 15 to 20, from price and earnings momentum to valuation and special events like stock buybacks. In 1990, when Guardian Park Avenue was floundering because it was tilted toward small-cap and value stocks, Mr. Albers started using some broad, macroeconomic factors in his system, to help choose stocks both by market capitalization and their growth or value bias.
He is still updating his method. "We're making midcourse corrections every week," he said. "It's a combination of science and art." The Main Street fund's turnover is 81 percent, versus 63 percent for the average large-cap blend fund.
His recent trimming of his position in Microsoft illustrates how the system works. Through the end of April, Microsoft was the fund's biggest holding, at more than 5 percent of the portfolio. Since then, he has cut it to 3.6 percent, roughly equal to Microsoft's weighting in the S. & P. 500.
He made the change for various reasons: Microsoft is no longer scoring in the top 10 percent in his screens for likely outperformers. And his model indicates that a genuine market sea change may be in the works, finally giving value stocks an advantage over growth. "We have to try to align ourselves with the way of the universe," he said, using a phrase he often repeats.
Among the signals, he said, are an improved picture for corporate profits; a steepening of the yield curve, which indicates stronger business conditions ahead, and the very high valuations on growth stocks like Microsoft, which has a price-to-earnings ratio of more than 50.
Mr. Albers also thinks that the outcome of the Government's antitrust case against Microsoft will be a negative for the stock.
It is too early to tell whether the outperformance of value stocks over the last month and a half will last, so, Mr. Monoyios said, he and Mr. Albers are not making major changes to the fund. "We want to see a persistence of factors over the long term," Mr. Monoyios explained.
Technology remains a major focus of the fund; I.B.M. is the biggest holding, at about 4 percent of the portfolio. Mr. Albers also finds Hewlett-Packard attractive. He first bought it in March at $71.35 a share, and at $94.3125, where it now trades, he still considers it cheap.
ANOTHER big part of the portfolio is in consumer cyclicals, primarily retailers. Demand remains strong, while many retailers' costs for labor and goods are flat or falling, he said, resulting in wider profit margins. Wal-Mart Stores and Home Depot, both of which had positive earnings surprises recently, are among his top 10 holdings.
Other stocks that he finds attractive in his favorite sectors are BellSouth in telecommunications and Merck in pharmaceuticals.
As Main Street's returns indicate, the big challenge for Mr. Albers and Mr. Monoyios will be lifting this leviathan -- with $15 billion in assets, counting all share classes -- above market averages.
"This fund is not going to be the No. 1 fund in any one year, because we don't take those kinds of risks," Mr. Monoyios said. Still, he added, "being consistently good in the short run makes you excellent in the long run."

HEADLINE: MARKET WATCH;
Pop! Goes the Bubble

BYLINE: By GRETCHEN MORGENSON 

 BODY:
HAS E-phoria ended? Highflying Internet stocks look mighty weak. Even on Friday, when other Nasdaq stocks were strong, many Internet shares couldn't muster their typical big moves up. As the week ended, shares of Internet concerns were well off their mid-April peaks. The Interactive Week index of 50 stocks is down 17 percent from its highs, while Thestreet.com index of 20 Net companies has fallen 26 percent since April 12.
Battered E-investors are wondering whether the recent action is simply a pause in an upward trend or the beginning of the end of a glorious ride in these stocks. 
 While no one is certain, there is a host of reasons why the downturn may continue. Broadly speaking, investing in Internet shares has changed. And investors who don't realize that are vulnerable.
The factors working against these stocks are both macro and micro.
One of the big-picture problems is rising interest rates -- bad for almost all equities, but especially damaging to stocks of Internet companies, which rely heavily on debt to finance their money-losing operations. As interest rates rise, these companies' debt costs do, too.
Recent figures on Web use are also worrisome. Merrill Lynch's estimates of the total number of Internet users declined from 61.6 million in March to 61.1 million in April. While Merrill's resident Internet bull, Henry Blodget, cautions that one month may not make a trend, he points out that April's drop was only the second such decline in the last 12 months.
Perhaps more significant than either interest rates or Web use, however, is the shift in supply and demand that has changed the way these shares trade. Simply put, there are many more E-sellers than there were before -- a change, almost imperceptible, that is weighing on many Internet stocks today.
Start with the throngs of companies taking their shares to market. According to Commscan L.L.C., an investment banking research company in New York, there have been 69 initial public offerings of Internet-related shares so far this year. During all of 1998, there were only 42 such offerings. And while E-commerce companies accounted for 11.5 percent of all new issues last year, excluding closed-end funds and American depository receipts, Internet I.P.O.'s have accounted for 44 percent of new stock issues in 1999.
Also adding to supply is the fact that as newly public Internet concerns age, more stock hits the market from executives and insiders. These individuals are generally prohibited from selling any shares until several months after the I.P.O.'s.
Even as extra Internet shares flood the market, demand is drying up. One measure: The money going into Internet-related mutual funds has dropped from $470 million in the week ended April 14 to $34 million last week.
Complicating matters is a small-picture issue: a dearth of natural buyers -- the short-sellers -- waiting to step in even if these stocks keep falling. Investors willing to risk their money selling stocks short must buy them back later to close their positions. As a result, short-sellers represent future buyers. But since so many who bought this way lost their shirts betting against Internet stocks, few remain standing or solvent.
At the height of the craze, Internet buyers were many while the sellers were few. Now the tables have turned. Ah, well. It was fun while it lasted.

HEADLINE: The World;
Hey, Allies, Follow Me. I've Got All the New Toys.

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY 

 BODY:
NATO's bombing campaign in Yugoslavia has exposed more than policy differences between the European and American alliance partners. As never before, it has revealed the vast technological gulf that separates American military capabilities from those of Britain, France and the others. And that, in turn, shows just how much the Europeans remain militarily dependent on the United States for their own defense -- dependent, and resentful.
For Europe these days, the word defense means the ability to deal with problems like Kosovo on their own continent. But that, more and more, means the ability to bring technology to bear. And since the Americans have most of the technology, the Americans call most of the shots on strategy and tactics. 
 Take the way the air war has developed.
From the beginning, all the allies hoped a ground war would be unnecessary. But nobody except the United States had enough sophisticated F-14, F-15, F-16 and F-18 fighters and fighter-bombers to launch the kind of campaign that might avoid a ground war.
And nobody but the United States had the "stealth" F-117 fighters and B-2 bombers whose strange shapes and composite materials make it hard for Yugoslavia's sophisticated air defenses to shoot them down.
So the allies agreed on an air war but had to accept American ideas about how it should be fought.
The primary American concern was to minimize allied losses, and the allied losses have been negligible. But the bombing campaign has not prevented continued ethnic cleansing in Kosovo, and now this is widening fissures that were probably there from the start.
Consider one reason the B-2's fly their missions all the way from Whiteman Air Force Base in Missouri: The United States does not want even most of its European friends to get a close look at the planes. And among all its allies, the United States has judged only Britain trustworthy enough to acquire any of the satellite-guided American-developed Tomahawk cruise missiles, which can carry out precision attacks from ships hundreds of miles away. Even the British got only 65, while the United States has hundreds.
Having to deal with Kosovo the American way, with bombing instead of ground troops, had advantages for the Europeans, but also drawbacks. The longer the bombing drags on inconclusively, the greater the frustration and resentment in both Europe and America.
Now, as some military thinkers are beginning to say a ground campaign may become necessary before autumn, all that the allies have been able to agree on is a plan to put 50,000 troops in Kosovo as peacekeepers if Yugoslavia's President, Slobodan Milosevic, finally backs down and lets international troops escort Kosovo's refugees back home. But even that plan, calling for perhaps 7,000 American ground troops, would not dull the edge that technology gives the Americans.
If tens of thousands of fresh troops must be flown in quickly as peacekeepers, only the United States has enough giant C-5, C-141 and C-17 air transports to do the job. And if the allies decide this summer that air power alone is not enough to make Mr. Milosevic back down -- which might or might not be more likely now that he has been indicted as a war criminal by the international tribunal in The Hague -- only the United States could quickly fly in or provide the 100,000 or more troops required to drive Yugoslav forces out of Kosovo.
Europe's ability to move around huge numbers of troops is "deplorably slow," in the words of NATO's recently retired senior military officer, Gen. Klaus Naumann of Germany.
For now, the United States continues to dominate the skies over Kosovo, with up to 815 aircraft (including Apache anti-tank helicopters still waiting for combat and KC-135 refueling planes). All 18 other allies together provide 277, with more than half of those coming from France (102 Mirage, Jaguar, Super-Etendards and other planes), and Britain (38 Harrier GR-7 and Tornado GR-1 fighter-bombers and support aircraft).
ONE result is that Europeans are talking again, as they have been doing for years, about a need to fix the imbalance.
And as flaws in the American air-war strategy emerge, some Europeans are blaming its failures on the Clinton Administration's reluctance to expose air crews to danger. Most allied pilots fly only above 15,000 feet, beyond the reach of conventional Yugoslav anti-aircraft artillery. But they are far less effective against Serbian armor than they would be if they flew lower.
"Military pilots are paid to risk their lives, and most of ours feel frustrated a lot of the time," a French official said. "But the United States has a zero-deaths policy, and the United States is running the show."
A reluctance to take casualties is only one factor. The availability of an alternative is another. As General Naumann pointed out, most European countries lack the Americans' ability to launch laser- and satellite-guided bombs at a safe distance from ground fire. "Most European planes," he said, "have to fly more or less over the targets, which is the most stupid thing you can do, since you expose yourself to the enemy air defense."
Britain and France agreed late last year that one lesson of previous wars in the Balkans was that Europe needed to build up autonomous defense capabilities within the alliance. The day would come, the French argued, when the United States might tire of entanglements and the Europeans would have to act alone. Paradoxically, it is American involvement in Kosovo, rather than withdrawal, that has revived the issue now.
But in order to achieve real change, Europeans would have to spend billions of dollars to catch up with the technological revolution that the American military achieved in the 1970's and 1980's.
The United States, General Naumann said, spends $36 billion a year on defense research and development, while all the European allies and Canada together have been spending only $10 billion. Last year, according to NATO figures, the United States spent the equivalent of 3.2 percent of its gross domestic product on defense, 27 percent of it on equipment and infrastructure; the European allies averaged 2.1 percent.
"You can't want a European military organization that can get along without the Americans and reduce military budgets," a retired French general, Pierre M. Gallois, said. And the United States is increasing military spending, while the Europeans are continually cutting back.
Instead of spending, they vow at NATO summit meetings like the one in Washington in April to build a stronger European security and defense identity within the alliance. They will probably make another one at a summit meeting of the European Union in Cologne at the end of this week, now that NATO has agreed to let European countries use NATO facilities for future operations that the United States doesn't join.
But, as General Naumann commented, these arrangements exist only on paper. "What we now need to see are deeds, and it is urgent that something is done," he said. Kosovo might finally make the allies actually do something.

HEADLINE: The China Agenda

 BODY:
Washington's battered relations with Beijing suffered serious new damage last week from the Cox committee report on Chinese theft of American nuclear and missile secrets. Working ties will have to be rebuilt, but on more realistic foundations than before. While it still makes sense to encourage China to open its economy and play a constructive role in international affairs, Washington must give clear priority to protecting American security.
The report showed that for the past 20 years Beijing has exploited its commercial and scientific exchanges with the United States -- and lax security at American nuclear labs -- to learn how to increase the power and range of its nuclear arsenal. During those same years, Washington unwisely relaxed controls over militarily sensitive exports to increase opportunities for commercial gain, a tendency that grew during the Clinton Administration. Congress and the White House must move quickly to restore better balance to American policy. 
 That does not require freezing discussions with China on every subject from trade to Kosovo, as some in Congress now propose. It is in America's interest to complete talks on Beijing's entry into the World Trade Organization as soon as possible. Meanwhile, China's favorable trade status under American law should be renewed for another year.
But changes are urgently needed in other areas to address the specific security problems uncovered by the Cox report. The Senate made a promising start on this last week. A bipartisan majority approved a useful package of measures introduced by Trent Lott, the Senate majority leader. These included increased Pentagon monitoring of satellite sales to China and a strengthened role for intelligence agencies in reviewing export licenses. The Senate package also provides for more rigorous security training at the nuclear labs, with the Federal Bureau of Investigation supervising employee background checks. It also requires the Central Intelligence Agency to report to Congress on foreign efforts to steal sensitive American technologies.
Satellites and their launch by Chinese rockets require particular scrutiny, since they incorporate technology that can be used in ballistic missiles. Advanced computer sales also need careful review because these powerful machines can be used to simulate missile trajectories and nuclear weapons tests. Ideally, all countries capable of exporting such militarily sensitive goods should adopt common restrictions. But absent that, the United States, the world leader in advanced technology, must tighten its own controls.
Nuclear lab security can be improved without compromising civil liberties or scapegoating Chinese-Americans. All lab employees doing sensitive weapons or computer work must be carefully vetted and supervised. Those known to have shared inappropriate information with foreign colleagues or to have copied restricted files onto insecure computers should be excluded from such work and denied top-secret clearances. Clearances should be administratively suspended whenever a lab employee comes under investigation.
Academic interchange, through publications and conferences, is essential to the labs' scientific work. Additionally, exchange visits by foreign scientists, including those from Russia and China, are essential to arms control programs that strengthen American security. But all exchanges must be carefully planned and monitored to avoid leakage of military secrets.
Clearer lines of jurisdictional authority should be established among Government agencies responsible for nuclear and computer security. Top Justice Department officials need to be made aware of and take charge of suspected cases of nuclear espionage. The F.B.I. and C.I.A. both need to develop greater expertise on Chinese methods. Continued engagement with China is a necessity. But it can be pursued without carelessly exposing American military secrets.

HEADLINE: Editorial Observer;
Where Will Hillary Clinton Love New York?

BYLINE: By ELEANOR RANDOLPH 

 BODY:
Bill and Hillary Clinton are said to be planning a vacation in New York this summer. The First Lady needs to learn more about the state she might like to represent in the Senate, but their problem is where exactly to go.
Most Americans put a high priority on beaches or battlefields, canyons, mountains or theme parks. Mrs. Clinton would presumably prefer to spend her time with voters and donors. She will also need enough scenery for photo opportunities -- the kind of exquisite backdrop for a commercial that brings out the healthiest fresh-air look in even the wonkiest of indoor politicians. Unfortunately, many of the state's most scenic spots have already been used by Gov. George Pataki. 
 The Clintons' analyzers are said to be looking at the electoral possibilities of Shelter Island, the Finger Lakes and the Adirondacks. Shelter Island has a few advantages. It is close to Steven Spielberg's place in East Hampton, and some of the nation's biggest campaign contributors are a short ferry ride away. Also, Shelter Island would not be considered too fancy because, technically, it is not a Hampton. In fact, some people on Shelter Island have been hunkering down for years, hoping that nobody from the Hamptons looks north and discovers them. Since it would be hard for the locals to remain invisible with the Clintons there and since Shelter Island has a particularly hazardous colony of journalists, the couple might prefer more remote territory.
The Finger Lakes between Syracuse and Rochester would be an easy choice, with the universities and the home of Susan B. Anthony. But Adirondack State Park offers a challenge -- six million acres worth of everything from wilderness to water parks. The real problem is that the region is so heavily Republican that a local Democrat usually tries to keep such tendencies hidden from the neighbors.
Republican roots go deep in this wild territory. Teddy Roosevelt started coming to the Adirondacks as a boy. Calvin Coolidge summered near Saranac Lake. One Democratic President, Grover Cleveland, apparently visited sometime in the previous century. A fisherman who weighed in at 300 pounds, Cleveland was undoubtedly a challenge to the watercraft of the day.
As for donors, most of the old rich -- like the Rockefellers, Vanderbilts and Morgans, who owned "camps" the size of Blenheim -- are gone. The next moneyed generation has arrived, discovering anew the incredible beauty and discomfort of an Adirondack twig chair. So far, however, there seems to be some reluctance about hosting an outgoing President and his up-and-coming wife.
Gary Heurich, a Washington, D.C., brewer who owns one of the park's splendid private properties, has sent word that the Clintons are welcome to visit. Mr. Heurich, however, warns that he is not a Democrat but an independent, which, he says, "may be as close as they'll get."
Mr. Heurich's camp, whittled down to a few hundred acres after the family gave a few thousand acres to the state as forest preserve, has three houses, including a renovated lighthouse. There is also a helipad and a mile of shoreline on Lake Champlain.
Among the attractions of the area are golf courses and such scenic spots as the place where Samuel de Champlain shot two Iroquois chiefs with his harquebus (gun) and started the French and Indian War. Birders bring their binoculars for over 100 species of birds. Nearby are also the beaches where young Canadian humans come south each August to swim in the nude.
If such amusements do not appeal to the First Lady, the park is full of serious issues as well. Adirondack lifers can talk for days about acid rain, motor-boat hazards, seasonal economics, prisons or wildlife, particularly the common loon. Whether they will vote for her, even if they find her surprisingly charming, is another matter.
Finally, the Adirondacks include a number of places that are great but not so grand, like the western hills where my family celebrates summer. For us, a star-studded evening means a chair on the nearest dock. Gatherings feature bring-your-own casseroles and beer, and everybody smells like a bath oil known for offending not merely humans but also bugs. It's a lovely place to August, but socially, at least, we are a long way from the Hamptons and a lot closer to rural Arkansas.

HEADLINE: PERSONAL BUSINESS: DIARY;
Priced Right Out Of the Mailbox?

BYLINE: By DAVID CAY JOHNSTON 

 BODY:
Ever wondered how the banks that inundate Americans with credit card offers can afford all those come-on letters and tempting introductory rates? Perhaps some of them can't. The mailbox of the future may still groan under the weight of credit card solicitations, but they will come from a smaller number of banks, Moody's Investors Service says.
Just as big airlines can drive new carriers out of business by temporarily lowering prices, bigger lenders can do the same to smaller banks by temporarily lowering the price of borrowing money, and that is what they are doing with credit cards. 
 "Cutthroat competition between issuers remains the rule," said David Fanger of Moody's, a credit rating agency, "while the prevalence of teaser rates continues to encourage consumers to surf" for new cards. All of that shaves profit margins and gives an advantage to the big lenders that can afford to stay longer in the game.
The cost of just signing up a new customer now ranges from $90 to $110, double the cost six years ago, mostly because of the expense of mass mailing, according to The Nilson Report, a credit card industry newsletter in Oxnard, Calif. That may decline as issuers turn to the Internet: "Acquiring a new customer over the Internet may be only half as expensive," said David Robertson, the president of Nilson. But smaller issuers who cannot match the low-rate offers from big lenders may not be in the game long enough to realize the Internet savings. DAVID CAY JOHNSTON

HEADLINE: DATABANK: MAY 24-28;
Time for Batman? Week of Wild Swings

BYLINE: By MICKEY MEECE 

 BODY:
It was like trading inside a comic strip. Bam! Pow! Kerplunk. Investors dodged and volleyed the best they could in a week marked by four consecutive swings of 100 points or more in the Dow Jones industrial average.
 The Dow fell 2.5 percent for the week, to 10,559.74, down 5 percent from its most recent high. Nasdaq slipped 2 percent, to 2,470.52, off 9 percent from its recent peak. And the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index dropped 2.1 percent, to 1,301.84, down 5 percent from its recent record. 
 And now that Nasdaq and the New York Stock Exchange want to extend trading hours, will investors be up to the challenge?
 The week's volatility was powered by recurring fears that any hint of inflation would cause the Federal Reserve to raise rates. Those worries were tempered on Friday by a decline in the Purchasing Management Association of Chicago business activity index.
 Alan Ackerman, senior vice president at Fahnestock & Company, told The Associated Press on Friday, We still are in a tug-of-war between those people who think interest rates will go higher and those that don't.
 Markets in Europe, Japan and Hong Kong fell for the week. MICKEY MEECE

HEADLINE: Passing the Endurance Test;
The Anatomy of Discretion, Or, How to Fit Brassieres

BYLINE: By ANDREA KANNAPELL 

 BODY:
Deborah Sherman remembers what it was like. "I used to go with my mother in the Bronx," she recalled the other day, with not much nostalgia. "At the store, these huge old ladies would mrrff, rrrmm" -- her hands smushed the air in front of her chest into imaginary A-cups. "And you're just dying, but they don't care."
Dr. Sherman, now a gastroenterologist, found a better way to get undergarments that do what they're supposed to. She is one member of an uncounted, savvy sisterhood that depends on the brassiere fitters at the Town Shop, at Broadway and 80th Street on the Upper West Side. 
 Bra fitting, once routine at finer boutiques and neighborhood department stores, is almost unheard of these days. Some smaller lingerie stores have clerks who will peek into the dressing room and opine, and a few heavyweights -- notably Bergdorf Goodman and Saks -- have fitters provided by major lingerie makers. But Lord & Taylor, Barneys and Bloomingdale's rely on their staff members, and Macy's depends on a tape measure in the rear of the lingerie section. Bendel and Takashimaya don't even carry bras.
At the Town Shop, a small, old-timey store with walls of pink drawers labeled with brand names and cup sizes, Eyvette Manigault, Margaret Croom and a handful of other women "size" fully clothed women with a glance, but they don't leave it there. They grab a handful of bras -- which cost no more than they would in a department store -- and escort the customer to one of five fitting rooms, helping her out of clothes and into undergarments with a matter-of-fact manner that dispels shyness.
The scrutiny is keen -- and not knee-jerk flattering -- when the bras go on. The point, Ms. Manigault said, is to fit the cups to the sideward expanse of the breast.
"Some women go their whole lives without knowing their true size" is a truism often repeated by the shop's fitters, owners and customers, most of whom discuss the finer points of the subject with their hands gently cupped upward and vaguely bouncing in front of their torsos.
A rather handsome, nearly doe-eyed man often takes the command position behind the counter. He is Daniel Koch, 40, and he is following in his father's, grandmother's and great-grandfather's footsteps. "It doesn't faze me," he said. "I know what we sell, and I also know that just about every woman in America is wearing a bra."
Last week, he found himself faced with a client with her arms folded across her chest who said: "I wonder if you have strapless minimizer bras? Maybe a 34C?" He pulled out a drawer full of strapless minimizer 34C's, and they had a discreet conversation about materials and comfort. His eyes never appeared to dip below her neck, but when she disappeared into a fitting room with Ms. Maginault, he said, "She probably should be wearing a 32D, but I don't say that because I don't want them to know I'm looking."
Mr. Koch's grandmother, Selma Koch, a few weeks shy of her 92d birthday, is the Town Shop's C.E.O. (chief everything officer). She has kept the business running through Depression, recession and inflation, but today's challenges worry her. Costs keep increasing. The monthly rent rose to $10,000 from $3,000 a few years ago, when a new landlord took over, she said, and it's now up to $18,000.
"I am serving four generations," she said. "I get worried; these women come in, they're all gray, they can't walk, and they say: you fitted me for my trousseau!"
As if to prove Mrs. Koch's point, Dr. Sherman pointed out Ms. Croom and said: "I think she was the one who fitted me for my wedding and honeymoon 10 years ago. And she made me get all these things that I never would have gotten -- like a leopard bikini." Ms. Croom, overhearing, smiled. "It was nice though," she said, "wasn't it?"
 ANDREA KANNAPELL 
http://www.nytimes.com

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Nationalist Group Fights for Identity in South Africa

BYLINE: By DONALD G. McNEIL Jr. 

DATELINE: JOHANNESBURG, May 29

 BODY:
The party whose slogan used to be "one settler, one bullet" is trying to stay in office by appealing to the nostalgia vote.
The Pan Africanist Congress now preaches "non-racialism" and says it welcomes anyone who considers himself an African first, including white Afrikaners. 
 But it insists it is the true torchbearer of the ideals upon which the African National Congress was founded in 1912. The A.N.C., it argues, went off track in 1959 when it embraced the Communist Party, Indians and mixed-race voters, thus diluting what was then supposed to be a black nationalist struggle.
Its leader, the Rev. Stanley Mogoba, reminds voters that, like President Nelson Mandela, he too was imprisoned on Robben Island.
In its early days, the party was a serious challenge to the A.N.C. for leadership of the struggle. It led the 1960 Sharpeville demonstration that ended in the massacre of 69 protesters by the police. It too, had a guerrilla army in exile. It tended to hit soft targets like bars; in 1993, its partisans stabbed to death Amy Biehl, a California college student dropping friends off in a black township.
Having formally abandoned violence, the party still seems stranded on the banks of the river of history. Pollsters say it will get only 1 percent of the vote on Wednesday. It got 1.2 percent in 1994. Since then, its five members in the National Assembly did not really make names for themselves by taking the Government to task as, for example, the seven members from the Democratic Party did.
Mr. Mogoba, a Methodist Bishop, did bring the party back into the headlines recently by publicly musing that murderers and rapists should have their offending parts amputated. He said the party had not "worked out the details," such as whether the chopping would be done in an operating theater or a public square.
"We don't say we'd like to cut people's limbs off," he said. "But rather than allow criminals to cut throats, to rape, we'd contemplate it."
He argues that the present Government is soft on crime and soft on corruption, has let moral standards decline so children no longer defer to their elders, and has developed a "trickle down" economic policy while he prefers a focus on the poorest of the poor. He also favors aggressive redistribution of white-owned land.
He and his secretary general, Ngila Michael Muendane, reject their lowly 1 percent poll rating, saying some poll sponsors are controlled by the A.N.C. They accuse the national television network of giving them short shrift on the evening news. A.N.C. members tear down their posters and even threaten gullible farm workers, Mr. Muendane said, "by saying they can see their vote, and if they vote P.A.C., they'll lose their housing or their pension."
In its literature, the party assures voters that "the P.A.C. has a plan" for every ill of the country: crime, disastrous public schools, overwhelmed hospitals, the housing shortage. The plans apparently involve "investing massive resources," although it is not clear where the money would come from.
Its literature contains a seven-stanza poem honoring the struggle of the homeless, the jobless, the worker, the student, the woman and the aged. One verse:
You can't earn a cent
And all your money's spent
So you think about crime
But why do time?
For you the jobless,
The struggle continues.
There is an odd echo to the party's platform. With its emphasis on renewing morality and zero tolerance for crime, it sounds like the other angry opposition parties, including even the National Party, the latest incarnation of the party that instituted apartheid but which now also proclaims itself multiracial and would probably back all the P.A.C. goals except farmland redistribution and aggressive affirmative action.
The opposition is fragmented and the A.N.C. is expected to sweep more than 60 percent of the vote.

HEADLINE: North Korea Unresponsive In U.S. Talks, Envoy Reports

BYLINE: By NICHOLAS D. KRISTOF 

DATELINE: TOKYO, May 29

 BODY:
Former Defense Secretary William S. Perry said today that during his just-completed trip to North Korea, he had "clearly and firmly" expressed concern about North Korean military programs, but that he did not get any definitive response.
Mr. Perry spoke in Seoul, South Korea, after concluding a four-day visit to North Korea, where he apparently outlined a Clinton Administration proposal to offer North Korea a major expansion of economic and diplomatic ties in exchange for the North curbing its nuclear and missile programs. 
 If accepted, the arrangement could eventually lead to a far-reaching relaxation of tensions in northeast Asia, but there is widespread skepticism that the deal will work smoothly, if at all.
One inauspicious sign was that the North Korean leader, Kim Jong Il, declined to meet with Mr. Perry, despite American hopes for such a meeting.
Mr. Kim almost never meets foreigners, so it was not a great surprise, but it reflected a lost opportunity to signal a new start in relations.
Mr. Perry did pass on to North Korean officials a message to Mr. Kim from President Clinton, as well as from South Korean and Japanese leaders.
Although former President Carter visited North Korea in 1994 on his own, Mr. Perry as a presidential envoy was heading the highest-ranking delegation to North Korea since the Korean War, yet his North Korean counterparts were mostly at the vice minister level.
Mr. Kim said in Seoul that he had spoken to officials close to Mr. Kim and had tried to convey to them "clearly and firmly U.S. and allied views and concerns," The Associated Press reported.
But he said "it will take some time" for each side to reflect on the visit and decide what to do next.
From Seoul, Mr. Perry flies back to Washington to brief Mr. Clinton and to put the finishing touches on his review of policy toward North Korea.
The aim of the review is to achieve a more bipartisan policy, with backing from Congress, dealing more consistently with North Korea and seeking a way out of the existing stalemate.
One underlying problem in achieving any breakthrough in relations is the deep distrust with which each side views the other.
Many Americans, including a number in the Congress, see North Korea as a Communist menace that does not keep its bargains and is starving its own population, yet has somehow become the largest recipient of American aid in East Asia.
North Koreans argue, however, that they have kept their side of the landmark 1994 agreement to improve relations, and that it is the United States that has backed out of its promises to lift economic sanctions and normalize ties.

HEADLINE: U.S. May Try To Stop Loan Seen as Bad For Tibetans

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS 

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, May 28

 BODY:
The United States appears to be heading for another clash with China, this time over a divisive World Bank development loan that would help Chinese authorities resettle tens of thousands of Chinese in regions traditionally inhabited by Tibetans and Mongolians.
The Clinton Administration seems to be getting ready to oppose the plan when it comes before the World Bank's executive board for a final approval next month, though White House officials say no final decision has yet been made. 
 Private pro-Tibetan groups are lobbying against the project, which would spend $160 million to resettle 60,000 poor farmers from eroded hillsides in China's western Qinghai province to better lands about 300 miles farther west.
China and the World Bank see the plan as part of a campaign to alleviate rural poverty in remote areas by offering farmers the chance to make a better living and relieving population pressures in vacated areas.
But critics argue that moving these farmers, who are mainly Han Chinese and members of the minority Hui, Tu and Salar ethnic groups, will further erode the position of the Tibetan inhabitants of Dulan County in Haixi Prefecture, where the farmers are to be resettled and which is a designated Tibetan and Mongolian Autonomous Area. Tibetans are already in a minority there.
Qinghai province is outside the borders of the Tibetan region China annexed in 1959, but has traditionally been strongly ethnically Tibetan and is the birthplace of the Dalai Lama, the Tibetan spiritual leader.
Although Qinghai has been a province of China since the 17th century, critics see the proposed resettlements as part of a broader campaign by the Chinese authorities to weaken Tibetan national identity.
Asked about the project during an appearance before the House Banking Committee last week, Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin said, "We are enormously concerned about it," adding that the United States is "inclined to oppose it."
John Ackerly of the International Campaign for Tibet, a Washington-based organization fighting for Tibetan rights, said: "This relocation, which the World Bank now proposes to help finance, is part of a larger Chinese policy which is now the greatest threat to the continued existence of the Tibetans as a distinct people and culture."
Critics say the plan violates World Bank guidelines, which state that indigenous ethnic minorities should "not suffer adverse effects from bank-financed projects and that they receive culturally compatible social and economic benefits."
"The proposed resettlement will make the indigenous Tibetans even more marginal than they are already," said Kate Saunders of the London-based Tibet Information Network, which first highlighted the dangers of the project.
Critics also argue that the scheme is part of a broader plan to establish the infrastructure needed to exploit the region's mineral resources, which would inevitably attract additional inflows of non-Tibetans.
For the moment, the World Bank is standing behind the project. In a briefing paper issued this week it defends the resettlement plan as an integral part of its efforts to reduce poverty in China, which has cut the number of rural poor from 280 million to 80 million over a decade.
The farmers to be resettled, it argues, are "among the poorest people in the world, with incomes of about $60 a year." But it admits that the resettlement would weaken the position of indigenous Tibetans and Mongolians in the area.
In Haixi Prefecture, the Tibetan percentage of the population would fall from 11.1 percent to 10.3 percent, while in Dulan County it would decline from 22.7 percent to 14 percent. For Mongolians the declines are from 7.6 percent to 6.5 percent and from 14.1 percent to 6.7 percent.
The number of Han Chinese in Haixi Prefecture would rise from 236,918 to 261,375 and in Dulan County from 27,977 to 52,434.

HEADLINE: Black Groups in Florida Split Over School Voucher Plan

BYLINE: By STEVEN A. HOLMES 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 29

 BODY:
Exposing the sharp debate over school vouchers among black people, two long-time allies from the civil rights struggle in Florida find themselves on opposite sides on whether the state should help pay for some children to attend private schools.
The plan, passed last month by the Florida Legislature and awaiting the expected signature of Gov. Jeb Bush, would permit students from the lowest-rated public schools in the state to attend private schools, including religious ones, with vouchers paid by taxpayers' money. Florida would become the first state to have a statewide school voucher program. 
 The National Association for the Advancement of Colored People and several other liberal groups have vowed to sue to block the voucher plan, contending that it would reduce support for public education and violate the constitutional separation of church and state.
In response, the Urban League of Greater Miami, breaking with its national headquarters, has retained a Washington law firm to intervene in court on the state's side in defense of the program.
"Isn't that healthy?" said T. Willard Fair, president of the Miami Urban League, referring to the differences of opinions among civil rights leaders. "It shows that we are now getting to the point where we can behave like human beings. We can like and dislike. We can select and reject. We are not bound by color the way we used to be bound, where people said, 'You can't think that way because you're black.' "
Most prominent civil rights leaders and organizations, including the National Urban League and its president, Hugh Price, remain firmly opposed to school vouchers.
The organization's New York headquarters issued a statement in the name of Mr. Price, who was on vacation.
"Our Urban League of Greater Miami affiliate must do what it feels is best for the communities it serves," the statement said, "and the National Urban League will continue to do what we feel is best for our constituents."
Unlike the N.A.A.C.P., which insists that its local chapters hew to a policy line worked out at its convention or by its board, the National Urban League is much more decentralized. Local affiliates have their own boards, raise most of their own money and can take independent positions.
While describing Mr. Fair as "one of the most successful Urban League directors in the country," Leon Russell, president of the Florida Conference of N.A.A.C.P. Chapters, sharply disagreed with his stand on school vouchers. Mr. Russell and other critics assert that vouchers would damage the public schools by siphoning off the best students.
"He might be correct in terms of wanting to do something to improve public education," Mr. Russell said. "But I think his concept of how to do it is all wrong."
The split between the two civil rights groups in Florida mirrors the divisions among black people over the voucher issue. While liberal civil rights groups generally oppose school vouchers, polls show that many low-income African-Americans, especially those with children in poor-performing public schools, support the concept. Conversely, more affluent and college educated black people are much more wary of such programs.
A 1998 survey by the Joint Center for Political and Economic Studies, a research organization specializing in issues involving black people, found that 48.1 percent of African-Americans favored allowing state money to be used for private school tuition, while 39.8 percent opposed it. The poll also indicated that whites opposed such vouchers by a margin of 50.2 percent to 41.3 percent.
Mr. Fair is a strong ally of Mr. Bush and, like the Republican Governor, an enthusiastic advocate of using changes in education policy to put pressure on public schools to improve. These include the voucher plan and charter schools, which are public schools licensed by local school boards to pursue innovative teaching methods. In 1996, Mr. Fair and Mr. Bush founded the Liberty City Charter School in a poor black neighborhood in Miami.
"If we plan to make the system do things it's never done before," Mr. Fair said, "we have to be prepared to do things that we've never done before."
The voucher program, if signed by Governor Bush, is expected to go into effect in September. The plan calls for all public schools to be graded A to F, based on statewide standardized test scores, attendance, disciplinary actions taken against students and graduation rates. All students in public schools that received a grade of F in two out of four years would be eligible to receive a voucher worth about $4,000 to pay tuition at private school.
Mr. Fair's group has also angered some liberal civil rights groups by retaining the Institute for Justice, a conservative legal foundation, to represent it in any litigation involving the voucher program. The institute has been an outspoken critic of affirmative action programs.
Clint Bolick, the institute's director of litigation, said he had approached Mr. Fair's group two weeks ago and persuaded it to join in the defense of the school voucher program if the N.A.A.C.P. carried through its threat to file suit. Mr. Bolick said he wanted to insure that any judge hearing the case did not have the impression that black opinion was "monolithic" when it came to school vouchers.
"If the first name you see on a lawsuit is the N.A.A.C.P.," he said, "that unquestionably creates an impression that the black community is on one side of that lawsuit. That image in this case would be demonstrably false. Having the Urban League on the other side evens the equation."

SECTION: Section 1; Page 14; Column 1; National Desk 

LENGTH: 1629 words

HEADLINE: As Agriculture Struggles, Iowa Psychologist Helps His Fellow Farmers Cope

BYLINE: By DIRK JOHNSON 

DATELINE: HARLAN, Iowa

 BODY:
After hauling oats to his cattle before sunrise on the Iowa prairie, Michael Rosmann drives to an office and sits down with farmer after farmer to listen to their troubles. A fourth-generation farmer as well as a clinical psychologist, he speaks the language of men and women on the verge of losing their place on the land.
"These are good people who feel like terrible failures," said Dr. Rosmann, who knows what it is to endure sleepless nights spent worrying about crop prices and peril to family tradition. "Somebody needs to tell them it's not their fault." 
 Dr. Rosmann, a big man in a leather vest and cowboy boots who lost part of a foot in a farm accident, sees most of his patients at his office, off the town square in this small western Iowa town. But he will counsel wherever the need takes him, practicing psychotherapy while standing in a cornfield or leaning against a tractor tire and talking to a farmer in bib overalls.
"Last week I took a guy fishing," Dr. Rosmann said. "He's so down he had stopped talking. So we sat. And we fished."
A quiet desperation shrouds the countryside in places like Harlan, where plunging corn, bean and livestock prices are bringing ruin to farms nurtured by families for generations. But if troubles are in plentiful supply in much of rural America, psychological help like Dr. Rosmann's most often is not.
So, with the farm economy lurching toward the dreadful conditions of the early 1980's, when suicidal gunshots rang out from red barns, rural social and church groups are trying to extend solace to men and women on the verge of breakdown, both financial and emotional.
Dr. Rosmann himself recently helped organize the Sharing Help Awareness United Network, or Shaun, named for Shaun Petersen, a 13-year-old Iowa boy killed in a farm accident in 1989. The nonprofit group promises to find counseling for farm families that need it, regardless of their ability to pay.
In rural Adair County, east of here, a group of scholars, social workers and clerics gathered the other day for an Ecumenical Retreat on Family Farm Stress, where they mapped mental health strategies to help people faced with financial ruin.
In Nebraska, a series of workshops called Working With Distressed Farm and Ranch Families, for clerics, families and social workers, is set for July 6-9 in Lincoln, Kearney, Omaha and South Sioux City.
And in the rural communities of eastern Washington, social workers have been counseling farmers' children and checking for signs of strain.
"When you're a farmer, your work is your home, and there is no escape from the suffering," said Fred Moskol, a researcher in rural mental health at the University of Wisconsin Medical School. "And the poor kids, they're watching it all."
The root of the suffering is a market that has sent the prices of crops like corn and soybeans to 12-year lows. Livestock, too, has been hit hard. At the end of last year, hogs were selling for as little as $9 per hundredweight, barely a fourth of what a pork farmer with little debt would need to break even; hog prices have since rebounded to $35 or $40 per hundredweight, but that is only half their mid-1990's highs. The United States Department of Agriculture reports that average farm income dropped 33 percent from 1996 to 1998 and that an additional dip of 10 percent is expected for this year.
Without psychological help, says Larry Barber, a rawboned 57-year-old patient of Dr. Rosmann who recently lost a farm that had been in the family for more than a century, he would have lost his mind as well. For patients who can afford it, Dr. Rosmann charges $100 an hour. But he has charged some distressed farmers as little as $20 for a two-hour session. And others he has not sent a bill at all.
A week before Mr. Barber was forced to sell his cattle, he sat and cried, tears streaming down his face, making no sound, his body shuddering.
"I lost all desire to do anything," Mr. Barber said, tears now again in his eyes as he drew hard on a cigarette. "This is like a death, you know."
Riding the agricultural economy's roller coaster, farmers like Mr. Barber saw record prices for their crops just three years ago, before the Asian economic crisis stifled demand for American agricultural products.
Mark Drabenstott, a vice president of the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City, said: "It's a classic tale of supply and demand. When prices hit records in 1996, farmers responded by bringing more product to market. Just then their best customer, Asia, starts going into terrible financial problems."
Mr. Drabenstott said that while the American farm economy was now "clearly in the throes of a serious downturn," the severity was not yet that of the 1980's, when interest rates, always a crucial index to a way of life that runs on borrowed money, soared to nearly 20 percent and land prices plunged, leaving many farmers without sufficient collateral to back their loans. The value of farm acreage has lately been holding steady, Mr. Drabenstott said, and interest rates have been relatively low.
A steep drop in income has nonetheless meant disaster to farmers already working on slim margins.
Besides the low commodities prices, Mr. Drabenstott said, American agriculture is seeing "substantial structural change," with more farmers working under contract with big processing companies, especially in the hog business. Since the processors find it most efficient to deal with large farms, the smaller, family farms, particularly those with big debt loads, have been the ones most often squeezed out.
While farmers like Mr. Barber and his wife, Linda, may understand that it is forces beyond their control that are driving them out of business, that knowledge does little to relieve a certain guilt.
"People tell me, 'It's not your fault,' " Mr. Barber said, "but it is a little like driving a car, then skidding on a bad patch of ice and hitting a child. It wouldn't be your fault, but you'd live every day with the doubts and thoughts of how could I have avoided this terrible thing."
Farmers tend to be an independent breed, of course, and many are reluctant to ask for psychological help, even where its shortage in rural areas does not place it out of reach.
"In a small town," said Mr. Moskol, the University of Wisconsin researcher, "you walk into the mental health center and everybody sees you do it."
But for the most part, Dr. Rosmann said, farmers understand the benefits of psychotherapy and drugs in treating depression and anxiety.
"These are smart people," he said. "They're stubborn, but they're also innovative. They've got to be. A good manager will try anything that helps the operation. If that means going to a psychologist, then fine."
The big problem, he said, is making the time out of a busy schedule, especially if you are forced to drive 50 miles or more to find a counselor.
His caseload growing with the worsening of the farm economy, Dr. Rosmann rented out his corn and bean acreage this year so that he could devote more time to his patients. He says farmers appreciate that he can talk about the ins and outs of agricultural work as well as the emotional pressures. Other psychologists, he notes with a smile, might be at a loss should the discussion turn to crop rotation.
Dr. Rosmann, a native of Harlan who taught psychology at the University of Virginia before returning here to farm, says the suicide to which financial pressures sometimes drive farmers tends to differ from suicide among city dwellers, or even among rural people in other occupations.
For one thing, he said, farmers who try suicide are far more likely to achieve it. While suicide attempts among nonfarmers often occur after a Friday or Saturday night drinking bout, farmers are more likely to be sober and deliberative, taking their lives on a Sunday morning when their wives are away at church.
"And then we have a lot of 'accidents' on the farm," Dr. Rosmann said, referring to those cases that could be suicide and presumably are. "They want to make sure the insurance will be paid to their families."
Mr. Barber, who is now on antidepressants, said he would never have taken his own life. But his wife believes he would have been driven to the grave by stroke or heart attack had it not been for Dr. Rosmann.
Mr. Barber still struggles terribly with the changes in his life, swallowing hard when he is asked about his new job in a lumberyard. His wife was once active in volunteer work, winning awards for her efforts on behalf of shelters for battered women and in helping the poor with their taxes. Now she is looking for a job, as a receptionist or a secretary.
"I also go to Dr. Rosmann," Mrs. Barber said. "There are a lot of things I need to work through."
When they farmed, Mrs. Barber liked to do the disking in the fields. She would ride her tractor and talk to her husband on a walkie-talkie as he rode a combine.
"Waving to each other across the field," Mrs. Barber said, her voice breaking, "I miss that so much."
The old Barber farm is a pretty place, with bur oaks, ponds and rolling prairies. The Barbers were able to hold on to their modest house there, and these days they often sit at the kitchen table, looking out at the countryside when they are not just staring into their coffee.
Simply getting from one day to the next has been such a struggle, they said apologetically on one recent evening, that the housekeeping has suffered a bit lately. A stack of newspapers, magazines and files leaned against a computer in the kitchen, and in the living room stood an artificial Christmas tree, still wrapped in tinsel.
"A Christmas tree in May -- it doesn't belong," Mr. Barber said. "That's how I am, I guess. Just out of place."
