HEADLINE: Testing Welfare Applicants for Drugs

BYLINE: By ROBYN MEREDITH 

DATELINE: DETROIT, May 29

 BODY:
In a controversial and unusual effort to move more welfare recipients into the work force, Michigan plans a pilot program that would require thousands of those applying for aid to take drug tests to qualify for benefits.
Starting in October, Michigan welfare applicants under 65 in three locations yet to be chosen will be required to take drug tests or forfeit their benefits. People already receiving benefits at those locations would be randomly tested. 
 Those who test positive for illegal drugs would be required to get treatment to collect welfare money, and those who refuse treatment would be dropped from the welfare rolls.
Against a tide of opposition from civil libertarians and welfare experts and mixed reaction from welfare recipients themselves, Gov. John Engler explained that the program would help those who needed drug treatment to get it and to obtain jobs.
"The idea is that workplace testing today is so common," Mr. Engler said. "This is a tool that will help identify some of the problems that need to be treated."
Michigan was one of the early leaders nationwide in the effort to end the decadeslong system of guaranteed welfare benefits and to replace it with time limits and other changes intended to force those on welfare to work.
Since October 1992, when Michigan's welfare changes began, the state's caseload has dropped about 60 percent, to 89,866 families from 225,359, the 14th largest drop among states, according to Federal statistics.
With many of those most able to find jobs already working, Michigan is trying a new approach on welfare. The mandatory drug tests, which other states have considered but rejected, are just one indication of its new direction.
Other initiatives include a plan to reduce fraud by taking computerized fingerprint images of those applying for welfare. And the welfare agency plans to expand its "Project Zero" program to 25 additional welfare offices around the state, bringing to 60 the number of locations offering flexible, expanded social services, like child care and transportation, with a goal of reducing welfare cases in a given area to zero.
But of all the initiatives, mandatory drug testing has raised the most concern, especially among civil libertarians, social workers and welfare experts.
The Michigan chapter of the American Civil Liberties Union is considering suing the state to challenge the program on constitutional grounds, arguing that the mandatory drug test violates the Fourth Amendment prohibition against unreasonable search and seizure.
"The state is starting from the assumption that the poor are criminals," said Kary L. Moss, executive director of the A.C.L.U. of Michigan. "The state is saying that if you want money for food and shelter you have to give up the Fourth Amendment rights that others have."
Social workers are concerned that the new program could have unintended consequences.
"It is very demeaning," said Sharon Parks, senior research associate at the Michigan League for Human Services, a nonprofit organization. "It might have a real chilling effect on people even coming in and applying."
Welfare experts object to the program and say it has a number of shortcomings.
"Michigan is the only state in the country that is doing this," said Gwen Rubinstein, director of policy research at the Legal Action Center, a nonprofit organization based in New York that focuses on alcohol and drug policy and tracks drug testing efforts nationwide. "Drug testing often sounds on the surface like a good idea, but it has so many flaws."
First, Ms. Rubinstein said, drug testing will not turn up those with alcohol abuse problems, which are more prevalent among welfare recipients. The tests are intrusive, she said, and casual drug users who are able to work will test positive along with addicts who need treatment.
Because testing is also expensive, it can divert money that could otherwise be used for drug treatment and other social services, Ms. Rubinstein said.
Sheldon Danziger, an economist and professor of social work and public policy at the University of Michigan who conducted a study of Michigan welfare recipients, said money used to test for drug use could be put to better use screening for mental health problems that needed treatment.
"A much greater percentage of women have mental health problems like depression, anxiety disorder and post-traumatic stress disorder than have substance abuse problems," Professor Danziger said.
But Douglas E. Howard, the new director of the state welfare agency, defended his plans.
"Michigan's approach is very unique," Mr. Howard said. "It is not just testing; it is testing followed by treatment."
He said the falling welfare caseload in the state dictated a change in direction.
"We're in an economy now where job placement isn't as tough as it was 10 years ago," he said. "The challenge is in job retention."
The state plans to select the three pilot sites in June, with one likely to be in Detroit, one in another large city and one in a rural area, Mr. Howard said. Drug treatment availability varies by region, and the sites chosen will be those with adequate treatment centers.
"What we are trying to do is remove barriers to work," he said.
It is unclear how much the testing and treatment programs will cost because neither the sites nor the testing material -- urine, sweat or hair follicles -- have been decided on. Most drug tests cost about $40 a person, Mr. Howard said, but he was not sure how many people would fall under the jurisdiction of the three locations.
Some welfare recipients here vehemently oppose his plans, and others applaud them.
"It definitely would be humiliating to me," said Barbara L. Whitfield, 61, who has been raising four grandchildren since her daughter died nine years ago. "They should find a better way to do that, a more dignified way."
Others supported the plan. "I think it is a good idea," said Talonda D. Loggins, 25, a mother of three who works as a nurse's assistant when she can find good child care and collects welfare when she cannot.
"Some recipients are not using their money the way it is supposed to be used," Ms. Loggins said, adding that the testing would reduce recipients' use of drugs.
Other states have considered similar drug testing programs but have backed away.
Last year, Florida set up a pilot program in two regions to detect drug-abusing welfare recipients. It asks drug-use questions of all applicants, but uses physical drug tests only on those it has reasonable cause to believe use drugs.
Louisiana passed a law in 1997 requiring drug testing for welfare recipients and certain public employees. But after a task force was set up to carry out the law, the state decided instead to require applicants to answer a questionnaire about drug use. Answers to the questions determine whether a drug test is administered.
New York and Maryland planned to require drug tests but found that other types of screening like questionnaires were cheaper and more effective. Four other states -- New Jersey, Minnesota, South Carolina and Wisconsin -- randomly test welfare recipients with felony drug convictions.

New Era of Civilian Rule Begins in Nigeria
Nigeria's military ruler turned over power to an elected leader, opening an era of civilian government in the nation where one in six Africans live. Seconds after he was handed a copy of Nigeria's 24-day-old Constitution and 20 years after he stepped down as its military ruler, Olusegun Obasanjo, a retired general, was sworn in as President. 1
 
Open Elections in Indonesia
The first freely contested general election in more than 40 years is being held in Indonesia. The election will complete the transition of power that began when President Suharto resigned one year ago, following riots and student demonstrations. 3

Prime Minister Atal Behari Vajpayee said that India would continue air strikes against what he described as Pakistani-backed infiltrators dug into Indian territory, and would only be willing to hold talks with Pakistan about the conflict if they were not predicated on a halt to the air raids. 3

Robert Hughes, the Australian art critic and author, was in serious but stable condition in a Perth hospital after being badly injured in a car crash in Australia's remote northwest, officials said. Mr. Hughes suffered severe chest injuries and "broken bones all over his body" when his car collided head-on with another vehicle Friday night near the northern city of Broome. (Reuters)

HEADLINE: A Year After Lilco Takeover, Power Use Surges but Critics Hang In

BYLINE: By BRUCE LAMBERT 

DATELINE: GARDEN CITY, N.Y., May 28

 BODY:
Ever since New York State took over Long Island's electricity system a year ago today and cut rates by an average of 20 percent, customers have reacted like bargain hunters at a sale. They are using more electricity than ever.
For two decades, power consumption was largely stagnant on the Island, as the Long Island Lighting Company pushed rates to the highest level in the contiguous United States. But since last year, power use has surged by nearly 5 percent. Nationwide growth averages 2 percent to 3 percent per year, according to the Edison Electric Institute, a trade group. 
 "The increase is a huge surprise," said Richard M. Kessel, chairman of the Long Island Power Authority, which took over the utility. "I have no doubt we'll hit another peak this summer."
Cheery optimism is new for a power company on Long Island, where for years the name Lilco was an epithet, a synonym for corporate arrogance and exorbitant prices. After Lilco disappeared in a complex takeover engineered by Gov. George E. Pataki and financed by a sale of $6.8 billion in municipal bonds, the biggest in national history, its successor, LIPA, began billing itself as "a change for the better."
But in the aftermath of the changeover, as new problems have arisen and old ones have persisted, die-hard critics have challenged that boast. They continue to attack the deal as a giveaway to Lilco stockholders, one that bailed out the company after its $5 billion fiasco, the never-opened nuclear power plant at Shoreham, and left consumers with a staggering debt. Even after being reduced, the new rates are still the nation's seventh highest. And critics say the rate cut is a deception made possible by a five-year delay in debt payments.
"I'm not ready to light a cake and sing 'Happy Birthday' to LIPA," said Gordian Raacke, director of the Citizens Advisory Panel, a court-appointed watchdog group. "We still have to pay back an unprecedented debt."
As could be expected, little naysaying was heard at LIPA's first-anniversary board meeting in Riverhead on Thursday, where a large poster read, "Promises made, promises kept."
"We lowered electric rates, just like we said we would," said Mr. Kessel, calling the rate cut the largest in the nation.
LIPA and its supporters can cite other good news: Agency revenue is stronger than expected. Customers are pumping nearly $500 million a year into the Island's economy using the savings on their electricity bills.
"The economy has never been better," said Matthew T. Crosson, president of the Long Island Association, the Island's largest economic development group. He said he had heard that one supermarket chain was saving $3 million a year, more than its usual profit.
And electricity service has been good. A State Public Service Commission study for 1998 showed that Long Island had the fewest and shortest power disruptions in the state. One exception was a power failure during a severe thunderstorm last summer that drew numerous complaints. And a snowstorm in March also caught LIPA by surprise. Mr. Kessel said LIPA was improving its response time.
And unexpectedly low interest on its bonds enabled LIPA to shrink the 30-year repayment period to 16 years, saving customers $4 billion. "We've begun to lift the burden of Shoreham debt from the backs of future generations," Mr. Kessel said. "Once we get rid of it, we can cut rates another 10 percent."
A spokesman for Mr. Pataki, Michael McKeon, said, "The Governor is extremely pleased."
But challenges remain. The higher demand for electricity poses one problem. "The downside is we're going to get into a tight situation on supply," Mr. Kessel said, "and we have to move quickly." To insure power for future generations, LIPA is scrambling to build generators and lay a $200 million cable underneath Long Island Sound to import electricity.
About 1,000 jobs have been eliminated as part of the merger of parts of Lilco with Brooklyn Union Gas to create Keyspan Energy. Its executives are contending with tensions between Keyspan's two halves while simultaneously expanding operations around the world and trying to boost a slumping stock price.
And critics of the takeover remain as adamant as ever. The State Assembly's Energy Committee chairman, Paul D. Tonko, said: "The consumers got ripped off. They paid too much for Lilco and got saddled with debt, while other areas will benefit in the new era of energy competition."
A year ago today, teams of Wall Street lawyers, state officials, corporate executives and bond underwriters were winding up an all-night marathon of document signing.
That elaborate transaction abolished Lilco, and LIPA used tax-free municipal bonds to buy the utility's power lines and customer service division. The rest of Lilco -- its work force, electricity generators and gas division -- merged with Brooklyn Union to become Keyspan. (Confusing matters, the company briefly called itself Marketspan.) LIPA hired Keyspan to run the electricity system.
No sooner than the deal was done, a scandal erupted over $67 million in "golden parachute" payments for 26 Lilco executives who were kept on at Keyspan. The biggest winner: Lilco's often pilloried chairman, William J. Catacosinos. He became Keyspan's new chief and still collected $42 million.
Critics accused LIPA of ignoring the fine print that granted the payments. But Mr. Kessel charged that Lilco had deceived LIPA and had violated a ban on such payments. Mr. Pataki denounced the payments as "disgraceful" and moved to force out Dr. Catacosinos. Officials from Brooklyn Union said they had been betrayed by their new partner.
A battle broke out for control of Keyspan. Dr. Catacosinos marshaled institutional investors who hoped that he would drive up the stock price by selling the company, perhaps to Consolidated Edison. His rival was his second in command, Robert B. Catell, who had led Brooklyn Union. He favored expanding Keyspan, not selling it. Finally, in an emergency board meeting, Dr. Catacosinos quit and Mr. Catell emerged victorious.
The payments to the Lilco executives prompted state investigations and about 20 shareholder lawsuits. As a result, Keyspan, as Lilco's successor, agreed to pay $6 million to LIPA, $1.5 million to the State Attorney General and $7.9 million to settle the shareholder suits.
Dr. Catacosinos vacated his office this month and resurfaced on Tuesday as leader of an investor group planning a $1 billion leveraged buyout of TNP Enterprises of Fort Worth, which provides electricity to 229,000 customers in Texas and New Mexico.
Keyspan, after pocketing $2 billion in the LIPA takeover deal, was considered a candidate to buy another company or be taken over itself. Instead, its stock slumped and has hovered around $27.
"It was at $38, so you're down 11 points from the high," said Edward J. Tirello, a utility analyst for BT Alex. Brown, an investment bank. A fan of Dr. Catacosinos, Mr. Tirello still credits Mr. Catell for moves like buying back $600 million in stock. "But to get the price up, they have to look for a merger partner. The number of candidates is declining daily. I would say they have a year or two."
Keyspan says it aspires to become "a premier energy company in the Northeast." To that end, it is buying Con Ed's 2,168-megawatt and 415-megawatt generators in Ravenswood, Queens, for $597 million and the Philip Fritze & Sons heating and ventilating contractors in New Jersey for an undisclosed sum. It is also investing hundreds of millions of dollars in gas exploration in the Gulf of Mexico, gas processing in Canada and gas pipelines and storage nationally and in Northern Ireland.
The company cut its work force to 7,300 from 8,300 through attrition and early retirement, without having to resort to layoffs. But meshing Lilco and Brooklyn Union has not been simple. Aware that mergers often fail because of clashing styles, Keyspan holds seminars on the corporate cultures of the two old companies and the aims of the new one. "We're putting all the employees through it," Mr. Catell said. "It's coming along quite well."
Not so, says a leader of Keyspan's biggest union. "Regrettably, I am taken aback that the integration hasn't progressed better," said Ralph Ranghelli, business manager of Local 1049 of the International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers, which has 2,000 members at Keyspan.
"We from the former Lilco side see ourselves as adopting more of Brooklyn Union's culture, and that is troubling," he said. "In the old Lilco, things were much more structured and focused from the top down, whether you liked it or not. Brooklyn Union's method is much more loosely constructed. Executives freelance and change as they go along. Their team has become dominant."
Keyspan seeks to be evenhanded, Mr. Catell said. Nearly half of Lilco's top executives forfeited $3.8 million in compensation payments to stay with Keyspan, he said.
Keyspan said it would extend to Long Island the community development programs that won good will for Brooklyn Union. For its part, LIPA just approved an expenditure of $32 million a year to promote energy conservation and new technology.
As a public utility, LIPA is exempt from regulation. Critics have proposed voluntary oversight, perhaps through the State Consumer Protection Board, and Mr. Kessel is agreeable. "Look, we've started to turn the corner in the war between the public and its electric utility," he said. "This is a big sea change on Long Island."

HEADLINE: Times Sq. Dreamer or Visionary, He Hopes for a Better Deal

BYLINE: By CHARLES V. BAGLI 

 BODY:
Real estate developers branded Charles B. Moss Jr. a hopeless romantic and worse last year after he walked away from a $92 million deal at his Broadway block front between 44th and 45th Streets.
His critics said that Mr. Moss, 53, whose family roots are sunk deep into the vaudeville theaters and movie palaces of Times Square, could not bear to let go of the property where his grandfather had opened the Criterion theater 63 years ago. So after months of agonizing negotiations, Mr. Moss scuttled a deal with Bertelsmann A.G., the giant German communications conglomerate, which was willing to pay him a fortune to build a new office tower on a platform over his property, while allowing him to keep his storefronts. 
 But Mr. Moss, who is now busy marketing the property, rejects that view, saying sentimentality did not enter into his decision. He said he could make more money leasing the wide expanses of space at 1514 Broadway than he could have in a deal with Bertelsmann, which wanted to fill some of that space with supporting columns for the tower.
"Our family goes back a long time with this building," said Mr. Moss, who lives in Aspen, Colo. "But I like to think of myself as a rational businessman. The Bertelsmann deal just didn't make economic sense."
By next month, Mr. Moss will have cleared the three-story, 140,000-square-foot building of all but one tenant. The leases on the giant billboards attached to the building are also running out. Mr. Moss is betting that entertainment companies and national retailers will pay some of the highest rents in Times Square for his 200-foot stretch of Broadway.
Now that the once shabby district is attracting the likes of Madame Tussaud's, Disney, Virgin Records and MTV, rents have tripled in recent years to as much as $300 a square foot for prime, ground-floor space. C. Bradley Mendelson, a broker with Insignia/ESG who is representing Mr. Moss, said he was negotiating with potential tenants.
But more than one real estate executive is waiting to see whether Mr. Moss made a mistake. Some retailers are complaining that rents in Times Square have jumped so high that they have been priced out of the market.
Still, more than 20 million tourists pour through Times Square every year, spending an estimated $650 million at retail stores. "It's an amazing building, dead center in Times Square," Mr. Mendelson said of Mr. Moss's property. "It has within its four walls the best space in the city."
Although Mr. Moss grew up in Scarsdale, N.Y., where the developer Seymour Durst was his baseball coach, his family's roots were in the theater business. His grandfather, Benjamin S. Moss, moved to New York at the turn of the century and opened a string of vaudeville theaters named after American historical figures or themes: Franklin, Jefferson, the Colony. Headliners included Milton Berle and George Burns.
After vaudeville faded, the Moss family switched to movie theaters, leasing space for the Criterion at 1514 Broadway. It opened in 1936.
Other tenants there included Woolworth's, Crawford Clothes and the International Casino, the largest and most dazzling of the big band nightclubs. Later, Bonds, a clothier, bought the building and ejected its rival, Crawford Clothes.
Mr. Moss said the police used to stop traffic in Times Square for movie premieres at the Criterion, while klieg lights brightened the sky and workers rolled out a red carpet from the theater, which showed the premiere of "My Fair Lady" and "Funny Girl," to the old Astor Hotel, where there was a party for the actors.
Mr. Durst, the late developer, bought the building in the 1960's with plans to build a 10-acre office complex that would rival Rockefeller Center. But during a recession in the early 1970's, Mr. Durst abandoned the property to his lender. Mr. Moss and his father, Charles S. Moss, reluctantly agreed to buy it in 1977 for $4 million in what had become a rundown district.
"At the time we bought the building, it was to preserve the Criterion," Mr. Moss said. "It was a huge financial risk for us."
Mr. Moss, who eventually sold most of his family's theaters, leased space to a succession of discotheques, restaurants and electronic stores. After several false starts in the 1980's, he decided to develop the property in 1997, when Times Square was emerging from a long decline.
Last summer, he opened talks with Bertelsmann, which had a giant tower next door at 1540 Broadway and was looking for a new home for its latest acquisition, Random House. Mr. Moss and his architect, Fox & Fowle, came up with a plan that would allow him to keep his retail space and billboards, by building a 35-story office tower on a platform over the existing building. Under that plan, the platform required only four support columns.
But just as the deal appeared to be coming to a close in November, Mr. Moss said, Bertelsmann executives said they needed a dozen columns, not four, for the platform. Complaining that the columns would consume too much of his retail space, Mr. Moss broke off negotiations.
Random House is now looking at a deal at Broadway and 55th Street, while Mr. Moss is looking for new tenants.
"Developing real estate in New York can be as complex as solving Rubik's Cube," said Ross Moskowitz, a real estate lawyer at Stroock & Stroock & Lavan. "The difference here is that logic will only give you part of the puzzle because ego, personal relationships and other intangibles come into play."

HEADLINE: Despite Grim Test Scores, a School Marks Its Small Triumphs

BYLINE: By RANDAL C. ARCHIBOLD 

 BODY:
The day was winding down in a hectic week for Magalie Pierre's class of fourth graders at Public School 129 in Harlem, and so a party was in order.
Every party has to have a toast, and the duty on Friday fell first on Juriel Gomez, who set aside his plate of pizza, grapes, popcorn and macaroni, pushed back his tiny classroom chair and held aloft a plastic cup filled with soda. 
 "A toast, to everyone who did well on the reading test," Juriel said to applause, as other students rose to offer more salutes to themselves, the school, Ms. Pierre, the principal -- even the people who wrote the test -- so they could have this party.
The students had not yet received their individual scores from the first sitting of the state's new fourth-grade reading exam, but the school did on Tuesday, along with the rest of the state. And at first blush, the festive mood and the toasts might appear to contradict the statistical reality.
At P.S. 129, one of 97 city schools on the state's roster of chronically low-performing schools, just 25 percent of the 95 fourth-graders met or exceeded the state standard. That was 8 percentage points below the citywide average of 33 percent, and 23 points below the state average of 48 percent.
But the school scored above other "schools under registration review," as those on the state roster are officially called; they registered an average of 16 percent. And it ranked third among 13 schools in District 5, whose average was 19 percent.
Beverly N. Lewis, the principal, who had prepped her students with extra tutoring over Christmas break and on weekends and wrote a special reading curriculum with teachers, greeted the results with a mixture of optimism and concern.
The school may have fared better than its low-performing peers, but it is still forever compared with schools across the state that lack the social ills her pupils face.
Nearly every one of her 800 students in kindergarten through sixth grade comes to the school, at 425 West 130th Street, from families poor enough to qualify for free lunches. A quarter of the students live in a shelter for homeless families down the street.
And on top of it all, the stakes are higher than ever. The school system plans to begin holding back failing students in the third, sixth and eighth grades as early as this year, and the 4th through 12th next year. Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, reacting to the reading scores, has called for the dismissal of principals of schools performing most poorly on this and other tests.
Next week, the fourth graders will take the state's new mathematics test, which like the one on reading given in January, is said to be more rigorous than previous standardized tests because it asks students more analytic questions in addition to the usual multiple-choice questions.
"I'm very proud of our progress, but want everyone to be aware we have a long way to go," Dr. Lewis said in her office, festooned with slogans like "Readers Are Leaders" and "When You Believe in Yourself, Anything is Possible."
"Everyone" extends to the children. And so later on, in Ms. Pierre's classroom, Dr. Lewis picked up a cup and offered her own toast: "I'd like to toast all the children. I'm very proud of you and I know we are going to continue to make progress."
Progress is a slippery but important concept here. In November 1997 the school landed on the state's list -- with a threat that it would be closed after three years unless it showed gains on standardized tests and other measures. The next spring it posted a substantial gain on the state test, but still ranked well below city and state averages.
On exams the Board of Education gives in the third through eighth grades, 26 percent were reading at or above grade level last year, giving the school a rank of 619 out of 676.
The state has not yet decided what score on the new exam might take schools off its roster of poorly performing schools, or put new ones on.
Askia Davis, the District 5 administrator, said he did not expect an overnight success. "God is not going to wave a magic wand and say, 'Now you can all read on grade level,' " Dr. Davis said.
A state monitor, Sheila Evans-Tranumn, an associate commissioner at the State Department of Education, said she was heartened by the performance at P.S. 129, but wanted to see it and other troubled schools perform closer to citywide averages.
In the last few years the school has rooted out several apathetic teachers and received grants from the state, city and private sources to train teachers, offer after-school and weekend tutoring and buy computers.
Last year, about 250 students attended summer school, and Dr. Lewis said she expected the same number or more to do so this year, depending on financing from the board and other sources.
The school, a three-story brick building opened in 1958, slid academically as unemployment, drug abuse and homelessness brought down the neighborhood.
Adding to the trouble has been political turmoil in District 5, where Dr. Davis, a former central office administrator, arrived two years ago as interim superintendent to replace Bertrand Brown after a Board of Education report faulted him and the local school board for chronic educational failure.
Whatever the problems, the school's principal tends to put the best face on things.
"We have a lot of societal ills, but all our children can learn," Dr. Lewis said.
And so the school hums with activity from first bell at 8:30 A.M. till nearly 7 P.M. every day, and for several hours over winter and summer breaks and weekends.
Parents like Joyce Winn, who has daughters in the fourth and sixth grades and a son in kindergarten, said she had seen improvement. For instance, her fourth grader, Katherine, "instead of watching cartoons now reads books."
Ms. Winn said it was difficult to get parents involved in the school because they are working, caring for other children at home or slow to appreciate the importance of giving their children extra help at home, having lacked such attention themselves.
But Dr. Lewis and the staff of 47 teachers persevere.
During school hours, students are drilled with practice tests and extra reading of fables and folk tales, the kinds of stories that showed up on the fourth-grade exam.
Jennifer Tyler had her students keep journals and summarize virtually everything they read, even newspaper articles. For the state math test this week, the students on Friday went over work sheets filled with fractions.
Teachers try to boost self-esteem along with the scores.
"I tell them, 'Twenty years from now do you want to be pushing a broom?' " Ms. Tyler said. "I am pushing the stakes up."
The children speak of "staying on task" and "using reading strategies" -- meaning they read and write endlessly.
Aisha Smith, a fourth grader and one of a few pupils who did receive her score (the school is just beginning to distribute them), said all the preparation was worth it, even if she was a little disappointed that she scored a passing grade, level 3, but not the highest, level 4.
"I thought I would pass because our teacher gave us enough courage and enough help to do it," Aisha said.
The consequences of not doing well on such tests weigh on some minds.
"I'm very worried," said Ebony Wilder, a fourth grader. "I told my mother, 'Please don't be mad at me if I get held back.' "
But other fourth graders, like Melanie Hudson, do not mind the pressure. "Tests are good because you can prove to yourself that you passed and can go on to the next grade," she said.
In Ms. Pierre's class, Jose Garcia, who got over the anxiety of one test, must now face another on Wednesday, when he takes the math test.
"I like tests if it involves reading," he said. "But my worst subject, the one I don't really hate, but I don't like, is math. I don't know if I'm going to do well, but Ms. Pierre is helping us."

GREENE-Jerome L. The President, the Board of Trustees and entire Montefiore Medical Center community deeply mourn the death of our dear friend and supporter, Jerome L. Greene. A devoted and active trustee since 1972, he had an extraordinary impact on the quality of health and life of thousands of Montefiore patients and residents of the Bronx and Westchester. Combining his skills as a lawyer with his belief in Montefiore as an instrument of social responsibility, he led the medical center in the founding, 18 years ago, of the Mosholu Preservation Corporation, a nonprofit community development support corporation that has, with his guidance, saved housing, revitalized neighborhoods and created innovative projects such as moderate income housing, a community newspaper that serves 15,000 households, the first and only NYC business improvement district sponsored under the auspices of an academic institution, a vast program of graffiti removal and a youth job training program-all of which have played a role in the Bronx's nationally recognized resurgence. He believed in the crucial role of academic medical centers such as Montefiore in advancing the health of communities through research, education and provision of high-quality care, and with his beloved wife, Dawn, he helped Montefiore create the Jerome L. and Dawn Greene Medical Arts Pavilion, a state-of-the-art building that opened in 1997 offering a full range of general and specialty services in an ambulatory care setting. More recently, he and his wife have been generous benefactors of The Children's Hospital at Montefiore, scheduled to open in 2001. In 1991, at its annual Celebrate Montefiore dinner dance, the medical center honored this remarkable man of vision and commitment for his exemplary stewardship. This year, Montefiore nominated him for the United Hospital Fund's 1999 Distinguished Community Service Award. The medical center has lost one of its greatest friends. We extend our heartfelt condolences to his loving wife, Dawn, and their entire family. Spencer Foreman, MD, President Jay B. Langner, Chairman Board of Trustees Montefiore Medical Center
GREENE-Jerome L. The Directors and Officers of Mosholu Preservation Corporation (MPC) note with profound regret the passing of our founding Chairman, Jerome L. Greene. Mr. Greene's foresight in founding MPC, Montefiore Medical Center's non-profit community development support corporation, has served the community and its health for 18 years. The establishment of MPC was rooted in Mr. Greene's strong belief that Montefiore's commitment to broader public health issues and its long tradition of social responsibility was directly related to neighborhood preservation. His generosity and hands-on leadership of the Corporation made possible the creation of solutions to major problems of community life. His vision helped form a national model for institutional participation in community development. His efforts will mean better lives for people for generations to come. HEADLINE: Presidential Candidates Seem Indifferent to a Salary Rise

BYLINE: By DAVID STOUT

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 29

 BODY:
The people who want to be President seem almost indifferent to an issue of public policy that could affect one of them personally: How much should the next chief executive be paid?
Despite repeated requests, fewer than half the candidates commented last week on a proposal in Congress to raise the pay of the President to $400,000. The salary has been $200,000 since Richard M. Nixon took office in 1969. 
 "Al Gore has spent his career in public service to help people, not to go after a pay raise," said Melissa Ratcliff, a spokeswoman for the Vice President.
Former Senator Bill Bradley, Mr. Gore's rival for the Democratic nomination, had a similar view. "You don't run for this job for the annual salary," Mr. Bradley said through his spokesman, Tony Wyche. "I think we should leave it up to Congress."
The leading Republican candidate agreed entirely. "It's the job of the Congress to decide," Gov. George W. Bush of Texas said through his spokeswoman, Mindy Tucker.
One of his rivals for the Republican nomination, former Gov. Lamar Alexander of Tennessee, also declined to involve himself in the debate. "I shouldn't help to set the salary I will receive as President," Mr. Alexander said through his chief press aide, Bo Harmon.
Steve Forbes, another Republican candidate, opposes a raise for the next President, said his spokeswoman, Juleanna Glover Weiss. The wealthy Mr. Forbes, of course, does not have to worry about money.
Gary L. Bauer, a social and religious conservative, is regarded by pollsters as the longest of long shots for the Republican nomination, but on the issue of Presidential pay his views -- as far as they go -- are in line with those of his countrymen.
"I can tell you that Gary opposes doubling the President's salary all at once," Mr. Bauer's spokeswoman, Jessica Morris, said.
So do most Americans, according to a recent survey by the Pew Research Center for the People and the Press in Washington. Only one person in four thought the President's pay should rise by more than $20,000.
About two-thirds of those surveyed said the President should get a raise at least once a decade. Told that the President's pay had not gone up since 1969, 49 percent said the next one should get a raise while 39 percent said no.
But when those surveyed were told that the President now makes $200,000 (a fact only 19 percent of the respondents knew beforehand), plus $100,000 for travel and $50,000 for expenses, only 41 percent favored a raise, while 55 percent opposed one.
The survey was based on telephone interviews among a nationwide sample of 1,179 adults from May 12 through May 16 and had a margin of sampling error of plus or minus 4 percentage points.
The public's feelings notwithstanding, witnesses who appeared last week before a panel of the House Committee on Government Reform were "nearly unanimous" that the President's pay should go to $400,000, said Russell George, chief counsel and staff director of the Subcommittee on Government Management, Information and Technology.
Former Presidential aides who might agree on little else, politically or historically, were united in their view that the President's compensation (even with its many fringe benefits) ought to go up.
"This is not even a close call," said Kenneth Duberstein, a former chief of staff to President Ronald Reagan. "This needs to be addressed now. It is a case of simple equity."
Alexander M. Haig, onetime chief of staff to Nixon, agreed, as did John H. Sununu, who held the job under President George Bush, and Thomas F. McLarty 3d, former staff chief to President Clinton.
Mr. McLarty said that other high officials, unlike the President, are eligible for Congressionally approved cost-of-living increases but that those increases were becoming minimal out of fear that the pay of some officials would overtake the President's. For instance, the Vice President, the Chief Justice of the United States and the Speaker of the House make $175,400.
If Congress does not act on a Presidential raise in 1999, it may be even less likely to do so in 2000, since the pay of public officials is a sensitive issue to take up in an election year. If nothing is done before noon on Jan. 20, 2001, the next President will not get a raise.
Five Republican candidates, former Vice President Dan Quayle, Elizabeth Dole, Patrick J. Buchanan, Senator Robert C. Smith of New Hampshire and Representative John R. Kasich of Ohio, declined comment on whether the next person in the White House should be paid more.
But it is a safe bet that they agree with Governor Bush and former Governor Alexander on one point. "He'd take the job either way," Ms. Tucker said of Mr. Bush.
"That's our position, too," Mr. Harmon said for Mr. Alexander.
 
http://www.nytimes.com

CORRECTION-DATE: June 3, 1999, Thursday

 CORRECTION:
An article on Sunday about the opinions of Presidential candidates on whether the next President of the United States should get a pay increase misstated the attitude of Elizabeth Dole. Rather than being one of five Republican candidates declining to comment on the President's salary, Mrs. Dole told Speaker J. Dennis Hastert in a letter last week that Congress should reject a proposal to raise it to $400,000. She said the current figure of $200,000 was "fair and appropriate," considering the many perquisites of the office.

GRAPHIC: Graph: "DOLLARS AND SENSE: Presidential Pay"
Starting at $25,000 in 1789, the President's salary has been raised only four times. It was first raised to $50,000 for Ulysses S. Grant's second term. William H. Taft was the first beneficiary of the second raise; pay was raised again for Harry S. Truman's second term. Richard M. Nixon was the first to benefit from the most recent raise.
 
*Figures for estimating inflation based on consumer prices start in 1913.

HEADLINE: Report Cards: How the Island's Fourth Graders Are Reading

BYLINE: By JOSH BARBANEL 

 BODY:
The New York State Education Department released the first results of its new fourth-grade English Language Arts test last week, and the results were a disappointment to many parents and teachers across the state. The new test, administered over three days in January, was designed to measure how many fourth graders meet new standards requiring them to read complicated passages and write intelligently about them.
 The results showed that many students have a long way to go. Across the state, 52 percent failed to meet the standards. And even on Long Island, with some of the best-performing schools in the state, 38 percent fell short of the standards, and fewer than 7 percent scored at an advanced level. 
 What follow are the scores for every public school with a fourth grade, along with information on family income so schools with students of similar economic status can be compared.
 Here are explanations of the categories.
 STUDENTS TESTED The number of fourth graders who took the test. Unlike past results, these include special education students who took the test.
 INCOME Percentage of students who did not qualify for free lunch, based on Federal income guidelines in 1998. The higher the number, the higher the income. When schools did not provide a percentage, the state provided an alternative measure.
 PERFORMANCE LEVELS Percentage of students in each category.
 Levels are ranked from 1 (lowest) to 4. Students at levels 1 and 2 fall below the standards, with those at level 1 having serious deficiences and those at level 2 needing extra help.
 Students at levels 3 and 4 meet the standards. Those at level 3 are on track to meet the state's high school graduation requirements, and those at level 4 perform at consistently high levels.
 PERCENTAGE MEETING STANDARDS The black bar shows the percentage of students who scored at level 3 or 4.
 Note: Blanks indicate missing data.

SECTION: Section 3; Page 4; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1325 words

HEADLINE: BUSINESS;
Venturing (Widely) Forth With City Hall Capital

BYLINE: By MICHAEL BRICK 

 BODY:
CITIES and states everywhere long to be the next Silicon Someplace. Many have used tax money to set up business incubators and promote their attractions to entrepreneurs; some even throw subsidies and tax relief at technology companies to lure them to set up shop, create jobs and join the local tax rolls.
But as a piece of civic boosterism, the Prospect Street NYC Discovery fund is a strange animal. 
 It was created in 1995 by the City of New York to invest in fledgling technology companies, using tax money and contributions from several of the city's utilities. At the time, Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani said the city expected the fund's activities to generate 4,000 new local jobs, foster a hot new business sector and maybe even earn a decent return on the investment.
Four years later, the fund's returns have indeed been decent, though far from stellar. But the fund has sometimes strayed from its other goals. Few new jobs have been created directly, and several of the fund's investments have been in companies with a tenuous connection to high technology and none at all to the city.
Among the major beneficiaries of the fund's activities have been the people who run it. Executives at Prospect Street Ventures, the New York-based venture capital firm paid by the city to operate the fund, have also been compensated in cash and stock option grants by companies in which the fund has invested, for serving on their boards of directors.
The fund's initial capital of $30 million in equity -- $10 million of it from city coffers -- and $26 million in Small Business Administration loans has since grown to $76 million, with $48.3 million invested in 11 companies and the rest in liquid investments awaiting deployment. Prospect Street executives now sit on the boards of nine of those companies, positioning them to reap windfalls if the companies prove successful.
It is common for major early investors in a start-up company to get seats on the board, so they can keep a close watch on their investments and help the company grow. Prospect Street, which also invests for private-sector funds, routinely asks for such representation when the Discovery fund invests. "It's not a hard-and-fast rule," said Stephen G. Hall, a principal at Prospect Street. "But getting a board seat is typically a prerequisite."
In the Discovery fund's case, it is public money that the managers are investing and risking, while they personally reap the rewards of the board seats. "We're aware of this arrangement," said Bernadette O'Leary, a spokeswoman for the city's Economic Development Corporation, which oversees the fund. "It's very standard for the industry and it's in keeping with our agreement."
John F. Barry, the managing partner of Prospect Street, is himself on the boards of eight companies that have received money from the Discovery fund, including two that reported the compensation of their directors to the Securities and Exchange Commission. Those documents show that Mr. Barry has personally received cash, stock and stock options for his services -- for example, 18,750 shares of 24/7 Media, a New York-based Internet advertising company whose stock has risen sharply since its initial public offering in 1998 and closed on Friday at $28.50. Mr. Hall said the compensation is justified because "there needs to be appropriate incentives to take on that liability" of being a director, and added that Mr. Barry holds such stock and options on behalf of his firm, Prospect Street Ventures.
To be sure, the executives work for the money. A new Internet company can consist merely of a couple of sharp programmers with a bright idea but no management experience. Experienced directors like the Prospect Street executives often help shape business plans and recruit crucial employees. (Prospect Street highlights on its Web site the hands-on role of its executives in companies in which it invests.)
That kind of work has helped the fund post a cumulative return of 108 percent over four years -- not bad, though many technology-stock mutual funds have gained far more during the same period.
City officials say the fund's activities have helped accelerate the creation of Silicon Alley, a district in Manhattan extending roughly from the Flatiron Building to the financial district where Internet companies congregate. They say they are pleased with the results, though they acknowledge that Silicon Alley grew mostly on its own.
"It's not as if somebody in City Hall said, 'Hey, I know, let's get a bunch of kids in their 20's with shoulder-length ponytails, give them computers and and see what happens," said Charles Millard, the director of the city development agency, who is resigning to become a managing director at Prudential Securities. "They simply try to help the market that has occurred on its own function more powerfully."
The fund's managers estimate that its investments have created 600 jobs so far.
Prospect Street was selected from among four bidders to manage the fund. Its mission was to find suitable New York City technology companies for $1 million to $6 million investments. Almost immediately, records of the fund's investments indicate, Prospect Street began to stray from the target.
Some $3 million went into Bondnet Trading Systems, a Connecticut operator of an Internet securities trading service. Mr. Barry was named chairman and chief executive of Bondnet, and Mr. Hall was named to the board. Investing in a Connecticut technology company was appropriate, Mr. Hall said, because most of its business was done in New York. The company's assets were bought by the Bank of New York in 1997.
The fund also invested in All Service Computer Rental, an Englewood, N.J., company that seems to fit none of the fund's stated purposes: It is not in New York, has no direct ties to Internet commerce and, having been in business since 1985, is not a fledgling. "The most important goal of the fund is to receive a return," Mr. Hall said. "It's our most profitable company."
The fund's very first investment was also of questionable relevance, and has performed terribly. In July 1995, when the fund was less than two months old, Prospect Street bought 1.09 million shares of Skyline Multimedia Entertainment, which operates a virtual-reality ride at the Empire State Building and an arcade near Times Square, at $2.75 a share. Mr. Barry joined Skyline's board and by November 1996 had been personally granted options to buy 10,000 shares of Skyline stock at $3.50 to $5 a share.
The next month, the company began work on a new virtual-reality ride in Sydney, Australia, and sank nearly $1 million into film footage for it; the project collapsed when a lease fell through. Around the same time, the company made substantial loans to one of its executives. Still, the Discovery fund pumped in capital, $500,000 more in 1997 and a further $435,000 in 1998, while the stock was losing nearly all its value; on Friday, it traded for 6.25 cents a share.
"It's very common for a venture capital firm to continue investing in a company that we think has potential," Mr. Hall said. "We have some investments that are all-stars and some that are not."
Since 1997, the fund has also found successful Internet companies in which to invest, including Multex, Miningco.com (since renamed About.com) and 24/7 Media. Dean Margolis, chief executive of Comet Systems, which has grown to employ 40 people in New York, said Prospect Street chipped in $2.4 million of the company's $2.5 million in start-up capital.
But of the 14 Manhattan technology companies that planned public offerings in the second quarter of 1999, only one, Juno Online Services, has received money from the Discovery fund. Juno went public last Wednesday at $13 a share and closed on Friday at $12.25.
"We're really in the business of looking into the future," Mr. Barry said. "Unfortunately, no one has given us a crystal ball."

 CORRECTION:
An article last Sunday about Prospect Street NYC Discovery, a venture capital fund created with New York City tax money, misstated its performance. In the four years ended March 31, the fund's internal rate of return averaged 100 percent a year; it was not 108 percent cumulatively.
The article also referred incorrectly to securities granted to John F. Barry, managing partner of Prospect Street Ventures, the firm that operates the fund, for serving on the boards of companies in which it invested. The grants were entirely of stock options, not options and stock.

HEADLINE: TRAVEL ADVISORY: CORRESPONDENT'S REPORT;
Asia, Slowly Recovering, Still Offers Bargains

BYLINE: By EDWIN McDOWELL 

 BODY:
WITH the economies of most Asian nations appearing to be slowly on the mend, the region's airlines and hotels are no longer practically giving away their services, as they were less than a year ago. Nevertheless, there are still lots of travel bargains to Asia, and travelers whose plans can accommodate midweek departures, Saturday stays and other restrictions can save money, or at least enjoy an array of extras.
Experts say the bargains are also beneficial to countries in the region. "Tourism is actually helping to stimulate economic recovery and growth in destinations including South Korea, Thailand and Bali," said Joseph A. McInerney, the chief executive of the Pacific Asia Travel Association in Bangkok. "In Thailand, for example, tourist arrivals from the U.S. reached 361,705 in 1998 -- a 16.3 percent increase over 1997." 
 The biggest bargains, as usual, are those offered by the airlines, which typically arrange package deals with tour operators and hotels.
Northwest Airlines, for example, is offering a Bangkok package that includes air fare and five nights in a hotel for $669 for West Coast departures, and $717 from New York, Boston, Chicago, Detroit, Memphis and Minneapolis/St. Paul. After June 30, prices rise to $809 and $845, for travel through Aug. 31. Other Northwest packages are available for travel to Hong Kong, Singapore, Beijing, Shanghai and Tokyo.
United Airlines' tour branch, United Vacations, is offering a $999 package for travel through Dec. 31 that includes air fare between the West Coast and Hong Kong, five nights at one of two Mandarin Oriental hotels there and 5,000 bonus miles in United's frequent flier program. From New York the price starts at $1,059.
Cathay Pacific's $999 All Asia Pass is back, allowing passengers to fly from New York, San Francisco or Los Angeles to Hong Kong, then to as many as 15 other cities. The pass, available only through travel agents, is valid for 21 days, but passengers who register at www.cathay-usa.com receive an additional 10 days. Travel is valid between Aug. 22 and Nov. 16; for travel now through Aug. 21, add $200.
Malaysia Airlines has a package starting at $700 a person including air fare from Newark or Los Angeles to Kuala Lumpur or any of six other destinations in Malaysia. Designed for two or more passengers traveling together on the same reservation, the package includes three nights in a hotel and daily breakfast. It must be booked by June 30 and travel completed by July 10.
Thai Airways' specials include a $999 round-trip package from Los Angeles to Bangkok, Chiang Mai and Phuket with eight nights' hotel and sightseeing. From New York, the price is $350 higher. The package, valid for travel through Sept. 30, costs $299 extra for travel June 1 through Aug. 31.
Air New Zealand has two specials through Aug. 31: $899 between Los Angeles and Auckland, New Zealand, with five nights' accommodations, and $999 between Los Angeles and Sydney, with five nights' accommodations, breakfast and airport transfers. Reservations: Newmans South Pacific Vacations, (800) 421-3326.
Passengers on Singapore Airlines can stay in Singapore for as little as $60 a night for two in a first-class hotel (such as the Excelsior) or for $142 for two in a Marriott or similar hotel. The package includes discounts for shopping, dining, sightseeing and rental cars, as well as admission to six attractions including the Singapore zoo and Jurong Bird Park.
Hoping to counter the past year's low occupancy rates, which plummeted below 50 percent in many major cities, many Asian hotels still offer bargain rates or a slew of enhancements.
From Tuesday through Aug. 31, the Mandarin Oriental Hotel Group -- with properties in eight Asian cities, including the Oriental in Singapore and Bangkok, and the Mandarin Oriental in Hong Kong and Jakarta -- is offering double occupancy at the single rate, with free use of hotel health equipment and breakfast.
Through Oct. 31, 90 Holiday Inn and Crowne Plaza hotels in 15 Asian countries are offering a free night to guests who stay one to three nights at a participating hotel and pay with a Visa card.
Peninsula Group hotels, which include the Peninsula Bangkok, Peninsula Manila, Peninsula Hong Kong and The Palace Hotel in Bangkok, offer an array of discounts, upgrades and other amenities. Through August, the rate at the Kowloon Hotel in Hong Kong is $168 a night, rather than the usual $310. Also included are a daily American buffet breakfast and 500 miles with either United Airlines or Japan Airlines.
Marriott Hotels are lowering rates, at least through September, and are making more of amenities packages. In addition to ground transportation to and from the airport, a daily newpaper and free use of the health club, the Marriott Royal Garden Riverside Hotel in Bangkok is offering a 50 percent discount on tennis lessons.
Hilton Hotels in Singapore, Tokyo, Bangkok, Taipei and Beijing have also trimmed prices modestly and added upgrades, transportation, breakfasts and other perks. Most of the hotels will offer the promotions at least through September.

SECTION: Section 7; Page 4; Column 1; Book Review Desk 

LENGTH: 1381 words

HEADLINE: Sharpshooter

BYLINE: By John Crowley; John Crowley's novels include "Little Big."

 BODY:

THE NIGHT INSPECTOR
 By Frederick Busch.
 Illustrated. 278 pp. New York:
 Harmony Books. $23. 
 EDMUND WILSON wrote a vast study of 19th-century American literature, "Patriotic Gore," in order to answer the question of why so few novels were written about the Civil War by those who experienced it. Perhaps, like the Holocaust (but unlike World War I), it was an event so overturning of values, so profoundly dehumanizing even as it promoted a noble end, that those who experienced it could not conceive of how to tell the truth about it in the modes available to them.
In time, we might learn how, or learn many different ways how, from Stephen Crane to Frederick Busch's new novel, "The Night Inspector." The book is centrally about how to tell truths the soul has not found words for or that no one else can hear -- or wishes to.
William Bartholomew was a sharpshooter in the war, the model in fact for Winslow Homer's famous rendering of a Union sniper in a tree. Detached from his unit with only a sergeant and a couple of privates, he stalked Southern soldiers, gaining a terrible reputation not only among the Confederate troops he preyed on but also among the Northern soldiers, who shunned him. We learn Bartholomew's war story in fragments as he haunts the infamous Five Points neighborhood of lower Manhattan after the war, among the outlaw gangs, wild pigs, lost children and sewer rats, human and otherwise. He wears always a papier-mche mask, made by a prosthetist to hide the disfigurement of a hideous bullet wound that ended his sharpshooting. "You have your limbs, God forgive us," the prosthetist tells him. "I suggest that I am proof of His unreadiness to do so," Bartholomew replies. He is in business now, making money in the brutal and very personal battleground of New York commerce, collecting his own debts on the streets like a gangster, and yet he is a profound observer and rememberer, as self-possessed as when he hid unmoving in the trees with his Sharps rifle.
Among the night people he comes to know downtown is a former writer of fiction, once very popular, then not popular, now nearly silent except for the obsessive writing of verse no one reads. He is employed as a deputy customs inspector; he wears a tin badge and carries locks with which to seal cargoes that he suspects of being illegally unloaded. Busch names him with a certain coyness as M, but he is indeed the author of "Moby-Dick."
"I am the man I was," he tells the masked Bartholomew. "I am my own secret now, however. I am my darkest, best-held secret. Do I wish to be? I would prefer not to. Do I choose? I do not. Shipmate: Like the nation, I was divided from myself; like the nation I was wounded; riven, like the nation I healed; like you now, Bill, I am healthy; we are whole." This Melvillean irony, so harsh and utter, is the spirit of this book; Busch's sinewy and truth-telling prose is its body.
Why did Bartholomew become a sharpshooter? Not out of allegiance to the Union cause, for he believes Abraham Lincoln to have been a vain blowhard and the real causes of the war to be economic. We are not allowed to know why, but we know that Bartholomew was capable before the war of plotting a dreadful death for a smarmy uncle who forced himself upon Bartholomew's widowed mother. He seems to others to be a creature of cold resolve, inhuman, death itself; he knows himself to have always been afraid.
Bartholomew remembers every Confederate he shot from ambush: the young officer reading a letter, the "Rebel whore" who tried to defend her house and her clients from his shots out of the darkness, the two men engaged together in sex. The death that stings him most is that of a dog he threw himself upon and killed with his knife so that it could not give him away: "His jaws, in the grip of my left hand, were under my belly, and he heaved beneath me like someone at love. Up and down we jerked and rode and sawed, I like death itself come down on him from the evening, and the tired, terrified, dying dog like any one of us."
Unwillingly, unwittingly, Bartholomew imports the tragic violence of his past life into his present. He sells his Army revolver to M for a pittance, as a gift for M's son Malcolm, a feckless and self-indulgent youth who has joined the National Guard. Malcolm, after a drunken night, kills himself in his bedroom with this pistol -- by accident or not no one can know.
Guns, locks and masks are invoked often, their varied significance noticed and discussed by Bartholomew and the grieving M. Bartholomew frequents an uptown brothel -- the same one where Melville's son was accustomed to drink -- and he spends his new money on Jessie, a former slave whose "golden" skin, obsessively referred to, is marked with tattoos, which she reads for him. She is one of two women who are allowed to see Bartholomew without his mask (the other being a sturdy Chinese washerwoman who bathes him in her steaming tubs). He allows Jessie to talk him into a plot to abduct and bring to New York a shipload of black children still held in slavery in Florida, an act of generosity to which he brings all his considerable resources, and into which he draws M, the night inspector.
The workings of Jessie's plot move very slowly forward; what compels the reader more are Bartholomew's conversations with M and with Sam Mordecai, a young reporter down from Boston who was one of the soldiers in Bartholomew's detachment during the war. Sam wants above all to be a writer; he is one of those few readers who remember and revere M for his books. Sam's attempts to understand and record the bristling and formidable M, and M's awful grief and relentless self-questioning about his son's death, are given to us in Sam's notebook, a reporter's scribbled transcriptions that are at the same time a truly Melvillean rhapsody, and a triumph for the writer -- for Busch, that is.
There are various ways of giving authenticity to a period novel, and Busch has chosen the simplest and most effective -- and the most difficult: his prose and his conversations are precisely, surprisingly and thoroughly of their time; these sentences ring like silver dollars on cobblestone. Since no one alive today can really know what the sound of conversation, or of private thoughts, really was then, there can be no question of actuality, but of authenticity we can judge: this is the voice of that time as we know it from its own writings, only spoken by a man more of our time than his.
It is not the only pleasure of "The Night Inspector." The sharpshooting scenes are compelling, and Busch shows great skill in moving us from the killing fields to postwar New York without fuss or confusion, and without the melodramatic jump-cuts that are the usual method. The harsh wit of the dialogue is wonderful: "A recent loss?" Sam asks when Bartholomew mentions his uncle's death. "No, you would have to call it a profit," Bartholomew says.
THE plot to save the slave children is not what it seems, and Bartholomew's belief that money and business rule the world is horribly borne out at the end. Sam, M and Billy join a nightmarish rescue attempt on the Hudson at night, more reminiscent of the 19th-century fantasist Charles Brockden Brown (whom Bartholomew once read with interest at Harvard) than of Melville.
Busch, pulling for shore in the last scenes of his book, loses something of the force of his characters and his language even as the darkness of the story deepens; it's the novelist's curse, the wrapping-up of the plot, and characters often shrink away in the process. But Busch's bitter closing is neatly allayed by a couple of postscripts, one tart, one sweet. In one, Billy, M and Sam observe the sensational performance of Charles Dickens reading from his own works -- unlike the forgotten Melville, "he would carry away from New York several hundreds of thousands of dollars"; the other is a gentle, almost Tolstoyan, last glimpse of Billy and the Chinese washerwoman: "a tea-colored woman with an oval, impassive face, and a man beside her whose face was a mask of another sort." "The Night Inspector" is a marvelously dark-hued story by a master craftsman, and watching mastery at work provides at least a part of the pleasure of reading it.
