SECTION: Section 6; Page 31; Column 1; Magazine Desk 

LENGTH: 3411 words

HEADLINE: A Peacenik Goes to War

BYLINE: By Josef Joffe; Josef Joffe is the editorial-page editor and a columnist for Sddeutsche Zeitung, a national daily published in Munich.

 BODY:
Think of Abbie Hoffman or Jerry Rubin, yesteryear's counterculture icons, and imagine them as Secretary of State today. That will give you a first approximation of Joschka Fischer, 51, the onetime political prankster, street fighter and revolutionary who was sworn in as Germany's Foreign Minister last fall.
When Fischer himself fell prey to a prankster earlier this month -- having his head and face splattered with red paint -- his personal transformation was virtually complete. Joschka Fischer, the perennial bad boy of German politics, was now the victim of political theater, the establishment object of counterculture protest. 
Thirty years ago, he battled against American "imperialism" in the streets of Frankfurt; this year, he closed ranks with the United States in Germany's first shooting war since 1945. Two decades ago, Fischer and his fellow Green Party members marched against the deployment of Pershing nuclear missiles in Western Europe; today, the defrocked pacifist looks on approvingly as the Pentagon lobs cruise missiles into Serbia.
Fischer recalls his last conversation with the Yugoslav President, Slobodan Milosevic, in Belgrade, just before NATO unleashed its planes on March 24. "I am ready to walk on corpses, and the West is not," the Serbian strongman said. "This is why I shall win." Milosevic's cynical arrogance, Fischer says, bristling, convinced him right then that the bombs had to fly.
Nor is the renegade from the peace uber Alles camp alone. His Chancellor, the former left-wing radical Gerhard SchrRated PG-13der, wants to "break Milosevic's criminal will," but not with ground troops, as he emphasized in a May 19 statement that reverberated throughout NATO capitals. That's a step too far, not only for Germany but also for virtually every European nation except Britain. Like Fischer, the Chancellor, a Social Democrat, says that "Milosevic must not win this war." And so do many of Fischer's Greens -- grudgingly, ambivalently. One of those is Angelika Beer, a Communist in her student days, who now represents the Greens on the Bundestag's Defense Committee. "Guarding the peace and protecting human rights," she intones, "are two Green objectives that cannot be realized simultaneously at this point."
Fischer's enemies in the party are not so polite, heckling him, jeering and, in that one celebrated instance, pelting him with a plastic bag filled with red paint. In that incident, during a convention called hastily this month to debate the Kosovo war, his eardrum was broken by the impact. Yet Fischer didn't give an inch. His address to the 800 delegates (protected by 400 police officers) was his final farewell to the party's fundamentalists.
"Peace," he thundered, "means that men aren't murdered, women aren't raped and people aren't driven from their homes." Some of his comrades were calling for an unconditional halt in the bombing. "If you pass this resolution," he bellowed, "I will not act on it!" The pacifist extremists got only 318 votes, while the moderate resolution introduced by the leadership carried the day with 444 votes.
The Greens did not bolt from the Government, and Germany did not go AWOL on the West's war against Belgrade. Today, the old America-basher Joschka Fischer has only kind words for the "last remaining superpower." No, he insists, he was "never anti-American." He was "against a lot of American policy," he said recently. "But I felt close to American culture. Berkeley was around the corner. Woodstock . . . was our own culture." He sounds like Madeleine Albright when he affirms that "the United States is an indispensable power for me -- even if Europe unites, it will need an Atlantic insurance policy."
In conversations with reporters, Fischer keeps insisting on the powerful connection between Germany's democracy and its Westbindung, or "community with the West." "If we turn our back on that community," he says, "we might jeopardize our democratic revolution." And that, he is quick to add, is the first in German history that has worked. No Schaukelpolitik for him -- the policy of maneuver and balance between East and West that has regularly brought grief to both Germany and Europe. "To me," Fischer insists, "the West is an indispensable insurance against the return of German nationalism."
But Germany is indisputably shedding the fetters of the cold war, raising difficult questions about what its foreign policy should be. Fischer is emphatic on what it should not be. It should "never again be back to Bismarck," a hegemonial policy that would try to run Europe from Berlin. That would reactivate precisely the "nightmare of coalitions" Bismarck had dreaded all his life. But isn't that too modest a role for Europe's most powerful country?
Fischer responds with a slight smile, "We lead from the second row, but we are doing quite well there." This could be Helmut Kohl speaking. Like the former Chancellor, to whose girth Fischer once aspired, the new Foreign Minister has the twin lessons of this century practically bred into his genes. When Germany struck out on its own, it reaped disaster. When it went for community and integration, it flourished.
"The more we pursue our interests multilaterally, through Europe," Fischer postulates, "the more we'll get for ourselves." In other words, you do well by doing good for others. Not bad for a medium-great power that has learned in the most painful way that wingtips and cell phones are far more useful than panzers and jackboots.
A pacifist going to war? An anti-Vietnam marcher praising America? Self-reinvention is Joschka Fischer's business. He reminds you a bit of Forrest Gump, who ran his way to fame and riches, grabbing and shedding personas as he went. Fischer, who began the morphing game when he ditched his official first name, Joseph (for the Joschka of his forebears, who lived in Hungary), started running seriously rather late in life -- when his third wife, Claudia, 17 years his junior, dumped him in 1996. (He just married No. 4, Nicola Leske, a 29-year-old journalism student.) Traumatized by the breakup, Fischer literally transformed himself. He was so fat back then that he would wheeze after a mere dash to the refrigerator. So he took up jogging with a fierce determination. Over the next several months, he drove his ballooning frame down from 240 to 165 pounds.
And he kept running. No more booze, cigarettes and wurst, just mineral water, veggies and muesli. And Puccini, especially "La Boh<heart>me," which struck him as "real tripping music, and more psychedelic than Pink Floyd." It was the Hamburg Marathon next, and -- joints willing -- it will be New York, his great dream, one of these days.
In the meantime, though, there was a bigger race to win: the 1998 election. His Greens, an infighting bunch of pacifists, ecologists, counterculturists and plain old leftists, had been around for almost 20 years, in and out of Parliament. Unlike his fundamentalist comrades (known as "fundis"), Fischer and his growing retinue of "realos" knew that '98 was the Greens' first, and perhaps only, chance to slip into Government -- as junior partners of Gerhard SchrRated PG-13der's Social Democrats.
So Fischer stopped pursuing the runner's high and instead began to jog for power. To bring in the vote, he crisscrossed the country, "a bit in each district," for a grand total of 300 miles. He drew enough recruits for the Greens (6.7 percent of the vote) to push the Social Democrats over the top.
On the night of Sept. 27, Kohl, Germany's apparent Chancellor-in-Perpetuity, was history. The man who had ruled Germany for 16 years was replaced by a Red-and-Green duo: by Gerhard SchrRated PG-13der as Chancellor and Joschka Fischer as Vice Chancellor and Foreign Minister.
No more Nike running shoes like the gleaming white pair Fischer wore at his swearing-in as Environment Minister in Hesse, his home state, in 1985. Back then, the good people of Frankfurt could not believe that this punk, who used to lead the shock troops of Revolution<diamond=rer Kampf (Revolutionary Struggle) against their finest, was now going to ride around in an official Mercedes-Benz.
Since last fall, however, nobody has seen the ex-punk in anything but a gray three-piece suit, complete with french cuffs and dark solid or polka-dot ties. He also sports a signet ring, the traditional affectation of German aristocrats. Considering his most humble beginnings, this get-up has "Only in America" written all over it. Though this is Germany, Fischer does embody an American career in spades -- from high-school dropout to the seventh floor of Foggy Bottom, as it were, in 33 years.
His father, a butcher in a meat-processing plant, was felled by a stroke when Fischer was 18. He recalls how he went to the hospital to pick up his father's belongings, "those work clothes reeking of fat and blood, the greasy butcher's satchel he had lugged around all these years." Back in his parents' apartment, he had an epiphany, as he recalls it. "I was at home, all alone, and I told myself, 'No, I ain't gonna live like that."'
He had already quit high school the year before, at 17, and there was Edeltraud, a girl he had just met. The two of them took off together, hitchhiking to Paris, Amsterdam and Marseilles, where they spent the nights in empty freight cars. To feed themselves, they covered the Marseilles sidewalks with chalk paintings -- Jesus figures and windjammers -- that might elicit some coins from passers-by. Then a run to Gretna Green, Scotland, where minors could marry without parental consent in those days.
Fischer (who never did finish high school) also tried his luck as a wedding photographer, auto worker, used-book seller and taxi driver, his most stable job. He wrote countless tracts on left-wing politics and taught his fellow travelers from the Revolutionary Struggle how to fight the "pigs." Did Fischer ever engage in "revolutionary violence" himself? He is adept at dodging the question. A former comrade in arms, Thomas Schmid, now the editorial-page editor of the conservative daily Die Welt, says, "No comment."
Since his latest reinvention, Fischer has roamed the world in a Luftwaffe Airbus, with his very own sleeping quarters decorated in haute motel-modern. In the back there is a retinue of 20 aides and two dozen reporters and photographers. Asked why he keeps recasting himself, Fischer stretches out on his cabin bed and clasps his hands behind his neck. "I hate nothing more than boredom," he says. "When you reach the point where you've exhausted all the possibilities of your present life, you choose another one -- just like a mountain climber who goes for the top only to start looking for the next one."
He has gone for a new life every 10 years or so, he reports, but, no, he is no Forrest Gump. "More like Robert De Niro, who really lives his roles. So do I, I live them, too. And now I am the Foreign Minister." Thomas Schmid agrees with the De Niro analogy, adding tartly, "He has an incredible mimetic talent."
So Fischer keeps changing and running, and the media lap it up. "They treat him like a rock star," said Richard Burt, the former United States Ambassador to Germany, as he watched a horde of photographers converge on Fischer at a recent strategy conference in Munich.
Fischer is quite specific about the events that made him veer off the revolutionary road, which by 1976 left him arrested and jailed after a police car was firebombed during a demonstration. With a young officer near death, Fischer and a dozen others were brought in on a possible charge of attempted murder. But at the arraignment the next day, the judge let all of them go. Two months later, pro-Palestinian German terrorists hijacked an Air France airplane to Entebbe, Uganda.
Suddenly, Fischer saw ugly reality behind the self-righteous anti-Zionism of his kaffiyeh-clad comrades, who chose to cast the Israelis as Nazis and the Palestinians as Jews. Why would young Germans concoct this bizarre projection if they were supposedly protesting not only against capitalism and imperialism but also against their parents' complicity with Nazism? By raging against the evils inflicted on the Palestinians by Jews, young Germans could regain the moral high ground precisely against those who kept reminding them of their inherited guilt. That was the unconscious function of anti-Zionism. Nor is this ancient history. During the gulf war, one of Fischer's Greens, Hans-Christian StrRated PG-13bele, declared that Saddam Hussein's Scud attacks on Israel were but payback for the occupation of Palestinian lands.
In Entebbe, Fischer recalls, "those pretending to be anti-fascists almost compulsively re-enacted what they had blamed their fathers for, where they separated Jewish and non-Jewish passengers." He deliberately uses the German word selektieren, the process by which Jews were chosen for the gas chamber.
Entebbe and a wave of terrorist murders that swept Germany in 1977 were the turning point where "illusions were lost," Fischer reports. At that moment, "the best ideals and intentions . . . degenerated into high crimes -- that was a defining experience for me." It was time for another metamorphosis.
In 1981, he joined the one-year-old Green Party and, after the 1983 election, suddenly found himself sitting face to face with Chancellor Helmut Kohl in the Bundestag. Though he would not drop one of his previous personas: the clowning provocateur who loves to play to the gallery. He remained an in-your-face agitator who, during one heated debate, hurled a scatological epithet against the President of the Bundestag before quickly adding, "If you don't mind, sir." Rhetorically, he bragged, he was doing to Kohl's parliamentary minions what "Attila and Genghis Khan had done to the West."
Throughout the early 1980's, Fischer continued his slow transformation from revolutionary to Realpolitiker. The strategic calculus was obvious. His Greens could climb to the top only on the backs of the Social Democrats, but as radical pacifists and neutralists, the Greens were untouchables -- even for the leftish Social Democrats. So while holding on to his pacifist cloak, Fischer started pushing to the right. It was a long, uphill battle.
In 1985, he signaled his party to grow up by subtly ridiculing its hatred of NATO. Cautiously, he expressed his "doubts" about "how far one could get with the slogan 'Out of NATO!"' Not very far, he might have added, if the Greens wanted the Social Democrats' stamp of approval. In the mid-80's they did not, putting ideology above interest. A year later, the party congress passed its notorious "Germany Out of NATO" resolution, which further stated that "there can be no peace with NATO." Fischer refused to vote for it -- an act of considerable courage in those days.
Fundamentalist strength reached its zenith in 1986, arguably the height of the cold war, but it took another 10 years before the intraparty balance of power began to tilt toward the "realos." Much of the credit belongs to Fischer, who patiently chipped away at the extremist positions of his brethren. In 1994, he published "Risiko Deutschland" (roughly, "Hazard Germany"), a long essay on German foreign policy. With the "fundis" watching his every step for "right-wing deviationism," Fischer still felt constrained to drape his pro-Western sentiments in the conditional. "If it is true," he reasoned, "that integration in the West is Germany's central raison d'tre, then the German left will have to rethink its nationalist-pacifist and neutralist course." Anything stronger could get you excommunicated, even in 1994.
The break with his party's peace uber Alles dogma came one year later, after the slaughter of more than 7,000 Bosnians in Srebrenica. But Fischer still had to fight pacifism with pacifism, as it were. "If war once more turns into a successful means of policy, then we can forget about a peaceful future for Europe."
By this year, he was openly advocating force to stop force. He was even willing to dispense with a United Nations Security Council mandate -- if there is no consensus while we are facing a humanitarian catastrophe or a grave threat to the regional peace," as he said last November. To those Greens who now claim that NATO's strikes against Serbia are morally worse than the daily massacre of Kosovars, Fischer says, "I learned not only 'No more war' but also 'No more Auschwitz."'
While Fischer has no doubts about Kosovo, or Germany's place in NATO and Europe, his Green Party is deeply divided. Its members still don't know whether they are in politics for good or whether, though fuller of girth and grayer of hair, they want to continue as a utopian movement that clings to principle over the exercise of power. They will have to decide soon.
The Greens have much the same problem as Levi Strauss, which has been closing blue jeans plants all over the world because kids refuse to wear the same pants as their parents. Neither do German youngsters want to meet their bearded and Birkenstock-shod math teachers in the local Green Party meeting.
So the young are absconding in droves, and in the most recent German election, in Hesse, Fischer's home state, the Greens' take plummeted from 11 percent to 7 percent. They have lost votes in 11 state elections since 1994, as they did in the national vote in 1998. And no wonder. The Greens are essentially a party of functionaries, teachers and public servants whose jobs are as safe as their fringe benefits are fat. Largely sheltered from the "real" economy, with its permanent double-digit unemployment, many of these 50-somethings are wedded to statism and the status quo: to high Government spending, anticompetitive regulations and a reactionary utopia that romanticizes trees and vilifies technology.
In an open letter to Fischer, four young Greens protested that "the party no longer reaches people because it has moved away from the realities of life." Those include a high unemployment rate and the country's leisurely passage from manufacturing to a job-rich service economy. The solution is not windmills or solar power, they wrote, but "cars, computers, chip cards and cell phones." In its conservatism, they said, the Green Party "resembles the Catholic Church."
If anybody grasps the existential quandary of the Greens, it is Fischer, the man who smells a trend faster than Dick Morris. Was his party just the one-shot project of a protest generation that is now slowly moving toward retirement? "Perhaps," he concedes. "I've been warning them for years about this danger." Asked how he would crack the quandary, the Foreign Minister slips into generalities. "We have to combine the professionalism of a party in power with the visionary elan of a programmatic reform movement."
But as Fischer well knows, environmentalism was co-opted by the established parties, and the Greens' other big issue, peace, rings not only hollow but also morally bankrupt when people are dying in Bosnia or Kosovo. The party has aged, but not matured; it is in power, but not in control of its future.
So where is Fischer? In Washington, at NATO's 50th-birthday celebration. Or in Moscow, pushing his peace plan for Kosovo. Or in Brussels, Amsterdam or Luxembourg at one of those innumerable inter-Governmental meetings seeking to reform the screeching decision-making machinery of the European Union. Or in Sana, Djibouti or Beijing.
He is running as always, crisscrossing the world in his Luftwaffe Airbus, using a small corner of his brain to think about the next way to reinvent himself. For as much as he wants to give his "utmost," he hates "boredom." So when "something is exhausted," he muses, "it is time to leave it behind."
His life, Fischer explains, "unfolds in 10-year cycles." So he'll keep that Luftwaffe plane until 2008? Given the extraordinary longevity of German Governments -- from 13 to 16 years in the past -- it's a good bet that he will, especially after that last party congress this month. His comrades may have hit him with paint bombs and ultrapacifist resolutions, but they did not bolt, and so the Red-and-Green alliance continues to hold. Maybe those 50-somethings have grown up after all. If so, they owe it to Joschka Fischer, the high-school dropout and former pavement painter who has developed into one of the most extraordinary politicians of his generation.

SECTION: Section 2; Page 27; Column 1; Arts and Leisure Desk

HEADLINE: TELEVISION/RADIO;
Another Award, Other Memories Of McCarthyism

BYLINE: By JEFF KISSELOFF; Jeff Kisseloff is the author of "The Box: An Oral History of Television, 1920-1961."

 BODY:
AROUND the time the first peals of outrage were heard in Los Angeles over the honorary Oscar for Elia Kazan, who had named names to the House Un-American Activities Committee during the McCarthy era, a small ceremony in New York was conducted with none of the dissonance that accompanied the Hollywood salute.
In February, Frank N. Stanton, the former president of CBS, who oversaw the network's blacklisting policies in the 1950's and 60's, was given a lifetime achievement award for his First Amendment work by the New York chapter of the National Academy of Television Arts and Sciences. 
 Like Mr. Kazan's, Mr. Stanton's associates can point to a host of solid achievements. Over the course of his 27 years at the helm, Mr. Stanton often stood tall. When President Richard M. Nixon sent armed process servers to intimidate him into turning over outtakes for "The Selling of the Pentagon," Mr. Stanton refused at risk of a contempt citation. When the Teamsters threatened to shut down CBS over a documentary on Jimmy Hoffa, he told them to go ahead and make his day. And when Congress tried to close the door on television cameras, Mr. Stanton battled until he won.
"I don't think anyone in this business has ever fought harder for the First Amendment," William F. Baker, the president of WNET (Channel 13), who presented Mr. Stanton with the award during a lunch, said in a recent interview.
While Mr. Baker said he knew nothing about Mr. Stanton's involvement with the blacklist, a number of television veterans, still scarred by the McCarthy era, are all too familiar with that other side of Mr. Stanton's work: when those whose only crime was to take the right of free expression seriously were banned from the network. Not surprisingly, some question whether such an award is appropriate.
"I'll use a word from my granddaughter's new vocabulary: despicable," says Allan Sloane, a leading radio writer of the 50's who was blacklisted by CBS, referring to Mr. Stanton's being given the award. "Here was someone who had to make a choice between right and wrong, and he chose wrong."
Mr. Stanton's award and reactions to it reflect the television industry's disparate attitudes, almost 50 years afterward, toward the blacklisting era as a whole. Mr. Kazan and Mr. Stanton may take home trophies, but their deeds live on in the memories of those they affected. Given a choice between forgiving and forgetting, Mr. Sloane chooses neither. "I don't believe in that kind of redemption."
He is not alone.
"The blacklist was an atrocity for the Bill of Rights," says the former CBS producer Norman Corwin. "It was the blacklist that was true un-Americanism. It was mean, and it was also cowardly."
Norman Siegel, the executive director of the New York Civil Liberties Union, calls the blacklisting era "one of the darkest moments in American history."
"You would hope there would be leaders in the Government and in the industries who would not succumb to that kind of pressure, including financial boycotts," Mr. Siegel said, "but history tells us we don't have those kinds of leaders -- or at least they are rare."
Sally Bedell Smith, the historian and author of a biography of William S. Paley, the longtime chairman of CBS, looks more kindly on Mr. Stanton. "I think that award was justified, because he really did do great things for the First Amendment," she said. "The blacklisting period should not be considered an indelible mark against him." She is less generous in her book, however. In it she calls CBS's political screening "a reprehensible violation of basic civil liberties."
Blacklisting took root in 1947. That year, three former F.B.I. agents began publishing Counterattack, a newsletter with the expressed purpose of exposing Communist influence in the entertainment industry. In reality, the editors were engaged in a protection racket. Those named in Counterattack could be cleared by the publishers for a fee, and producers were ordered by network executives to call in their cast lists for clearance before hiring. A producer who hated the process added Santa Claus to the list. Santa was cleared, for $5.
In 1950, Counterattack's publishers issued Red Channels, a booklet accusing 151 people of belonging to or supporting the Communist Party or sometimes merely backing progressive causes like the United Nations and integration. Some were blacklisted for speaking out against blacklisting. As John Henry Faulk, a CBS blacklisting victim, wrote in "Fear on Trial," his 1964 book, "The cry to end injustice was now more of a target than the injustice itself." Almost all of those listed in Red Channels were immediately blacklisted.
Each of the networks caved in to some extent. NBC abruptly canceled "The Aldrich Family" in 1950, for instance, until an actress could be found to replace Jean Muir, its star. Muir did not work in television again for eight years. The actress Kim Hunter has recalled being blacklisted by CBS, ABC and NBC, in that order. Delbert Mann, who directed "Marty" for "Goodyear TV Playhouse," has said he was so fed up with NBC's blacklist that he had to be talked out of leaving the network. But none of the other networks went as far as CBS.
In 1950, Mr. Stanton approved a companywide loyalty oath to reassure advertisers and self-proclaimed patriots of the political correctness of CBS employees. The next year, with Paley's approval, Mr. Stanton took the network beyond Red Channels with the creation of a security office staffed by former F.B.I. agents to investigate the political leanings of its employees.
The screenwriter Walter Bernstein was contributing scripts to CBS until his blacklisting. Mr. Bernstein, a World War II veteran who has written two movies ("The Front" and "The House on Carroll Street") and a memoir ("Inside Out") on the subject, still recalls the panic of not knowing how his rent was going to be paid. Both he and Mr. Corwin believe that the network has never acknowledged its wrongdoing.
"What I would like is some acknowledgment of what they did -- just to say they participated in something very bad," says Mr. Bernstein.
In a recent interview, Mr. Stanton, 91, said blacklisting had been necessary to stave off pressure from advertisers and affiliates who were threatening to abandon CBS and possibly shut it down. He conceded, however, that the network's response to the pressure may not have been the best one.
"I didn't have the wisdom, nor did anyone else," Mr. Stanton said. "The head of the law department was one of the fairest people I've ever known. When he said this was the course we should follow, we went along with it."
Told of Mr. Stanton's remarks, Mr. Corwin, 89, had kind words for his old friend. "Well, that's a lot better than Kazan thumping his chest over what he did."
Still, the broadcast historian Erik Barnouw says CBS was never in any real economic danger in the 1950's. "To reduce their income by 10 percent, that would have been enough to upset them." He does not believe the sins of the past can be dismissed.
"It's almost callous," Mr. Barnouw said, "to simply shove under the rug the horrors committed by the blacklist situation."
As Mr. Sloane, 83, paged through a copy of Red Channels in the office of his New Canaan, Conn., home, he chuckled to himself as if he were looking through his old high school yearbook. "There I am between Bill Shirer and Howard K. Smith. I'm in pretty good company." But after a few more moments of more names, he shook his head sadly. "This opens an old scab," he said. "I thought it was a scar, but it isn't."
He pulled a glassine envelope from a desk drawer. Inside were scraps of bones he scooped from the ovens of Dachau as a reporter in 1947. "I would speak to kids, and I would shake the envelope and say, 'This is the sound of fascism.' When I was blacklisted, I asked someone why I couldn't get work. He said, 'Because you want to change the world with your typewriter,' and they didn't want that."
"CBS decided not to carry my stuff any more, and the world went on, but not for Edward Bromberg," who was named by Mr. Kazan and died of a heart attack after testifying before the House committee, he said, "and not for Phil Loeb. One day he was on 'The Goldbergs,' and the next day he was untouchable. Ask him how it felt. Find a medium that is in touch with him."
Philip Loeb was the co-star of "The Goldbergs," a sitcom about a middle-class Jewish family in the Bronx. The show was canceled by CBS in 1951 when its star and producer, Gertrude Berg, refused to dismiss Loeb, the object of four letters of protest. And when the series re-emerged the next year, on NBC, it was with a new actor in the role. Loeb never worked on television again. In 1955, when his mentally ill son had to be moved to a state home because there weren't enough funds for his care, Loeb became despondent and killed himself.
Ms. Smith, the historian, says it's important to place the blacklist period within the context of the times.
 THERE was little economic pressure in 1963, however, when an episode of the law series "The Defenders" revealed just how hypocritical CBS's actions were.
That year the show's creator, Reginald Rose, proposed doing an episode about race, a touchy topic at the time. "They were very nervous about it," says Mr. Rose. "I said, 'Let me do a show on blacklisting, and I'll forget this show.' " To his astonishment, network executives agreed. But when the script was completed, it ran into trouble with CBS's program practices department.
David Shaw, the show's story editor, recalled what happened. "The head of program practices said: 'This has to be set in the movies. We don't have a TV blacklist.' He was in charge of the blacklist."
But the ultimate shock came when the cast list had to be submitted for clearance. "I said, 'My God, we're doing a show on the blacklist,' " recalled Ernie Kinoy, who wrote the episode, " 'and we have to submit the names!' "
The episode won two Emmys, but the blacklist was still being enforced. The process did peter out not long afterward; Faulk's legal victory over those who blacklisted him (he won a libel suit against the publishers of Counterattack and Red Channels) had also made it unprofitable for the practice to continue.
Some 35 years later, in an editorial on Mr. Stanton's career, Broadcast and Cable magazine hailed "the Stantonian period" as a high-water era for the Bill of Rights. "They're not rewriting history," says Abe Polonsky, a blacklisted writer who helped write CBS's classic show "You Are There" under a pseudonym. "They can't rewrite the facts, but they can rewrite what we think about those facts."

HEADLINE: TECHNOLOGY;
Surfing to Soothe a Savage Breast

BYLINE: By JANE L. LEVERE 

 BODY:
The Comcast Corporation, a cable giant with diverse holdings that include stakes in the television and on-line versions of the QVC shopping channel and E Entertainment Television, has added a more highbrow investment to its portfolio: Culturefinder.com.
Culturefinder is a Web site that lists arts events and sells tickets to exhibits, operas, symphonies, dance and theatrical performances around the United States. 
 For Julian A. Brodsky, vice chairman of Comcast and chairman of the six-month-old fund, Comcast Interactive Capital Group, that announced the $3.75 million investment two weeks ago, the move was part of his company's effort to "leverage our involvement with the Internet and create an investment hedge against the changing relative values of content, commerce and distribution."
For Eugene Carr, the founder and chief executive of Culturefinder.com, the investment by Comcast, which is based in Philadelphia, represented even more: further validation of a business that has enabled Mr. Carr to combine his entrepreneurial impulses with a lifelong affinity for the performing arts.
Mr. Carr, a cellist who studied at the Juilliard School of Music while still in high school, earned an M.B.A. at Columbia University. He was the executive director of the New York-based American Symphony Orchestra when he started Culturefinder.com in 1995.
"I perceived that the Internet would be big and so would massive sites that collected around specific interests," he said in an interview in Culturefinder's crowded offices, which are situated, appropriately, near Carnegie Hall. "I knew that someone would do an arts site, and I thought it would be an incredible opportunity to do it myself."
After polling administrators of 50 arts groups to see if they would support a site that listed their programs, Mr. Carr invested $50,000 of his own to start Culturefinder.com. He also got $300,000 from America Online to participate in its Greenhouse project -- an effort, since dropped, that backed content providers in their infancy.
Some other Web publishers, like the Sidewalk operation of the Microsoft Corporation, list concerts and other performance arts. And some, like Ticketmaster Online-Citysearch Inc., sell tickets. But Culturefinder.com is the only national on-line directory of arts events and seller of electronic tickets for more than 2,200 organizations in some 1,300 cities. The institutions, which vary from the New Century Chamber Orchestra in Mill Valley, Calif., to the New York Philharmonic, do not pay Culturefinder to be included in the listings. Instead, much of Culturefinder's revenue comes from the fees -- ranging from $2 to $4.50 -- it charges for each ticket sold.
"Organizations like Ticketmaster Online compete with Culturefinder only on high-profile events," said James McQuivey, senior analyst for Forrester Research in Cambridge, Mass. "Focusing on art and culture gives Culturefinder an extra market Ticketmaster is not in."
In partnerships with the on-line retailers Amazon.com, CD Now, Image Exchange and Reel, Culturefinder also peddles compact disks, arts posters and videos. For patrons who might want to venture far afield in search of a good scherzo, it has an alliance with Microsoft's Expedia on-line travel agency. The site also offers various articles, program notes and primers about the arts aimed at its estimated 250,000 different visitors each month, an audience that surveys indicate is two-thirds female, largely urban, highly educated and, on average, 36 years old. That gives Culturefinder a desirable demographic for its on-line advertisers, the largest of which is the Baldwin Piano and Organ Company.
Mr. Carr and his staff of 14 "bring passion, experience and an intense focus on arts consumers and arts organizations," said Mr. Brodsky, the Comcast executive. "This, combined with superb technology and the dramatically quick adoption of E-commerce among upscale consumers, bodes well for Culturefinder's future."
To stimulate sales, Culturefinder.com recently reached ticket sales-referral agreements with three other Web sites: the search portal Lycos.com, America Online's city guide service Digitalcity.com, and Ovationtv.com, the site of the arts-oriented cable television network.
As a privately held company, Culturefinder.com does not report its financial results. But Mr. Carr said he expected revenue this year of about $1.5 million and a loss that he declined to quantify. He predicted that the company would become profitable as soon as 2001.
He estimates that institutions in the United States like those represented by Culturefinder sell close to $3 billion worth of tickets annually. But he said those organizations typically sell only 70 percent of their available tickets -- representing an opportunity for companies like his to help fill lots of empty seats.
Besides America Online and Comcast, a number of prominent individual investors have put a total of $1.75 million in Mr. Carr's venture. They include Arthur Zankel, a partner in First Manhattan, a New York-based investment advisory firm, and a member of the board of Carnegie Hall; Robert H. Lessin, whose privately held Wit Capital Group specializes in Internet-related investments; Thomas G. Stemberg, chairman and chief executive of Staples Inc.; Stuart G. Moldaw, chairman of Gymboree, and David Horowitz, former chief executive and president of MTV Network.
"I gave him money for the American Symphony Orchestra and had a high opinion of what he accomplished with it," Mr. Zankel said. "I have great admiration for Gene's marketing skills and imagination, and I haven't been disappointed."
David Kitto, director of marketing and ticket operations for Carnegie Hall, said Culturefinder could help arts organizations of all sizes expand their audiences, whether or not the arts groups have their own Web sites. "All of us are looking for ways to connect with the audience," Mr. Kitto said. "Culturefinder is not competing with us; they're providing an alternate means to purchase tickets."
In Mr. Kitto's view, the biggest challenge Culturefinder now faces is creating identity and brand awareness. "They need to work to become the place arts consumers go to to find out about ticket availability," he said.
For his part, Mr. Carr wants Culturefinder.com to expand to include arts listings outside the United States. And to generate new commission income, he intends to begin offering special tour packages late this year, like an individually tailored Broadway weekend in New York with hotel accommodations, theater tickets, meals and museum admissions.
Unlike many Internet start-ups at this stage of business, Culturefinder is not now destined to be sold or to issue stock in an initial public offering, Mr. Carr said, though he does not rule this out in the longer term. "We've been very focused on raising the venture capital round," he said, referring to the Comcast infusion. "At the appropriate moment, to raise additional funds, every financing option will be considered -- including an I.P.O."
Mainly, the Comcast money will help Culturefinder promote its brand more actively, Mr. Carr said. "I'm trying to be Mr. Arts in cyberspace," he added, "to be front and center so when opportunities present themselves, I'm first in line."

HEADLINE: Advertisers Spend Eagerly For TV Time in Fall Season

BYLINE: By STUART ELLIOTT 

 BODY:
The advance sale of commercial time by the broadcast television networks for the fall season, known as the up-front market, is ending on an even stronger note this year than the optimistic forecasts in recent weeks had anticipated.
"There is even more demand than we had expected," said Jamie Kellner, president of the WB Network in Burbank, Calif., which is owned by Time Warner Inc. and the Tribune Company. "We were in a position to increase rates." 
 If current estimates are correct, the six broadcast networks will sell $7 billion to $7.2 billion worth of commercial time in advance for the prime-time programs in the 1999-2000 season. Though negotiations are not entirely wrapped up -- some dickering is to continue this week and into June -- the estimates are about 9 percent higher than the approximately $6.4 billion worth of commercial time sold ahead of the 1998-99 prime-time season.
"There was amazing speed and earliness" to the demand, said Joe Mandese, editor of The Myers Report, a media newsletter, who tracks the annual performance of the up-front market.
"The other day parts were pretty strong, too," he added, referring to sales of commercial time during morning, news and late-night programs. "It all speaks to the underlying strength of the media economy."
The robust results came despite continued declines in ratings for most of the prime-time programs on the broadcast networks during the 1998-99 season, which ended last week. But even as viewership continues to erode, those six networks -- ABC, CBS, the Fox Broadcasting Company, NBC, UPN and the WB -- still offer marketers the chance to pitch products before large audiences.
As usual, the networks focusing their programs on the younger consumers most coveted by advertisers performed particularly well. WB, for example, which seeks viewers ages 12 to 34, sold about $450 million worth of commercial time in the up-front market, compared with $300 million last year.
"Everyone looking for young eyeballs was showing up," Mr. Kellner said. "There were a lot of new advertisers, and then incumbent advertisers were asking for more inventory."
Fox, the unit of the News Corporation that aims its programs at viewers ranging in age from 18 to 34 and 18 to 49, sold more than $1.3 billion worth of commercial time, compared with about $1.15 billion last year.
Demand was consistent among advertisers for many categories of goods and services, Mr. Mandese said, with retail and telecommunications advertisers especially eager to make commitments.
Internet and so-called dot.com marketers were "not a big factor so far," he added, but should be later, when the networks sell commercial time during the season in what is called the scatter market.
The demand for broadcast commercial time augurs well for the cable networks, which conduct their up-front market after that of their broadcast counterparts. Ratings for many cable programs rose during the 1998-99 season as viewers sought alternatives to broadcast fare.

HEADLINE: In an Antitrust Suit, a Tiny Ex-PartnerIs Taking Aim at Microsoft

BYLINE: By STEVE LOHR 

 BODY:
As the trial in the Government's antitrust case against the Microsoft Corporation resumes on Tuesday in Washington, evidence in a separate trial set to open this week in Connecticut seems to offer tantalizing support for an assertion that the company's rivals have made for years -- that Microsoft wields its industry-standard Windows operating system as a club to control the software business.
Central to the Connecticut case is an issue that has been raised peripherally by several Government witnesses in the Washington case -- how Microsoft controls its competitors' understanding of application program interfaces, or A.P.I.'s. 
 A.P.I.'s are the paths into and out of the operating system that programmers must follow for their software -- spreadsheets, Internet browsers, picture editing and so on -- to run efficiently. Rivals have long said that Microsoft gives them only rudimentary maps of the Windows A.P.I.'s, making it impossible to compete with the designs of Microsoft's own applications programmers, who enjoy a detailed road map of Windows, complete with little back roads and alleys that can be used as shortcuts.
Microsoft dismisses such contentions as whining by competitors. But in an internal E-mail memo to several senior executives on Feb. 28, 1996, Mark Ryland, a company manager, observed matter-of-factly, "Microsoft learned long ago that to control the A.P.I.'s is to control the industry."
That memo is among thousands of internal Microsoft documents filed in United States District Court in Bridgeport, Conn., as part of a private antitrust suit scheduled to go to trial on Wednesday. Among other things, they show a spirited debate inside Microsoft over how much of its software should run "cross-platform" -- that is, on competing operating systems like Unix, the platform for many large corporate networks.
And some of the E-mail offers vivid glimpses of Microsoft's winner-take-all style of competition. In one internal memo, a Microsoft manager discusses a proposal to enable some of its data base technology to work on Unix. This was a topic previously raised with data base companies like Sybase Inc. and the Oracle Corporation, which compete with Microsoft in data base software but also develop products that run on Windows as well as on Unix.
On April 19, 1996, James Allchin, a Microsoft senior vice president, wrote: "Please give me one good reason why we should even consider this. (Hint: any good answer needs to include making more money and helping kill Unix, Sybase or Oracle.)"
The suit in Connecticut is not directly related to the Government's sweeping antitrust case. But the Justice Department and the 19 states suing Microsoft are eagerly awaiting the evidence in the Connecticut case because it focuses squarely on the issue of technology access.
The plaintiff, Bristol Technology Inc., a small software maker in Danbury, Conn., charges that Microsoft illegally cut off its access to Windows technology after three years of cooperation and says that Microsoft had a "duty to deal" with it.
The Government's more traditional antitrust suit charges that Microsoft used its Windows monopoly to stifle competition from Internet software. The Justice Department left the issue of technology access to be addressed in the remedy phase of the trial if it wins, and it is a crucial subject in continuing settlement talks with Microsoft.
One Justice Department official observed, "I think we should put a sign up in the war room here, 'It's the A.P.I.'s, stupid' " -- a play on President Clinton's 1992 campaign theme stressing the economy.
Microsoft says that reading too much into a few E-mail messages is unfair. The company, its lawyers note, is unapologetically filled with highly competitive people with a passion for the software business -- a passion reflected in their E-mail. "And a lot of the E-mail that Bristol has filed as evidence has nothing to do with this case," said Steve Aeschbacher, a senior corporate lawyer for Microsoft. "There are discussions about cross-platform strategies all the time within Microsoft."
Indeed, Bristol is not going to win its antitrust suit against Microsoft merely with piles of inflammatory E-mail that raise important industry issues. It must produce convincing evidence that Microsoft violated the antitrust laws, and legal experts say that is a stiff challenge.
Bristol has assembled a legal team led by two veteran antitrust experts known for defending the International Business Machines Corporation, Patrick Lynch, a partner at O'Melveny & Myers, and Anthony L. Clapes, a former I.B.M. assistant general counsel. But legal experts say that "duty-to-deal" cases are hard to win outside industries with traditions of regulation, like telecommunications, or businesses involving natural assets, like ski slopes.
"The courts are very reluctant to say that dominant firms have a duty to deal, because that takes you down a regulatory path," William Kovacic, a professor at the George Washington University Law School, said. "It also reduces incentives to innovate if a dominant firm is forced to share its hard-won assets -- like intellectual property in the software business -- with other companies."
But Bristol charges that Microsoft used it as a tool in a cynical campaign to gain a foothold in the market for heavy-duty corporate computing and then tried to shift those customers over to using Microsoft's most advanced operating system, Windows NT. Windows NT has gradually improved over the years, but after it was first shipped in 1992, it struggled to compete against Unix in the market for big corporate systems.
Microsoft's main advantage was the popularity of the many software applications -- an estimated 100,000 -- written to run on Windows. For example, a scientist using a powerful Unix work station for research might very well want to use Microsoft Word to write memos or an Excel spreadsheet for doing budgets.
Bristol developed a product that solved that problem. Its Wind/U software enabled programs written for Windows to run on Unix work stations, or hub computers called servers, that send data to workers' desktop machines. That opened mainstream corporate business to the legions of Windows software developers.
But for Bristol's translation software to work, the company needed access to Microsoft's technology. In 1994, Bristol signed a three-year contract with Microsoft giving Bristol virtually open access to all Windows A.P.I.'s. Lifted by its partnership with Microsoft, Bristol thrived. In 1997, Inc. magazine ranked Bristol 140th among the 500 fastest-growing companies in the nation, with a payroll of 70 people and estimated revenue of $8 million.
Yet that was the high point for Bristol. It began negotiating with Microsoft on a contract for access to the newer versions of Microsoft's operating-system technology. After the two sides failed to reach an agreement, Bristol sued last August contending that Microsoft's offer -- a fourfold increase in price and much-reduced access to Windows technology -- was an "apple core," as a Microsoft E-mail message described it.
According to Bristol, the changed terms were no mystery. Windows NT has made real strides in the corporate market against Unix. Cripple the cross-platform software provided by Bristol, and many customers that have become dependent on Windows applications thanks to Bristol's software would move from Unix to Windows. "Microsoft's dealings with Bristol are a classic bait-and-switch," Mr. Lynch, Bristol's lead trial lawyer, said.
Microsoft replies that Bristol has no legal grounds for its case and has embarked on litigation as a business tactic. It points out that the 1994 contract covered access only to Windows NT 3.0, Windows 3.1 and Windows 95, and it contained no language about future versions of Windows.
True, says Keith Blackwell, the president of Bristol. But he points to the many public statements Microsoft made about its commitment to the cross-platform program, called the Windows Interface Source Environment, or WISE. One document posted on the Web in 1995 stated, "Microsoft is committed to providing WISE licensees" -- like Bristol -- "with future versions of Windows family source code."
Mr. Blackwell said, "Microsoft repeatedly and publicly made all these representations that the WISE program was a safe way to go."
Other companies also had conflicts with Microsoft over access to Windows technology, including AT&T, the inventor of Unix, which eventually settled a contract suit seeking access to Windows NT 5.0 programming code.
But a Bristol rival, Mainsoft Inc., did reach an agreement with Microsoft last year. And Bristol, according to Microsoft, rejected the same contract terms that Mainsoft found acceptable. The negotiations with Microsoft were lengthy and difficult, William McCarthy, Mainsoft's vice president for business development, said. "But there's nothing we can't do that we want to do under the new contract."
Bristol's duty-to-deal case falls apart because "Microsoft has not refused to deal with Bristol -- it was offered the same terms as Mainsoft," the Microsoft lawyer, Mr. Aeschbacher, said. "Just because someone is not happy with the terms of a deal they are offered, that is not an antitrust case."
Bristol replies that Mainsoft was struggling and willing to take whatever Microsoft offered to stay afloat.
An internal E-mail message certainly suggests, at least, that Microsoft found Mainsoft easier to deal with. On May 18, 1998, Dan Neault, a WISE program manager, wrote: "Mainsoft is in our pocket. Bristol is rattling sabres hard."
Bristol contends that the offer Mainsoft accepted is a hobbled version of the original cross-platform promise of the WISE program, and thus Microsoft has used its market power to undermine competition.
In court, Microsoft will seek to portray Bristol as a company bent on litigation, trying to win a huge financial judgment against a deep-pocketed defendant.
On July 7, 1998, a month before negotiations finally broke off, Bristol received a "strategic communications game plan" from Fleishman-Hillard, a New York public relations firm.
The document, entitled "David v. Goliath," recommended: "Position David as a reluctant, yet courageous plaintiff defending its small, successful and growing entrepreneurial company and Goliath as a predatory, deep-pocketed, unethical behemoth."
Bristol says it would have been foolish to take on Microsoft without some preparation. And it insists that if anyone was negotiating in bad faith, it was Microsoft.
In an E-mail message titled "Co-opting Unix," William H. Gates, Microsoft's chairman, listed as part of the company's strategy, "Picking Mainsoft as our partner for Windows A.P.I.'s on Unix and revving up their activities."
Mr. Gates wrote the message on Sept. 28, 1997, about 11 months before negotiations between Microsoft and Bristol ended.

Chart: "Legal Fights on Many Fronts"
An antitrust suit against Microsoft that begins this week in Bridgeport, Conn., is just the latest in a series of legal challenges to its business practices. Here is a look at the pending cases.
 
OPPOSITION: Bristol Technology
ISSUE: The company, which makes software that lets Windows programs run on other operating systems, says that Microsoft effectively cut off its access to the programming code for the new version of Windows NT.
STATUS: The case goes to trial this week.
 
OPPOSITION: Justice Department and 19 state attorneys general
ISSUE: The biggest challenge to Microsoft. The Government says that Microsoft engaged in anticompetitive behavior in an effort to dominate the market for browsers, the software used to navigate the World Wide Web.
STATUS: The trial resumes this week.
 
OPPOSITION: Sun Micro-systems
ISSUE: Sun says that Microsoft violated a licensing agreement on the use of Java, Sun's programming language, when Microsoft released a version of Java for Windows that was incompatible with Sun's version. According to Sun, Microsoft set out to undermine and destroy a potential threat to Windows.
STATUS: Last week, a Federal judge tentatively ruled that Microsoft had infringed on Sun's patents.
 
OPPOSITION: Temporary Microsoft employees
ISSUE: Thousands of temporary workers say they are entitled to many of the same benefits regular employees receive, including purchasing company stock at a discount, retirement benefits, health insurance and vacation, holiday and sick leave.
STATUS: A court ruled the workers are entitled to buy stock; Microsoft is appealing, and the other arguments are pending.
 
OPPOSITION: Caldera
ISSUE: The company, which made an operating system for PC's called DR-DOS, says Microsoft put pressure on PC makers and used illegal tactics to drive its system, which competed with MS-DOS, out of the market.
STATUS: The judge is handling pretrial motions; the case has yet to start.

HEADLINE: Joao Carlos de Oliveira, at 45; Held Triple Jump World Record

 BODY:
Joao Carlos de Oliveira, a Brazilian sports hero who held the world triple jump record for a decade, died Saturday at Beneficencia Portuguesa Hospital in Sao Paulo, Brazil. He was 45.
De Oliveira, who lived in Pindamonhangaba, in Sao Paulo state, was suffering from pneumonia, hepatitis and cirrhosis of the liver that caused internal bleeding, the hospital said. 
 De Oliveira won the triple jump at the Pan American Games in Mexico City in 1975 with a distance of 58 feet 8 1/4 inches, beating the previous world record of 57-2 1/2. The record was set at an altitude of 7,550 feet, with reduced air resistance.
The record stood until Willie Banks of the United States leaped 58-11 1/2 in Indianapolis on June 16, 1985. Banks's mark, at an altitude of 700 feet, also stood for 10 years. It was broken by Jonathan Edwards of Britain, who jumped 59 feet in July 1995 in Salamanca, Spain, and who set the current record of 60-0 1/4 in August 1995 at Goteborg, Sweden..
De Oliveira won bronze medals at the 1976 Olympics in Montreal and the 1980 Games in Moscow and captured gold at the World Cup in 1981.
His athletic career came to an end in December 1981, when his right leg was amputated below the knee after his car was hit head-on by a drunken driver being chased by the police going the wrong way at night on a highway in Campinas, Brazil.
De Oliveira was twice elected a state deputy in 1986 and 1990 but confronted financial problems afterward when business ventures foundered. Unemployed since he left public office, de Oliveira had been living on a monthly pension of about $700, according to local news reports.
Last year, de Oliveira announced that he would train for the long jump at the Paralympic Games for disabled athletes in Sydney, Australia, in 2000, when he would be aided by an artificial leg. But he abandoned training earlier this year amid illness and depression, friends said. The weekly magazine Epoca said he had been plagued by heavy drinking, which his family denied.
De Oliveira's body was taken yesterday to Sao Paulo's State Assembly, where he once served.

HEADLINE: Disabled Spouses Are Increasingly Forced to Go It Alone

BYLINE: By PETER T. KILBORN 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 30

 BODY:
As modern medicine extends the lives of people with chronic diseases from diabetes to cystic fibrosis, the rate of divorce among them is increasing, leaving a growing number without income, health insurance or support at home, health analysts say.
Among the reasons, they say, are the stresses on the health of the spouse caring for a disabled partner, and the pressure on family finances, especially if one spouse has to stop working to stay home and tend the other. 
 "More diseases that occur in adulthood are treatable, so we know more couples are living longer with one having a chronic illness," said Dr. John Rolland, a psychiatrist and co-director of the Center for Family Health at the University of Chicago.
Louis Harris & Associates, which has surveyed the relationships of people with disabilities for the National Organization on Disability since 1984, found that 13 percent of those it surveyed last year were divorced, compared with 11 percent in 1994 and 9 percent in 1984.
The Government's National Health Interview Survey of nearly 50,000 households in 1994 said 20.7 percent of the disabled adults among those polled were divorced or separated, compared with 13.1 percent of those without disabilities.
Other surveys as well as demographers, family therapists and divorce lawyers suggest that while the divorce rate among spouses with some health conditions, notably breast cancer and multiple sclerosis, is about the same as that of the general population, it is much higher for others.
For people with spinal cord injuries, said Dr. Michael DeVivo, an epidemiologist at the University of Alabama at Birmingham, "Marriage rates are below normal, and divorce rates are above normal."
When Russ Benning, a 50-year-old graphic artist from Antioch, Tenn., was divorced last year after 15 years of marriage, he lost not only his wife, but also her income and her health insurance. Mr. Benning had a painful blood-clotting condition in one leg when he was married, and it developed in the other leg a year later.
He cannot work, must use a wheelchair and makes do with disability payments of $10,000 a year. "It's not easy having the pain, and I do my best not to show it," he said, "but it's unavoidable. It's also not easy to look at somebody in pain and not be able to do something about it."
Among couples who stay together despite a disability, many say they come close to the brink of serious depression or splitting up.
"We were high school sweethearts," said Barbara Fleming, 52, of Mount Airy, Md., whose husband, Kenneth, 55, a retired firefighter, was told he had multiple sclerosis five years ago. "We were a boating family. We had a beach house."
But Mr. Fleming's condition has ended the old routines. He is lucid and speaks clearly, but he is nearly blind and walks with a cane. "We struggle along, but in the end it falls on me," Mrs. Fleming said. "I've gone outside the front door and mentally screamed. I've been in therapy and I go back when I feel I have to have someone to talk to who is not in the family."
Suzanne and Steven Mintz were the parents of a 5-year-old daughter when doctors told Mr. Mintz, an economist, that he had multiple sclerosis 25 years ago. "It turned our lives upside down," Ms. Mintz said.
Soon after the diagnosis, "We separated twice, for 18 months and again for two years," said Ms. Mintz, who used her experience to establish the National Family Caregivers Association, an advocacy group in Kensington, Md.
"It was hard to talk about feelings, to have a conversation about what was disturbing us," she said. "He's in a wheelchair and he needs help with daily activities. But he's still working, thank God. We've come to a better place. He has clinical M.S., but we both have M.S."
Since 1980, researchers at Pennsylvania State University, the University of Nebraska and Virginia Tech have been monitoring about 2,000 couples on a variety of issues, including health.
"What we find is that declines in health have an adverse influence on marital quality," said John N. Edwards, professor of sociology at Virginia Tech.
Tensions develop because the disabled spouse carries less weight in the household, shares in fewer activities and withdraws or becomes moody, angry or critical, Professor Edwards said.
Adrienne Asch, a professor of biology, ethics and human reproduction at Wellesley College and a past president of the Society of Disability Studies, said: "The data are that marriages do break up more often when there is a disability than when there isn't. We also know that men leave wives more often than wives leave husbands."
Lawyers say divorce among the disabled is also influenced by factors that have been driving up the overall rate of divorce, like more casual public mores, less stigma attached to divorce and more wives who work outside of the home.
"It's no longer considered necessary to hang in there with the disabled spouse," said Ira Lurvey, a divorce and custody lawyer in Los Angeles and former chairman of the family law section of the American Bar Association. "The real message we get from the icons of society is if the marriage is too hard, you're not expected to keep it going."
Willard H. DaSilva, a divorce lawyer in Garden City, N.Y., cited a client in her 30's who had filed for divorce. The client's husband had been struck by a car and suffered severe brain injuries, Mr. DaSilva said, adding: "He has difficulty walking, cognitive problems and needs constant attention. He is no longer the person she married."
The health analysts allow that sometimes, in cases of character-transforming conditions like severe brain injury, Alzheimer's disease or conditions requiring the constant care of a nursing home, separation is unavoidable.
Short of those extremes, however, disability can still change a marriage's foundations, said William Pinsof, a clinical psychologist and president of the Family Institute at Northwestern University, which trains therapists.
"It impairs your ability to travel with a spouse, to participate in social life," Mr. Pinsof said.
Dr. Sandra Weintraub, director of an Alzheimer's clinic at Northwestern, said: "There's a whole psychological layer. When you get married, it's not to be somebody's nurse, and suddenly you are somebody's nurse."
For better-off couples, a hired hand can save work and relieve tension. Dr. Weintraub said: "There are spouses who say, 'In no way will I diaper my wife.' So you go out and have somebody else diaper your wife. It helps to have money."
But money becomes a shrinking resource for couples that become caught in the bind of a lost job and the rising cost of care.
"Fully a third of adults with disabilities," the Harris study reported, lived in a household with an annual income of less than $15,000 in 1997, compared with only 12 percent of those without disabilities.
In the face of such pressures, the analysts say, a disability can take as big a toll on the caregiver as it does on the patient. The emotional state of patients with spinal cord injuries often improves within a year, but that of their spouses often declines, said Scott Richards, a clinical psychologist and research director in the department of physical medicine and rehabilitation at the University of Alabama at Birmingham.
The reason, Dr. Richards said, may be that other sources of support -- friends, family, agencies -- drift away, leaving the spouse alone.
Linda Teri, director of the Tornyay Center on Health Aging at the University of Washington in Seattle, said that "spousal caregivers have much higher levels of burden, depression, medical illnesses" than others. Her patients, the elderly, rarely resort to divorce. But younger generations, Ms. Teri said, "are more likely to say it's not worth it."

HEADLINE: Shift by Older Voters to G.O.P. Is Democrats' Challenge in 2000

BYLINE: By ROBIN TONER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, May 30

 BODY:
Older Americans were once among the most loyal of Democratic voters in Congressional elections, reliably returning the party of Roosevelt and Truman to power on Capitol Hill.
But the New Deal generation of older Americans is passing -- World War II veterans are dying at a rate of more than 1,000 a day by one Government estimate -- and their successors are more likely to have Ronald Reagan as a reference point than Franklin Roosevelt. 
 According to surveys of voters leaving the polls, Republicans carried the 60-and-over vote in House races in the last three elections, a source of considerable soul-searching among Democrats. The power of older voters is magnified because of a simple fact: they turn out at the polls.
And strategists in both parties now see older Americans as a critical swing vote in elections next year, a factor that could determine control of the House of Representatives, where Republicans hold a scant majority of 222 to 211.
"It's virtually impossible to take back the House or win the Presidency without taking back seniors," said Celinda Lake, a Democratic pollster. "That makes them the key battleground, and both parties know it."
In fact, some Democratic analysts think that their party would control the House today had it not lost the older vote so decisively last November, 45 percent to 55 percent, while winning or breaking even with every other age group.
The heightened competition for the 60-plus vote affects the policy agenda in a variety of ways, strategists say. It helps explain why the gaps in Medicare benefits, like prescription drug coverage and long-term care, are getting more attention on Capitol Hill these days. As Bill McInturff, a Republican pollster, puts it, "We're having a policy debate over a prescription drug benefit instead of what do we do about the uninsured."
This competition also makes it harder, in the short term, to reach bipartisan consensus on the financial difficulties of Social Security and Medicare, which both need shoring up before the baby boom generation retires.
"Each party is trying to one-up the other," said Representative Thomas M. Davis 3d of Virginia, chairman of the National Republican Congressional Committee. He added that he was ultimately optimistic about a solution. "It's difficult now because everybody wants the other guy to go first and take a shot at their proposal."
Nobody is quite sure why the older vote has shifted, but demographics are clearly part of it. Many analysts note that as a group, older Americans are substantially more affluent today than they were 30 years ago. The poverty rate for Americans 65 and older dropped to 10.5 percent in 1997 from 35 percent in 1959, according to the Census Bureau. Their median income, in constant 1992 dollars, more than doubled between 1957 and 1992.
"They're more affluent, more educated, more mobile, healthier, wealthier, and I'd go a step further and say more positively disposed to the private sector," said Susan MacManus, a political science professor at the University of South Florida in Tampa, speaking of people who are just now joining the ranks of older Americans.
Moreover, their political allegiances were formed in a different era. "Many of the elderly today were in the prime of their working lives during the Reagan era," said Representative Robert T. Matsui, Democrat of California. "They were Reagan Democrats. So ideologically, they're probably more centrist and to the right."
Robert Teeter, a pollster who long worked in Republican politics, noted that such generational shifts have occurred before. In the 1950's and 1960's, Mr. Teeter said, older Americans were considered reliably Republican voters. By the 1970's, he recalled, "you'd sit through all kinds of meetings" with Republicans trying to figure out why they were losing that vote.
The voter surveys showed Democrats carrying the 60-and-older vote in House races from 1980 through 1992, with one exception: the Reagan landslide year of 1984.
Robert Binstock, a professor of aging, health and society at Case Western Reserve University, has argued for many years against the notion that older Americans are a monolithic group voting solely on the basis of their age and their benefit programs. Other factors, like economic status and gender, affect their voting behavior, just as they do with every other age group, Professor Binstock said.
There are significant differences between older women and older men. Republicans began winning the 60-and-over male vote in 1994; they did not carry older women until 1998.
Several analysts argue that older women are much less economically secure than older men, and therefore more open to Democratic appeals based on protecting benefits.
Aside from demographics, there are also short-term explanations for the shifting allegiances of older Americans. Strategists in both parties say the scandal leading to President Clinton's impeachment probably played a role in the size of the Democrats' loss among the 60-and-over vote last November.
"Seniors are somewhat more sensitive to the values issues," said Mark Mellman, a Democratic pollster, "and we have not always presented our strongest face on those issues in recent years."
The voter surveys support this analysis: Social Security was the top issue on the minds of 60-and-over voters last November, but moral and ethical standards ranked No. 2, and 89 percent of those who said the moral issues were the determining factor in their vote selected a Republican candidate for the House.
Republican strategists and candidates have also gotten better at campaigning for the votes of older Americans, strategists in both parties say. For years, Republicans were on the defensive, battered by Democratic campaigns accusing them of plotting to cut Social Security or Medicare.
Representative E. Clay Shaw Jr., Republican of Florida, remembers one rough election in 1982, after the intense maneuvering around Social Security of the early Reagan Administration, including a Senate Republican plan calling for $40 billion in unspecified Social Security savings over three years. The plan quickly died, but the issue did not.
"I have vivid memories of the bumper sticker: Save Social Security, Vote Democratic," Mr. Shaw said. "I went home and I changed every political ad I had going, and everything was Social Security."
In recent years, Republicans have learned to anticipate those campaigns. Ed Goeas, a Republican pollster, said, "Rather than wringing our hands and waiting for the Democrats to use their scare tactics on Social Security and Medicare, we've been getting out there and saying, 'This is our position and don't believe the scare tactics of the last few weeks.' "
In the current maneuvering over the budget, Republicans have been promoting their "lockbox" proposal, pledging to "lock away" the Social Security surpluses. The House passed a version of the plan last week.
"When Social Security and Medicare are neutralized" as issues, Representative Davis said, "Republicans do very, very well."
Democrats see what the Republicans are doing as "fuzzing up these issues," in the words of Representative Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, the House minority leader.
Mr. Gephardt added, "We have not done a good enough job pointing out the real differences between the two parties on issues of real importance to seniors -- i.e., the patient bill of rights, getting prescription drugs to people on Medicare at a good price, long-term care insurance and tax breaks for long-term care, shoring up and keeping strong Social Security and Medicare."
Representative Martin Frost of Texas, chairman of the House Democratic caucus, contends that when Democratic candidates campaign hard for older votes on the traditional issues, they prevail. Democrats remain convinced that their party, which pushed through the legislation creating Social Security and Medicare, should, by all rights, have a natural advantage with older voters.
But Lawrence R. Jacobs, associate professor of political science at the University of Minnesota, and an expert on public opinion and the elderly, argues that the differences between the two parties are muted on some of the key issues, like putting more managed care into Medicare.
"They don't really perceive the kind of massive differences they have in the past," Professor Jacobs said of older voters.
All of these issues are the subject of intense speculation in both parties right now, with one fact adding urgency to it all. Older Americans are not simply a growing population, but one that continues to vote at a remarkable rate. They made up 28 percent of the total House vote in 1998, but can account for a third or more of the vote on Election Day in states like Florida and Pennsylvania. In recent elections, people age 65 and over were more than twice as likely to vote as people age 18 to 24.
So the struggle continues: a Gallup Poll released last week showed the two parties essentially in a dead heat among voters 65 and up, when asked whether a Democratic- or Republican-controlled Congress would be better for the country.
