HEADLINE: Stocks Drive a Rush to Riches In Manhattan's Silicon Alley

BYLINE: By AMY HARMON 

 BODY:
There was something about the wave of unrepentant jubilance sweeping through iVillage's downtown Manhattan offices the day the three-and-a-half-year-old Internet company went public that made Susan Weaver uneasy.
As employees who had been allowed to buy stock for as little as $6 per share watched it climb to $103 in its first afternoon of public trading, Ms. Weaver -- at 46, an elder in a company whose average hire is 31 -- addressed a cigar-wielding group gathered in the hall at iVillage, a provider of on-line information and discussion forums for women. "I just want everyone to realize this is Disneyland," she remembers saying that day in March. 
 Ms. Weaver, iVillage's vice president for programming and a former NBC producer, later explained: "I didn't want them to feel that they were entitled to what was happening to them. This doesn't happen all the time."
That is, it didn't used to. Particularly not in New York, where the cluster of new-media companies known as Silicon Alley has always been viewed by its Western namesake as an also-ran in innovation and wealth creation.
But since March, with Wall Street valuing infant Internet companies that have never made a profit in the same range as car makers and airlines, Silicon Alley has witnessed initial public offerings at the rate of one a week. And as investor fervor for Internet companies threatens to fade, the sprint for I.P.O.'s, as the offerings are known, has only picked up speed.
Even as Internet stocks faltered last week -- iVillage, for example, lost almost 10 percent of its value in one day -- three New York-based companies launched themselves on the public market to a respectable reception. And shares of StarMedia Networks, the most highly anticipated of last week's offerings, closed 74 percent above its initial offering price of $15 per share on Wednesday, its first day of trading.
"It's unbelievable, it's surreal -- it's good, I guess, would be the sound bite," said Fernando Espuelas, 32, who three years ago created StarMedia, which provides Internet access in Latin America. The I.P.O., he said, has made him worth $162 million on paper. "Wow, that's the first time I've said it out loud. It's kind of weird."
The Internet boom has created as many as a thousand paper millionaires among the founders and executives of Silicon Alley companies, said Uri Kaufthal, the president of AST StockPlan, which administers the stock benefit packages for several of them. Many junior employees among Silicon Alley's more than 100,000 workers -- at companies ranging from financial information services to Web site developers -- have collected enough to pay off student loans or consider buying an apartment.
In a city where the well-worn paths to success have led through the trading floor or the ranks of large media corporations, the emergence of sudden Internet-bred wealth holds out the promise of much quicker rewards. It is exposing New Yorkers to the Silicon Valley culture of entrepreneurial risk. And it is prompting resentment, envy and a scramble to be a part of it.
"All of us had friends on Wall Street who always made so damn much money," said Sunny Bates, an executive recruiter for New York-based Internet companies. "Now you can make a lot of money and you don't have to be an investment banker. Everybody wants to get on the train."
Still, Silicon Alley is not embracing the I.P.O. life style without a healthy dose of apprehension.
For one thing, the seeming arbitrariness of who is getting rich can be disconcerting, even for those who are now buying BMW's. Those at start-ups that have yet to issue a prospectus wonder whether their chance will come before it is too late. Workers in the Internet divisions of traditional media companies yearn for their operations to be spun off and taken public.
Meanwhile, veterans of other industries worry that a generation is emerging with the expectation that working late nights for a couple of years at the right company should make them set for retirement. And among some early denizens of the Alley, many of whom started out with the conviction that technology could democratize society and create new modes of expression, there is a sense of loss as talk turns from changing the world to when their options vest.
"There used to be a sense that we were on the cusp of a significant change that was at once economic and cultural," said Jason Chervokas, the editor of @NewYork, an industry newsletter that was recently bought by MecklerMedia. "But the original idea, which was, hey, let's build a medium with a new hip, gender-neutral image, has fallen by the wayside, and I regret that, I really do. The sense that you're allowed to experiment has been replaced by pressure for quarterly results."
For Alley veterans and newcomers alike, it can be hard getting used to running into 20-year-old millionaires at parties in downtown lofts.
"Being around this kind of gargantuan 'monetizing' day in, day out -- it warps your mind," Marisa Bowe, the editor of the on-line magazine Word, wrote in a recent essay in The New York Observer about watching her Silicon Alley friends cash in. "Just a few years ago, we were dance or literature majors, actors with day jobs. Now we live in a strange world where it seems as if every 10th person has won the lottery."
 
A Fast Ride On the Money Wave
But for bystanders, the logic of I.P.O. winnings is somehow harder to swallow than the luck of the lottery. Paul Debraccio, who spent 15 years selling advertising at the magazine publisher Conde Nast before doing the same at a lower salary for an Internet company, Tripod Inc., says some of his former colleagues have a hard time understanding how he has come to be bidding on real estate in the Hamptons this spring.
"The average person just can't conceive that in two or three years you can become a millionaire without having started your own company," said Mr. Debraccio, whose options in Tripod were converted to stock worth over $1.5 million when the company was sold to New York-based Lycos Inc. last year. "At Conde Nast, the most you could make is $500,000 a year, and that's if you were a publisher."
Mr. Debraccio is now the senior vice president for advertising at Uproar, an Internet games company in New York that trades on the Vienna exchange but may soon offer shares in the United States. As is typical for Internet company employees, he accepted a lower salary in exchange for stock options. "Part of it is about riding the money wave," he said. "This is probably going to turn out good as well."
Dave Kansas, the editor of the on-line financial news service TheStreet.com, is less sanguine about his paper wealth, which hovers around $9 million and has in recent days served as a somewhat obsessive topic of conversation in The Wall Street Journal's newsroom, where he used to be a reporter. (On May 11, the day his company went public, Mr. Kansas received several E-mail messages from former colleagues along these lines: "Dave, I was just going to lunch, but I'm a little short today. Do you think . . .")
"I've sacrificed a lot over the last three years," said Mr. Kansas, 32. "Can I value it at $9 million? I don't know."
Indeed, even the biggest evangelists of the Internet wealth culture wax insecure about its scale -- and its future. That is in part because Federal regulations require that institutional investors and senior executives hold onto their stock for six months after the initial public offering. (Junior employees are not subject to those restrictions.) Moreover, stock options that are granted to employees typically vest over a four-year period, so just how rich somebody is depends on how many options they actually own and whether the stock price holds up until they can exercise them.
"It's Monopoly money -- I don't count it," said Fred Wilson, a co-founder of Flatiron Partners, the New York venture capital firm that has financed several of the most successful Internet companies to go public lately, including TheStreet.com, theglobe.com and StarMedia. "You can watch your value go up and down millions a day and you can't do anything about it. It's surreal."
The giddy tone at many of the city's cyber-celebrations and the frequency with which new-media employees check the company stock price underscores the anxiety that their net worth is housed within a speculative bubble that is destined to pop. It is a fear that plagues the industry well beyond New York.
"Historically, to make a gazillion dollars in the computer business you had to have a good product, and there was pretty clear evidence about what made it good -- i.e., people bought it," said Jonathan Weber, the editor of the Industry Standard, a trade publication that follows the Internet economy. "The Internet boom and the I.P.O. phenomenon is so speculative that what separates certain companies from the pack is not always that obvious."
 
Finding Rewards In a Different Risk
Still, many who have benefited even modestly from the boom remember when their friends and families scoffed at their choice to forsake more traditional careers for this strange thing known as the Internet. They do not quite see why the risk of their own opportunities should be deemed less worthy of reward than the risks, say, that investment bankers take with other people's money.
For instance, Kate Hanley's starting salary at iVillage in 1996 was $15,000 less than the book editing job she was offered at Fairchild Communications. But as one of the first 30 employees of the company, she was granted 333 stock options at $6 per share. (iVillage's shares closed Friday at $47.875 -- less than half the price attained on the day it went public but still a healthy premium for Ms. Hanley's options, three-quarters of which have vested. She has not cashed them in.)
Ms. Hanley was also given "friends and family" options, which allow employees and others designated by the management team to buy stock at the initial offering price. The proceeds from that stock, which Ms. Hanley bought at $24 and sold at the opening price of $95, netting her about $25,000, served as a down payment for the Chelsea apartment that she moved into this month.
"People act as though I found this money in a bag on the street," said Ms. Hanley, 29, a producer for iVillage's computing site. "It's like, where were you when I was working for the last three years till 9 or 10 at night and on weekends?"
For Ms. Hanley's friend Annie Rhodes, 28, who works at ToggleThis, another Internet company three blocks away, the iVillage public offering was bittersweet.
"When they went public, I was sort of kicking myself,"' said Ms. Rhodes, who left iVillage two years ago and missed out on cashing in. "But it's also exciting. Until you know people who have hit it big with options, it doesn't really seem real. Now it seems like, God, it's not a joke. This could happen to us."
Filled with that sense of promise, business school students who used to head straight to the finance courses that would get them to Goldman Sachs are packing into the electronic commerce class at Columbia and oversubscribing the entrepreneurial course at New York University.
Those with M.B.A.'s are not the only ones to be drawn by the prospect of entrepreneurial freedom and fabulous wealth. Ken Perlin, a professor of computer science and the director of the Media Research Laboratory at New York University, said the Internet boom had changed the orientation of his students, particularly since Yahoo bought Webcal, the Web calendar that one of the laboratory students developed, for $12 million in stock last July.
"Young people in this field think they should be millionaires, and that's a new phenomenon," Professor Perlin said. "The concern is that it's deceptive. I know a number of students who tried to make a go of it and it didn't work."
But if there is angst or self-loathing among some of those who have tried their hand in Silicon Alley, Jeff Dachis, a co-founder of Razorfish, an Internet services company that went public April 27, does not share it.
"I don't want to be lumped in with the hucksters of the world, because we have the real deal," said Mr. Dachis, 32, who bought a BMW 5-series as an I.P.O. gift to himself. "I feel completely and utterly entitled to whatever success comes our way. Not everybody's good, not everybody has the winning idea, not every idea deserves to be funded or to be public. I'm sorry, but there are sheep and there are shepherds, and I fancy myself to be the latter."
Andrew Zolli, the vice president of the interactive division of the advertising firm Siegal & Gale, said he spent a week of sleepless nights pondering his past and future after watching friends and former colleagues at Razorfish make millions this month. Finally, though, he came to peace.
"I love my job, and ultimately I believe this is still the most important medium of the next half century, and our ultimate judge is not the size of our bank accounts," said Mr. Zolli, 28. "When this insanity passes, what will matter is who had the really good ideas and good work. I really believe that."

HEADLINE: Critic of CUNY, Badillo, Chosen To Head Board

BYLINE: By KAREN W. ARENSON 

 BODY:
Gov. George E. Pataki has named Herman Badillo, a sharp critic of the City University of New York and a primary force behind efforts to change it, as its next chairman.
The appointment gives Mr. Badillo, 69, a lawyer who has been CUNY's vice chairman and a close ally of Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, the opportunity to shape one of New York City's most important educational institutions, one with 200,000 students, at a time of enormous ferment. He will also be able to fill many top positions that are now open at the university and its 17 undergraduate colleges. 
 Like many public urban universities, CUNY draws many students who are not viewed as prime college material: welfare mothers, immigrants who speak little or no English, students from inner-city neighborhoods who have little to show for their high school years. And at a time of budget cutting and a renewed focus on standards, the education of these students has become a hotly debated issue.
Many CUNY traditionalists say that the university must not abandon its mission to admit all students and to try to help them. But Mr. Badillo and other critics, including the Mayor and Governor, have long accused CUNY of squandering its resources on weak students who never graduate.
"There is no question in my mind that CUNY needs reform," Governor Pataki said in a telephone interview yesterday. "Herman Badillo has a knowledge of the system, he's a product of the system and he has a vision for the system. That all made it plain that he's the man to reform CUNY."
To achieve that reform, CUNY's critics say, the university must set higher admissions standards to encourage applicants to work harder in high school, push students to complete remedial work and earn degrees more quickly, and establish tougher requirements for graduation so that a CUNY degree will be more respected.
"We need to make sure that the value of the City University diploma is accepted anywhere in New York City or the world," Mr. Badillo said. "That is my goal."
But critics of Mr. Badillo's efforts to transform CUNY expressed alarm at his appointment, which was first reported yesterday in The Daily News, and said it would result in further disarray.
"When the fox guards the chickens, you don't expect to have anything left except skin and bones," said Sandi E. Cooper a CUNY history professor and former chairwoman of its faculty Senate.
Edward C. Sullivan, chairman of the State Assembly's Committee on Higher Education, called the appointment "an insult."
"The CUNY board is in chaos, so why would the Governor want to reward the purveyor of chaos?" he said.
And Robert Hollander, a CUNY graduate student and adjunct professor, said he viewed Mr. Badillo as "CUNY's worst enemy," someone who did not appreciate CUNY's mission to provide both access and excellence to students.
Mr. Badillo said his intention was not to harm the university, but to improve it.
"I have no desire to curtail its traditional function as a gateway for the poor, but to do it even better," he said.
Partly because of Mr. Badillo's prodding, CUNY faces basic questions on the role it should play, how it is structured and run, and crucial policies like criteria for admissions and graduation. CUNY estimates that at least 60 percent of New York City high school students go on to CUNY, either directly from high school or later.
Mr. Badillo said his first three steps would be to find a new chancellor, to prepare CUNY's response to the State Board of Regents as it reviews the university's new policy on remedial students, and to respond to Mayor Giuliani's task force on CUNY, which is expected to release its findings on CUNY soon.
"The timing is perfect," Mr. Badillo said. "All three of these things will define CUNY's priorities."
CUNY's top policy slot opened last week when Anne A. Paolucci, the university's chairwoman, resigned unexpectedly. Dr. Paolucci, a former English professor at St. John's University and expert on Pirandello, never developed a close working relationship with the Governor. She said last week that she wanted to return to her scholarly work and would leave CUNY's board tomorrow. Mr. Badillo's appointment will take effect then.
Dr. Paolucci was unusual in coming from academia; like Mr. Badillo, most of CUNY's chairmen have been lawyers or businessmen with close political connections.
Mr. Badillo, a former Congressman, Bronx borough president and Deputy Mayor, has worked closely with Mayor Giuliani and his senior adviser on education. Both men say that CUNY does not set high enough standards for entering students and for graduation and that it allows students to spend too long in remedial classes.
At a time when the Mayor is battling for greater control over the city's public schools, the appointment of his ally Mr. Badillo appears to give Mr. Giuliani a more direct hand in CUNY. And it could deflect some criticism that CUNY is ignoring the interests of Hispanic and other minority students.
Mayor Giuliani, speaking during a visit to a fire station in Forest Hills, Queens, yesterday, said that Mr. Badillo's task would be "changing a culture of total failure."
"It's changing a culture that embraces a 1-percent graduation rate and tries to defend it, excuse it and alibi for it as opposed to dramatically trying to change it," the Mayor said, referring to the low percentage of CUNY community college students who graduate in two years. "He has got to have everyone take a leap of faith toward high standards."
CUNY has been a lower priority for Governor Pataki, as for many governors. The university was largely a city responsibility until the state took over most of the city's financial support for CUNY in the late 1970's after the city's budget crisis. And Mr. Badillo, a seasoned politician, has made himself a ready link between the Governor's mansion and the university, earning the Governor's trust.
"Anyone who ignores the Mayor and the Governor is a fool," Mr. Badillo said yesterday. "The money comes from the Mayor and the Governor and the policies come from the Mayor and the Governor. They are the top elected officials. That is why I get along with them; I understand that."
The Governor, who has the responsibility for selecting CUNY's chairman and vice chairman from among its 17 trustees, has joined with the Mayor and Mr. Badillo in charging that CUNY's standards are unacceptably low, and privately criticized Dr. Paolucci for not moving fast enough to remake CUNY.
Over the last year, there was wide speculation that he would ask her to resign as soon as he had someone to replace her and a more secure majority on the often-divided board.
The Governor said yesterday that Dr. Paolucci had started the reform process, but that CUNY still needed significant improvement.
"There is no question in my mind that CUNY needs reform," he said, "whether it is the failure of some campuses to educate their students properly or that so many of their teachers graduate who are unprepared to teach."
While Governor Pataki and Mayor Giuliani have had various public spats -- including the recent one on the repeal of New York City's commuter tax -- the Governor said yesterday that he and the Mayor "have a common view on the need to improve CUNY."
Mr. Badillo, a native of Puerto Rico who graduated from City College, has been vice chairman of CUNY's board since February 1997, when the Governor appointed him at the same time he named Dr. Paolucci chairwoman. Since then he has often been its effective leader.
After he learned that students at Hostos Community College were being allowed to graduate even if they could not pass CUNY's placement exam in writing in 1997, he formulated a policy requiring all community college students to pass the test to graduate. Over great protest from some students and faculty members, the board adopted the policy.
Working closely with both the Mayor and the Governor, Mr. Badillo was also the main architect behind the controversial remedial education policy passed by CUNY's trustees last year. The policy will bar students from entering CUNY's bachelor's degree programs if they fail to pass placement examinations in reading, writing and mathematics.
The policy is scheduled to be phased in beginning in January, but the Regents have said they intend to review it and have raised questions about its impact and whether it will change CUNY's mission.
Other appointments on the board could come in June and give Mr. Badillo a more secure majority than he and Dr. Paolucci have had. Of the 17 trustees, the Governor appoints 10 and the Mayor appoints 5, and the students and faculty each elect one, although the faculty trustee does not have a vote.
The Governor will be able to replace both James P. Murphy and Edith Everett, two trustees who frequently oppose Mr. Badillo. John J. Calandra, another gubernatorial appointee whose term is up, is more likely to be reappointed, since he has been a strong Badillo supporter.
The Mayor can also make two new appointments in June if he wishes, to replace Richard B. Stone and Ronald J. Marino. Both have generally backed Mr. Badillo, but are seen as being somewhat independent.
With the board in flux, CUNY faces several important actions.
One is the release of a yearlong investigation of the university commissioned by Mayor Giuliani to advise him on how to restructure it and how to privatize remedial education. Mr. Badillo, a member of that panel, said that the commission report "will set our goalposts of what to do," and that he would take its recommendations to the other trustees.
Mr. Badillo was first appointed to CUNY's board by Gov. Hugh L. Carey in the early 1980's and was later reappointed by Gov. Mario M. Cuomo. But it was not until Mayor Giuliani and Governor Pataki appointed trustees to the board three years ago that he had enough allies to press his agenda for change.
Other areas that Mr. Badillo would like to see changed are CUNY's curriculum -- he sees the new core curriculum adopted by the State University in December as an attractive model -- and its grading policy. He has accused CUNY's colleges of widespread grade inflation and setting the bar too low for graduation with honors.
But he said his first task was to reopen the search for a chancellor. The post has been filled by an interim chancellor, Christoph M. Kimmich, since the 1997 resignation of W. Ann Reynolds, who had battled with Dr. Paolucci and other trustees.
"That is the most important thing we have to do," Mr. Badillo said. "I want a strong personality, someone who is familiar with the problems of the City University, and someone who will move decisively."

HEADLINE: Spreading the Small-Town News;
A New Sort of Company Invests in Community Papers

BYLINE: By FELICITY BARRINGER 

DATELINE: CULLMAN, Ala.

 BODY:
Before sound bites, before the press was called the media, before the talk of broadband communications, narrowcasting or dot-com Web portals, there was The Cullman Times.
The Times, which sells 10,358 copies daily, has been there for 43 years. And it is still there for big events: it was the first to tell many of its readers of the death of Diana, Princess of Wales. More recently, it let them know when $13.50 worth of gasoline was stolen from the grocery-store pumps on Route 278. It went nuts over the state championship girls' basketball team from Cold Springs High School. It regularly notes who is admitted and discharged from the Cullman Regional Medical Center. 
 The Times is one of 940 daily newspapers around the country with circulations of 20,000 or less. Many such newspapers once had profit margins only a family could love. But recently, while attention was focused on cable and Internet companies, these far-flung atolls of the information age have been bought, sold and swapped like the most valuable properties in a manic Monopoly game.
In 1997 and 1998, more than 300 daily newspapers changed hands, according to Owen Van Essen, whose firm, Dirks, Van Essen & Associates in Santa Fe, N.M., brokers newspaper sales.
In the process, a new generation of companies is emerging with a different profile than earlier groups. The newcomers have borrowed the strategy -- favored by Thomson Newspapers and others -- of buying clusters of newspapers in one geographic area, sharing printing and accounting operations and offering big regional advertisers five or six papers in a single buy. But unlike the established publishing companies, which acquire large and small newspapers in both rural and urban settings, the newcomers have focused exclusively on community newspapers in out-of-the-way regions where the skies are truly dark at night.
Leading the new wave is a company in Birmingham, Ala., called Community Newspaper Holdings Inc., which bought The Times and 44 other newspapers late last year and, in the process, became the biggest little-newspaper company in the country with 95 dailies and 125 weeklies.
What's more, Community Newspaper Holdings is a rarity: its sole investor is a pension fund, which has provided all its working capital. The Retirement Systems of Alabama, which has $22 billion in assets, has loaned the company $1.13 billion to buy newspapers.
What would a pension fund want with small newspapers, the bottom end of a business that is, in the polite put-down of the financial world, mature? Small newspapers are suffering from the same problems as their larger brethren: declining circulation and limited growth potential for the ink-on-paper product. But Community Newspaper and others see gold in the steady if unspectacular cash flow of small businesses that often operate with little or no competition.
There are other incentives. Think synergy at its most basic. The director of the Alabama pension fund, David Bronner, wanted a solid return on his investment and a media platform from which to sing the virtues of his adopted state of Alabama. With Community Newspaper, he gets both.
John Morton, a newspaper industry analyst, set up the pension fund manager's calculation like this: "I can invest in the bond market and earn five or six percent -- or I can invest in all of these teeny cash-flow engines stuck all over the place" for double that return.
"Even if we have a national recession," Mr. Morton added, "these are pretty durable."
That durability, however, depends on putting the right combination of newspapers together. Not all regions fit Community Newspaper's vision. Ralph J. Martin, the company's top executive, is looking for areas with stable or growing populations, far enough away from large television markets that there are few competitors for local advertising dollars.
He has developed a rough formula. For long-term economic stability, Mr. Martin said, it helps if a newspaper is near a county seat (lawyers tend to be newspaper readers; governments take out legal advertising); near large educational and medical institutions (educated work force, recession-resistant employers); near large government operations (recession-resistant, stable employers), and near an Interstate highway (truck traffic and tourism keeps communities healthy).
He has selected Community Newspaper's newspapers with an eye to minimizing the effects of an economic downturn. Mr. Martin, the president and chief executive of Community Newspaper Holdings, said recessions tend to hit the country at large for 18 months to two years; in markets that fit his formula, he said, recessions have lasted eight months or less. To further reduce risk, he is diversifying. Community Newspaper's publications are based in a mixed basket of micro-economies in places from northern New York to Kentucky to Texas, reducing the risk that all the revenue streams would dry up simultaneously.
Even so, newspapers are not exactly a traditional investment for a pension fund, but then again, Mr.Bronner is not exactly a traditional pension fund manager. His investments have also included a Manhattan skyscraper -- 55 Water Street -- a steel mill in Birmingham, chemical plants in Mobile, Ala., a Wal-Mart distribution center in Cullman, and his favorite, a series of golf courses around his adopted state.
Norman Stein, a law professor and pension specialist at the University of Alabama, said some pension fund members regarded Mr. Bronner as "something of a genius."
"He does a lot of unorthodox things," Mr. Stein said. "He's an entrepreneur in an industry that is dominated by passive investors."
When Mr. Bronner took over the fund in 1973, Mr. Stein said, it had $500 million in assets -- "one dollar for every four dollars we owed." In 25 years, the assets have grown to $22 billion.
"There is a reason to be out on the cutting edge on some of the things I do," Mr. Bronner said. "But I think about it very hard."
Investment decisions, he said, are made for financial reasons first, and then are designed to meet one other unique criterion: "Can I change the image of Alabama?"
So when the state got a round of terrible publicity in 1990, when the Professional Golfers' Association held its tournament at an all-white country club, Mr. Bronner's response was to pour $120 million in Retirement Systems funds into a string of state-of-the-art golf courses, open to all. The Robert Trent Jones Golf Trail now has 324 holes to play in seven locations around the state.
Then Mr. Bronner wanted to spread the word. Which is where the newspapers come in. In addition to interest rates ranging from 9 to 10.4 percent, the Retirement Systems of Alabama requires, as a condition of its loans to Community Newspaper, that the company's papers provide free advertising space to the pension fund to promote the virtues of Alabama. (He made a similar deal when he invested in a small television company.)
Community Newspaper's creators had different, though complementary, agendas. Mr. Martin had worked for another large chain and wanted to run a company of his own. One of the company's other partners, Tom Lindley 3d, is a reporter turned editor turned publisher, who is living out old daydreams of running his hometown newspaper.
On paper, the company's financial goals fit Mr. Lindley's notion of the right balance between profit and investment in journalism. The interest payments to the pension fund consume 9 to 10 percent of Community Newspaper's profit; the overall margins expected from the newspapers, Mr. Martin said, range from 19 to 26 percent. That is well below the 30 percent-plus margins of some Wall Street favorites, like the Gannett Company. But Mr. Martin said he preferred to reinvest the difference in presses, computer technology and journalists.
"When the first generation of chaining was going on," Mr. Martin, a former executive at Thomson Newspapers, said, "there was so much opportunity from a consolidation standpoint" that executives focused on efficiency. "I don't think there was an attack on news," he added. "It just fell through the cracks."
He added that he wanted local editorial autonomy, and said he offered only a few guidelines. Editors and publishers may not double as local political leaders and no article may be run unless the subject of the piece has had a fair chance to present his or her side.
Mr. Martin has encouraged the 53 Community Newspaper publications in Oklahoma to create a common statehouse bureau that none could have afforded on its own; the bureau opened four months ago.
There is a question as to whether the state of Alabama, through its pension fund, could have a financial interest in a paper like The Cullman Times without tainting its journalism. Will Alabama get kid-glove treatment from Community Newspaper? "No," Mr. Bronner said, "because I know we will drag one foot forward and shoot it off every once in a while." All he wants, he said, is to make clear that "there are some positive aspects along with the negative aspects."
That philosophy did not work well at one twice-weekly newspaper, The Floyd County Times in eastern Kentucky, which Community Newspaper bought last year. The newspaper lost two senior reporters after a dispute with the publisher installed last year -- Mr. Martin's brother Ed. As described in the current issue of American Journalism Review, the reporters covering the corruption-ridden, economically depressed community balked at Ed Martin's insistence that, when it comes to news, "good and bad have the same weight."
Edward C. Dowling Jr., editor and publisher of The Cullman Times, said in a recent interview that a classic newspaper company mistake "is to think that all markets are the same and can be approached in a cookie-cutter fashion." He wants the latitude to earn his community's respect.
Mr. Lindley, who is also editor and publisher of The Evening News in Jeffersonville, Ala., has similar aims.
In fact, Mr. Lindley was the matchmaker between the two key players in Community Newspaper. He introduced Ralph Martin to David Bronner in late 1996. One of Mr. Bronner's conditions, on making the initial loan, was that Mr. Lindley would be one of the company's owners.
Mr. Lindley had first-hand experience of what small-town newspapers can go through. When he arrived at The Evening News (circulation 10,305) in August 1995, he said that he found a demoralized staff. Starting salaries for reporters were $265 a week. Just a few months before, a feature writer had been living in a homeless shelter. When Mr. Lindley asked the dean of Indiana University's journalism school to give him the names of prospective reporters, he said, he was told, "We don't recommend your newspaper."
He started to make changes. Then in mid-1997, the owners of Park Communications Inc., who had hired him, sold their newspapers to Media General Inc., now part of Cox Communications Inc. When Mr. Martin, who was running Park's newspaper division, called to tell him of the sale, Mr. Lindley urged Mr. Martin to form his own company and buy some of those properties -- like The Evening News -- back from Media General.
Mr. Martin had long thought about running his own chain, but was not sure where to get the capital. So Mr. Lindley introduced him to Mr. Bronner, whom he had covered as a statehouse reporter for The Birmingham News.
Mr. Martin and Mr. Bronner hit it off, and Mr. Bronner agreed to provide the company with working capital. In January 1997, Community Newspaper Holdings was formed with 13 former Park Communications newspapers. Then Mr. Martin went looking for more. Community Newspaper's most recent big deals brought it 28 newspapers from Donrey Media Group last September and 45 more from Hollinger Inc. in December.
For the moment, Mr. Martin said, Community Newspaper is resting on its laurels. Some future acquisitions and adjustments may be made, but none are on the immediate horizon.
Mr. Lindley now worries about both profit and news, but more about news. "If we don't do a good job in that regard, then the money will never come," he said. He has hired a city editor and a photographer, neither of which the paper had. As of now, he said, "It's not great yet, but it's gotten better."
The news staff is up from 9 to 11 and starting salaries are up 35 percent, to about $360. And letters to the editor are pouring in; at the end of last month's mayoral race, Mr. Lindley found the angry incumbent mayor banging on his door on a Saturday night, "scorched" in Mr. Lindley's word, by letters he didn't like.
At The Cullman Times, Mr. Dowling is still getting to know his new bosses. He said he had not lost "the local autonomy to do what we need to do."
"We want to be heavily involved in this community," he said. "We want to be a cheerleader when people deserve it, and we want to scold people that need scolding."

 CORRECTION:
Because of an editing error, an article in Business Day on Monday about companies that own small newspapers misstated the ownership of Media General. It is independent, not part of Cox Communications.
An article and a picture caption in Business Day last Monday about companies that own small newspapers misstated the location of The Evening News, owned by Community Newspaper Holdings. It is in Jefferson, Ind., not in Alabama. The article also misstated the surname of the editor and publisher of The Cullman (Ala.) Times. He is Edward C. Darling Jr., not Dowling.

GRAPHIC: Photos: David Bronner, left, director of the Retirement Systems of Alabama, invested pension assets in Community Newspaper Holdings, headed by Ralph J. Martin. (Dave Martin for The New York Times, left; Mike Clemmer for The New York Times)(pg. C1); Tom Lindley 3d, a partner in Community Newspaper Holdings, is also the editor and publisher of The Evening News in Jeffersonville, Ala. (Keith Williams for The New York Times)(pg. C6)
 
Chart: "Strength in Numbers"
Community Newspaper Holdings buys newspapers in areas that the company believes are resistant to recession because they are near one or more of the following: a county seat; a large educational or medical institution; an Interstate highway; or a large government operation. The company also looks for papers close enough to one another to share services like printing, and to entice advertisers by offering multiple ad placements. One cluster of papers that the company owns is in Oklahoma:
 
Checotah
PAPER -- McIntosh County Democrat (weekly)
ATTRACTION -- Intersection of two major highways
 
Eufaula
PAPERS -- Indian Journal (weekly), Lake Eufaula World (weekly)
ATTRACTIONS -- Largest lake in the state is pop-ular with tourists; county seat
 
McAlester
PAPER -- News-Capital & Democrat (daily)
ATTRACTIONS -- County seat; state penitentiary; medical center; college campus
 
Hartshorne
PAPER -- Hartshorne Sun (weekly)
ATTRACTIONS -- Nearby hospital and college campus
 
Poteau
PAPER -- Poteau Daily News (daily)
ATTRACTIONS -- County seat; college campus; large employer (pg. C1)
 
Map of Oklahoma shows location of McAlester. (pg. C1)
